Divine Attributes
beginner

The Positive Attributes and the Priority of Defining Power
Enumerates eight positive attributes and explains the priority of defining power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight positive attributes of Allah and why it is necessary to define 'al-Qadir' before discussing God's power.

Core Idea
God's power is defined by its connection to motives and deterrents.

This passage from Sanusi's work lists the eight positive attributes of Allah that are commonly discussed in Sunni theology. It then makes an important methodological point: before we can talk about God being 'Qadir' (Powerful), we must first clarify what the term 'Qadir' means, because that discussion involves understanding motives and deterrents that influence actions. This sets the stage for a deeper analysis of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the list of attributes and the author's instruction.
Notice that the list is eight attributes.
2
Identify the eight attributes
List the eight positive attributes in order as given. They are: Powerful, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Eternal, Wise.
These are the standard positive attributes in Ash'ari theology.
3
Understand the methodological note
The author says that before discussing God being Powerful, we must define 'al-Qadir'. This definition is connected to motives (dawāʿī) and deterrents (ṣawārif). Ask yourself: why would defining power require talking about motives and deterrents?
This shows the systematic nature of kalam: definitions precede doctrines.
4
Reflect on the logical order
Consider why the author prefaced the discussion of power with this note. What would be the risk of discussing God's power without first defining it?
Without definition, the discussion might be ambiguous or lead to misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات الايجابية وهي كونه سبحانه : قادراً ، عالماً ، حياً ، سميعاً ، بصيراً ، متكلماً ، باقياً ، حكيماً . واعلم : أن الكلام في كونه تعالى - قادراً ، يجب أن يتقدمه الكلام في تفسير القادر ، والكلام فيه متصل بأحكام الدواعي والصوارف فلهذا السبب كان تقديم الكلام فيه .
The Positive Attributes: And they are His being (glorified): Powerful, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Eternal, Wise. And know: that the discussion concerning His being (Most High) Powerful must be preceded by the discussion on the interpretation of 'al-Qadir' (the Powerful), and the discussion thereof is connected to the rulings of motives and deterrents, and for this reason the discussion of it was prefaced.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and a methodological note. The author begins by listing the eight positive attributes (al-ṣifāt al-ījābiyya) of God: power, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech, eternity, and wisdom. All these are affirmed for God, the Exalted. Then the author makes a methodological point: before discussing God's attribute of power (being Qādir), one must first define what 'al-Qādir' means (interpretation of the term). This is because the discussion of power is connected to the rulings (aḥkām) of motives (dawāʿī) and deterrents (ṣawārif) — factors that influence an agent's action. Therefore, the author had to preface the discussion of power with a discussion of its definition and related concepts.

Key Terms
الصفات الايجابية
قادر
عالم
حي
سميع
بصير
متكلم
باق
حكيم
دواعي
صوارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The list of eight attributes is exhaustive and power is the most important attribute.
Correction: The list represents the commonly discussed positive attributes, but it is not necessarily exhaustive. The author's note emphasizes a logical precedence in definition, not that power is superior.

Checkpoint
How many positive attributes are listed in this passage?
A) 5
B) 7
C) 8
D) 10

Key Takeaway
The eight positive attributes of Allah are: Powerful, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Eternal, Wise; and the discussion of God's power must be preceded by a clear definition of 'al-Qadir' due to its connection to motives and deterrents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Powerful,' have you ever paused to consider what that phrase truly entails? Are you connecting His power to the very motives and deterrents that shape your daily choices? How would your trust in Him change if you meditated on this link? Commit this week to reflecting on one situation where you feel powerless and consciously affirm that Allah's power is intimately aware of your inner struggles.
?
The author insists on defining 'al-Qadir' before discussing God's power. In your own study of Islam, how often do you rush to conclusions about Allah's attributes without first ensuring you understand the precise terms? Could your uncertainties about God's actions be rooted in a lack of clear definition? Resolve to look up one attribute in a reliable source this week and define it thoroughly before assuming you understand it.

Positive Divine Attributes and Defining Qadir
The author lists the positive attributes and explains why discussion of Qadir must be preceded by its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight positive attributes of Allah and why clarifying the interpretation of 'Qadir' is essential before theological discussion.

Core Idea
Defining 'Powerful' is the gateway to understanding divine attributes.

This lesson begins with the enumeration of Allah's positive attributes, then highlights a key methodological principle: before delving into the nature of divine power, one must first clarify what 'powerful' means. This careful approach prevents confusion and grounds the discussion in sound terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the list of attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the eight attributes listed: Qadir, 'Alim, Hayy, Sami', Basir, Mutakallim, Baqi, Hakim. These are the positive attributes of Allah.
The list is preceded by the phrase 'الصفات الايجابية' indicating these are attributes that affirm perfection.
2
Understand the methodological instruction
The author then states that before discussing Allah being Qadir, one must first provide the interpretation (تفسير) of 'al-Qadir'. Understand why this is necessary: the discussion is connected to the rulings of motives (دواعي) and deterrents (صوارف).
This shows the importance of precise terminology in kalam.
3
Connect to the broader theological context
Reflect on how defining 'Qadir' first helps in properly understanding divine power in relation to human actions, avoiding confusion about free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات الايجابية وهي كونه سبحانه : قادراً ، عالماً ، حياً ، سميعاً ، بصيراً ، متكلماً ، باقياً ، حكيماً . واعلم : أن الكلام في كونه تعالى - قادراً ، يجب أن يتقدمه الكلام في تفسير القادر ، والكلام فيه متصل بأحكام الدواعي والصوارف فلهذا السبب كان تقديم الكلام فيه .
The positive attributes: and they are that He, exalted be He, is: Powerful, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Enduring, Wise. And know: that discussion concerning His being exalted - Powerful, must be preceded by discussion of the interpretation of 'Powerful', and the discussion about it is connected to the rulings of motives and deterrents; for this reason, the discussion of it was presented first.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a list of the positive attributes (الصفات الايجابية) of Allah, followed by a methodological note. The author first enumerates eight attributes: al-Qadir (Powerful), al-'Alim (Knowing), al-Hayy (Living), al-Sami' (Hearing), al-Basir (Seeing), al-Mutakallim (Speaking), al-Baqi (Enduring), and al-Hakim (Wise). These are attributes that affirm perfection. Then the author gives an important instruction: before discussing that God is Qadir, one must first provide the interpretation (تفسير) of the term 'al-Qadir'. This is because the discussion of divine power is connected to the rules governing motives (دواعي) and deterrents (صوارف) – concepts related to human actions. Understanding what 'powerful' means is necessary to properly discuss God's power in relation to creation.

Key Terms
الصفات الايجابية
قادر
تفسير القادر
دواعي
صوارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that listing the attributes is sufficient theological knowledge and that no further terminological clarification is needed.
Correction: The author stresses that even after listing the attributes, a precise definition of 'Qadir' is necessary to avoid misunderstandings, especially regarding motives and deterrents.

Checkpoint
Why does the author insist that discussion of God being Qadir must be preceded by interpretation of 'al-Qadir'?
A) Because God is not truly Qadir
B) Because the discussion is connected to motives and deterrents
C) Because the attribute is not in the Quran
D) Because Qadir is not a positive attribute

Key Takeaway
The eight positive attributes are foundational, but defining 'Qadir' properly is essential before discussing divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Powerful,' what does that actually mean for how you live today? Are your actions based on a clear understanding of His power, or on vague assumptions?
?
If defining 'Qadir' requires understanding motives and deterrents in your own actions, how accountable are you for the choices you make in light of Allah's power? Will you examine a specific decision today through this lens?
?
How often do you speak about Allah's attributes without first checking your own understanding? What concrete step will you take to ensure you are not discussing divine matters without proper definition?

Definition of al-Qadir (the Capable)
Defines al-Qadir via the two possibilities of action and inaction depending on motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Qadir and how it relates to varying motives (al-dawa'i).

Core Idea
Capability is the power to act or not act based on motives, not raw ability.

Sanusi begins by defining the attribute 'al-Qadir' (the Capable), which is essential for understanding divine attributes. The definition emphasizes that capability is not simply the ability to act, but the ability to either act or refrain depending on internal motives. This sets the stage for a deeper analysis of what motives are and how they function.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of al-Qadir carefully. Notice it isn't about sheer power, but about the possibility of both action and inaction.
The phrase 'بحسب الدواعي المختلفة' ties capability to motives.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify 'الدواعي' as the plural of 'الداعي' (motive). Try to understand why motives are essential to the definition.
Think of how we attribute capability to someone who can choose.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this definition applies to Allah and to humans. What does it add to our understanding of divine power?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القصات الأولي في حد القادر أجود ما قيل فيه : أنه الذي يصح منه أن يفعل تارة ، وأن لا يفعل أخرى ، بحسب الدواعي المختلفة . وتفسير الدواعي : هو أن الإنسان إذا علم أو ظن ، أو اعتقد، أن له في الفعل الفلاني مصلحة راجحة . فعند حصول أحد هذه الثلاثة ، يحصل في قلبه ميل جازم إلى الفعل ( فإن كانت أعضاؤه سليمة فإن عند حصول ذلك الميل في قلبه يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل ((١) وأما إن علم ، أو ظن ، أو اعتقد أن له في الفعل الفلاني مفسدة راجحة ، فعند حصول هذا العلم ، أو الاعتقاد ، أو الظن ، يحصل في قلبه نفرة جازمة عن ذلك الفعل، فإن كانت أعضاؤه سليمة ، ترتب على حصول تلك النفرة ، مع سلامة الأعضاء : الترك. وهذا هو المراد بالداعي ويتفرع على ما ذكرناه مباحث : الأول : في بيان السبب الذي لاجله سميت هذه الأشياء الثلاثة بالداعي . فنقول : الدعاء : إسم للقول المخصوص . إلا أن الإنسان إذا علم ، أو ظن ؛ أو اعتقد أن له في فعل مخصوص خيراً راجحاً ، فإنه يصير ذلك العلم ، أو الاعتقاد ، أو الظن ، حاملاً له على الفعل ( فأشبه ذلك كون ذلك (۱) من ( س ) .
The first chapters on the definition of al-Qadir (the Capable). The best of what has been said about it: that he is the one from whom it is valid to act at one time and not to act at another, according to the varying motives (al-dawa'i). And the interpretation of motives (al-dawa'i) is: that when a human knows, or assumes, or believes that there is for him in a certain action a preponderant benefit, then upon the occurrence of one of these three, there occurs in his heart a decisive inclination (mayl jazim) towards the action. And if his limbs are sound, then upon the occurrence of that inclination in his heart, that action is emitted from him. And if he knows, or assumes, or believes that there is for him in a certain action a preponderant harm, then upon the occurrence of this knowledge, or belief, or assumption, there occurs in his heart a decisive aversion (nufra jazima) from that action. If his limbs are sound, then upon the occurrence of that aversion, along with the soundness of the limbs, the result is omission (tark). And this is what is meant by 'al-dai' (motive). And branches from what we have mentioned: The first: concerning the explanation of the reason why these three things are named 'al-dai'. We say: 'al-du'a' is a name for a specific utterance. However, when a human knows, or assumes, or believes that there is for him in a specific action a preponderant good, then that knowledge, or belief, or assumption becomes a motive (hamil) for him towards the action. So it resembles that being... (1) From (S).
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of the term 'al-Qadir' (the Capable) and then explains what 'al-dawa'i' (motives) are, which are central to understanding capability. The text begins by stating the best definition: al-Qadir is the one from whom it is possible to act at one time and not act at another, depending on varying motives. This definition emphasizes that capability is not merely the power to act, but the power to choose between action and inaction based on internal motivations. The author then interprets 'al-dawa'i' (plural of al-dai, motive). A motive is described as follows: when a person has knowledge (ilm), assumption (zann), or belief (i'tiqad) that a particular action brings a preponderant benefit (maslaha rajiha), a decisive inclination (mayl jazim) arises in the heart. If the limbs are sound (i.e., the person is physically able), that inclination results in the action being performed. Conversely, if the person knows, assumes, or believes that an action brings a preponderant harm (mafsada rajiha), a decisive aversion (nufra jazima) arises in the heart, and if the limbs are sound, this leads to omission (tark) of the action. This pairing of inclination-to-act and aversion-to-refrain is what is meant by 'al-dai' (motive). The passage then introduces branches that follow from this explanation. The first branch investigates why these three cognitive states—knowledge, assumption, and belief—are called 'al-dai' (the caller). The author explains that 'al-du'a' (call) is normally a term for a specific utterance. However, when a person knows, assumes, or believes that there is a preponderant good in a specific action, that knowledge, assumption, or belief becomes a 'hamil' (motive) that drives the person to act. Thus, it resembles that calling in its effect. The text ends abruptly during this explanation, likely continuing in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
القادر
الدواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'al-Qadir' mean according to the best definition in the passage?
A) The one who always acts
B) The one who can do everything
C) The one from whom it is valid to act at one time and not act at another depending on motives
D) The one who has power over others

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadir is defined by the ability to act or refrain, guided by motives, not just raw power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your last 24 hours. What motives drove your actions and inactions? How many were for Allah versus for your own desires? Before your next decision today, pause and ask: 'What is my motive? Is it for Allah?' and act accordingly.
?
You are accountable not only for what you do but also for what you refrain from doing. What is one sin you keep committing despite knowing it is wrong? Will you truly resolve to stop today, relying on Allah's help, and take a concrete step to avoid it?
?
Allah is al-Qadir, capable of both acting and refraining out of wisdom. When He withholds something you desperately want, how does this attribute help you trust His wisdom and submit to His plan? Write down one recent disappointment and consciously surrender it to Him.

The Mechanism of Motives (al-Dawa'i)
Interprets al-dawa'i as cognitive states causing inclination or aversion, leading to action or omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cognitive states (knowledge, assumption, belief) about benefit or harm produce decisive inclination (mayl jazim) or aversion (nufra jazima), which motivate action or omission.

Core Idea
Motives are internal cognitive judgments that cause either inclination to act or aversion to refrain.

After defining al-Qadir, Sanusi explains what motives (al-dawa'i) are. This passage details the psychological mechanism: when a person has a cognitive state (knowledge, assumption, or belief) regarding a preponderant benefit or harm, a decisive inclination or aversion arises in the heart, which, with sound limbs, leads to action or omission. This clarifies how motives function and why they are called 'callers' to action.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of motives
Read the Arabic description of how a decisive inclination (mayl jazim) occurs when one knows, assumes, or believes in a preponderant benefit.
Notice the three cognitive states: علم, ظن, اعتقاد.
2
Understand the opposites
Read the counterpart about a preponderant harm resulting in decisive aversion (nufra jazima) and omission (tark).
The structure mirrors the benefit case.
3
Link to capability
Reflect on how this mechanism underlies the definition of al-Qadir—it is through such motives that the capable agent acts or refrains.
This connects back to the previous lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What produces a decisive aversion (nufra jazima) from an action?
A) Knowledge, assumption, or belief of a preponderant benefit
B) Knowledge, assumption, or belief of a preponderant harm
C) Soundness of limbs alone
D) Absence of any cognitive state

Key Takeaway
Motives are internal cognitive evaluations of benefit or harm that produce either an inclination to act or an aversion to refrain, leading to action or omission when limbs are sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you knew an action was beneficial (e.g., praying on time) but you delayed or avoided it. According to this lesson, your heart lacked decisive inclination. What false evaluation of benefit or harm was holding you back? Will you examine that evaluation today?
?
Your aversions to certain deeds—like giving charity or seeking knowledge—are not just 'lack of motivation' but are themselves motivated by a perception of harm. What 'preponderant harm' are you assuming that contradicts Allah’s promise of reward? Are you willing to replace that assumption with true knowledge?
?
This passage ties action directly to what you truly believe (or assume) about benefit and harm. So if your actions do not reflect Islam, your cognitive evaluations are misaligned. Which belief about dunya or akhirah needs recalibration? What step will you take tonight to align your heart’s inclination with truth?

Divine Attributes
advanced
5 min
The Problem with Defining Qadir as One Who Can Do or Refrain
Author argues refraining cannot be attributed to the agent, making the definition of qadir problematic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the standard definition of qadir (capable agent) as one who can both act and refrain is problematic according to the author.

Core Idea
Refraining is just persistence in non-existence, and non-existence cannot be attributed to an agent.

The author begins his fourth research by challenging a common definition of qadir. He argues that saying 'he did not do' refers to a negation of original non-existence, and such a negation cannot be the effect of an agent's power. This cuts to the heart of what it means to be capable.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Examine the author's claim that the definition of qadir is problematic. Notice how he distinguishes between action (fi'l) and refraining (tark).
2
Understand why non-existence cannot be attributed
The author gives two reasons: non-existence is pure negation, and something in its state of remaining cannot be attributed to an agent.
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how the author concludes that qadir has no power over refraining, thus the definition fails.
This lesson only covers the initial argument; the attempted answer comes next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الرابع : إن قول القائل : القادر هو الذي يصح منه أن يفعل وأن لا يفعل بحسب الدواعي المختلفة كلام مشكل . وذلك لأن قولنا : إنه لم يفعل : إشارة إلى أنه نفي للعدم الأصلي، كما كان . والعلم يمتنع وقوعه بالفاعل . لأن القدرة صفة مؤثرة، والعدم نفي محض ، وصلب صرف فالقول بأن العدم وقع بالفاعل والقادر محال . وأيضاً : الشيء حال بقائه يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل . لما ثبت أن تكوين الكائن محال وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فالعدم الباقي يمتنع إسناده إلى القادر . لوجهين : الأول : إن كونه عدمياً يمنع من إسناده إلى الفاعل . والثاني : إن كونه باقياً يمنع أيضاً من إسناده إلى الفاعل . فيثبت أن العدم الباقي يمتنع استناده إلى القادر من هذين الوجهين . ولما ثبت أن الترك لا معنى له إلا البقاء على العدم الأصلي ، وثبت أن البقاء على العدم الأصلي يمتنع إسناده إلى الفاعل يثبت حينئذ : أن القادر لا قدرة له على الترك البتة . وإذا كان كذلك كان قوله : القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل والترك قولاً مشكلاً . ولهذا الكلام مزيد غور . وحاول بعضهم أن يجيب عما يقرب من هذا الكلام . فقال : إن العاجز لا يجد من نفسه أنه إن لم يشأ الفعل فإنه لا يمكنه أن لا يفعل وأما القادر على الفعل فإنه يجد من نفسه أنه إن شاء أن لا يفعل فإنه يمكنه أن لا يفعل وهذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. ولما كان العلم بهذا الفرق ضرورياً . فحينئذ لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان أنه كيف حصل هذا الفرق ؟ ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الجواب ضعيف . وذلك لأن ترك الفعل إما أن يكون أمراً زائداً على البقاء على العلم الأصلي المستمر ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . بل هو عبارة عن نفس ذلك العلم المستمر . والأول باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن على هذا التقدير يكون القادر قادراً على فعل الشيء وعلى فعل ضده. ولا يكون البتة قادراً على الفعل وعلى ترك الفعل لأن فعل الضدّ فعل خاص . والفعل الخاص لا يكون تركاً لمسمى الفعل ، وإلا لزم أن يكون أحد النقيضين عين الثاني ، وهو محال . فثبت أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون القادر قادراً البتة على الترك . والثاني : إن الله تعالى كان قادراً على خلق العالم وعلى تركه ، فإذا صار تركه مفسراً بفعل ضده ، فحين كان الله تاركاً للعالم، وجب أن يكون فاعلاً لضده . فلما كان في الأزل تاركاً لفعل العالم ، وجب أن يقال : إنه كان في الأزل فاعلاً لضد العالم، لكن الفعل الأزلي عند القوم محال . وأيضاً : فعلى هذا التقدير كان ضد العالم أزلياً ، والأزل لا يزول، فوجب أن لا يزول ضد العالم. وإذا امتنع زوال ضده وجب أن يمتنع دخول العالم في الوجود . وذلك باطل . والثالث : إن التارك للفعل يجد من نفسه أنه ما فعل شيئاً ، وأنه لا معنى لكونه تاركاً للفعل إلا أنه لم يأت بذلك الفعل البتة . فالقول بأن ترك الفعل عبارة عن الإتيان بضده قول فاسد ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن ترك الفعل ليس عبارة عن الإتيان بفعل آخر ، بل هو عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي المستمر. وقد بينا أن إسناد العدم المستمر إلى القادر والفاعل محال فيثبت أن القادر لا قدرة له البتة على الترك وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان القول بأن القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل والترك بحسب الدواعي المختلفة : قولاً باطلاً . والله أعلم .
The fourth research: Indeed, the statement of the speaker: 'The capable (qādir) is he from whom it is valid to do and not to do according to different motives' is a problematic statement. That is because our statement: 'He did not do' is an indication of a negation of the original non-existence, as it was. And knowledge is prevented from occurring of the agent. Because power (qudra) is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation and sheer nothingness, so the statement that non-existence occurs from the agent and the capable is impossible. Also: The thing in its state of remaining is prevented from being attributed to the agent. Because it is established that bringing into existence what exists is impossible. And if the matter is such, then remaining non-existence is prevented from being attributed to the capable. For two reasons: First: That it is non-existential prevents its attribution to the agent. Second: That it is remaining also prevents its attribution to the agent. So it is established that remaining non-existence is impossible to be attributed to the capable from these two aspects. And since it is established that refraining (tark) has no meaning except persistence on the original non-existence, and it is established that persistence on the original non-existence is impossible to attribute to the agent, then it is established that the capable has no power whatsoever over refraining. And if it is such, then his statement: 'The capable is he from whom action and refraining are valid' is a problematic statement. And this speech has depth. And some attempted to answer something close to this speech. He said: 'The incapable (ʿājiz) does not find from himself that if he does not will the action, he cannot avoid doing it. But the capable of the action finds from himself that if he wills not to do, he can not do.' And this difference is known necessarily. And since the knowledge of this difference is necessary, then we have no need to explain how this difference occurs. And a speaker may say: This answer is weak. That is because refraining from the action is either something additional to persistence on the original continuous non-existence, or it is not such, but rather it is an expression of that same continuous non-existence. The first is false for reasons: First: On this estimation, the capable would be capable of doing the thing and doing its opposite, and he would not be capable at all of doing and refraining from the action, because doing the opposite is a specific action, and a specific action is not refraining from the named action, otherwise it would necessitate that one of the two opposites is the other, which is impossible. So it is established that on this estimation, the capable is not at all capable of refraining. Second: God Almighty was capable of creating the world and of refraining from it. So if His refraining is interpreted as doing its opposite, then when God was refraining from the world, He must have been doing its opposite. Since He was in eternity refraining from creating the world, it must be said that He was in eternity doing the opposite of the world, but eternal action according to the people is impossible. Also, on this estimation, the opposite of the world would be eternal, and eternity does not pass away, so it must be that the opposite of the world does not pass away. If its opposite’s passing away is impossible, then the entrance of the world into existence must be impossible. And that is false. Third: The one who refrains from the action finds from himself that he has done nothing, and that there is no meaning to his being a refrainer from the action except that he has not performed that action at all. So the statement that refraining from the action is an expression of performing its opposite is a corrupt statement. So it is established by what we have mentioned that refraining from the action is not an expression of performing another action, but rather it is an expression of persistence on the original continuous non-existence. And we have shown that attributing continuous non-existence to the capable and the agent is impossible. So it is established that the capable has no power at all over refraining. And if the matter is such, then the statement that the capable is he from whom action and refraining are valid according to different motives is a false statement. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This is a complex dialectical passage. It begins by stating that the definition of the capable (qādir) as one who can do or not do according to motives is problematic. The reason: saying 'he did not do' refers to a negation of original non-existence, and knowledge cannot occur of the agent's act because power is an effective attribute while non-existence is pure negation. Attributing non-existence to an agent is impossible. Additionally, something in its state of remaining cannot be attributed to the agent. Thus, remaining non-existence cannot be attributed to the agent for two reasons: it is non-existential and it is remaining. Therefore, refraining (tark) which is only persistence in original non-existence cannot be attributed to the agent, so the capable has no power over refraining. Hence the definition is problematic. Then someone attempts to answer by appealing to a self-evident distinction: the incapable feels he cannot avoid acting if he wills, while the capable feels he can refrain if he wills. The author's representative replies that this answer is weak because refraining either is a separate thing from persistence in original non-existence or is identical to it. The first option is false for three reasons: (1) it would mean the agent is capable of an act and its opposite, but not of the act and its refraining, since the opposite is a specific act, and a specific act is not refraining; (2) applied to God, if His refraining from creating the world were interpreted as performing its opposite, then in eternity He would have been performing that opposite, implying an eternal action which is impossible, and the opposite would be eternal and never cease, preventing the world's existence; (3) the one who refrains knows in his soul that he has done nothing, so refraining is simply not performing the act. Thus refraining is persistence in original non-existence, and attributing that to the agent is impossible. Therefore, the capable has no power over refraining, and the original definition is false.

Key Terms
القادر
الترك
العدم الأصلي
الفعل
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refraining (tark) is a positive act of the agent that can be attributed to the capable.
Correction: Refraining is merely persistence in original non-existence, which cannot be attributed to an agent because non-existence is not an effect of power.

Checkpoint
According to the author's argument, why is the definition of 'qādir' as one who can do or not do problematic?
A) Because power is not an effective attribute
B) Because refraining is a positive act that cannot be attributed to God
C) Because refraining is merely persistence in original non-existence, which cannot be attributed to an agent
D) Because the definition is self-evident and does not need explanation

Key Takeaway
The capable agent cannot have power over refraining because refraining is pure persistence in non-existence, which cannot be the effect of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say you 'chose not to' sin, do you take pride in that choice? This lesson argues that refraining is not an act of your power. How does this change your view of your own self-control? Can you truly claim credit for avoiding evil, or is it merely the persistence of Allah's mercy keeping you from sin? What would it mean to rely on His power for both action and refraining?
?
The lesson states that non-existence cannot be attributed to an agent. Have you ever thought of your failures as 'things you did'? Perhaps being unable to concentrate or procrastinating is not something you 'do' but a lack. How does this reframe your repentance? Does it make you more grateful for moments of action?
?
If refraining is not an act of power, then your avoidance of sin is not your own doing. Could you become proud of your 'good deeds' while forgetting that the power to act comes from Allah? This lesson humbles the 'capable' person. How can you cultivate a sense of total dependence on Allah in both your actions and your refraining?

Divine Attributes
advanced
6 min
Rebuttal of the Self-Evident Distinction Between Capable and Incapable
Author rebuts the self-evident distinction by showing refraining cannot be interpreted as doing the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's threefold rebuttal of the argument that the capable/incapable distinction is self-evident and sufficient to define qadir.

Core Idea
The self-evident distinction fails because refraining cannot be reduced to performing an opposite action.

After presenting his initial argument, the author anticipates a counterargument: that everyone can tell the difference between being able to refrain and being forced to act. The author's spokesman exposes the weakness in this reply by showing that refraining cannot be interpreted as doing the opposite. Three clear reasons demolish this attempted defense.

Guided Path
1
Present the attempted answer
Read the counterargument: the incapable feels he cannot not act, while the capable feels he can refrain. This is said to be known by necessity.
2
Examine the first two reasons
The first reason shows that interpreting refraining as doing the opposite leads to absurdities: the agent would be capable only of opposites, not of refraining. The second reason applies this to God, leading to unacceptable conclusions.
3
Consider the third reason and conclusion
The third reason appeals to introspection: the one who refrains knows he has done nothing. Thus refraining is not a positive act, but persistence in non-existence, which cannot be attributed to the agent.

Commentary
This is a complex dialectical passage. It begins by stating that the definition of the capable (qādir) as one who can do or not do according to motives is problematic. The reason: saying 'he did not do' refers to a negation of original non-existence, and knowledge cannot occur of the agent's act because power is an effective attribute while non-existence is pure negation. Attributing non-existence to an agent is impossible. Additionally, something in its state of remaining cannot be attributed to the agent. Thus, remaining non-existence cannot be attributed to the agent for two reasons: it is non-existential and it is remaining. Therefore, refraining (tark) which is only persistence in original non-existence cannot be attributed to the agent, so the capable has no power over refraining. Hence the definition is problematic. Then someone attempts to answer by appealing to a self-evident distinction: the incapable feels he cannot avoid acting if he wills, while the capable feels he can refrain if he wills. The author's representative replies that this answer is weak because refraining either is a separate thing from persistence in original non-existence or is identical to it. The first option is false for three reasons: (1) it would mean the agent is capable of an act and its opposite, but not of the act and its refraining, since the opposite is a specific act, and a specific act is not refraining; (2) applied to God, if His refraining from creating the world were interpreted as performing its opposite, then in eternity He would have been performing that opposite, implying an eternal action which is impossible, and the opposite would be eternal and never cease, preventing the world's existence; (3) the one who refrains knows in his soul that he has done nothing, so refraining is simply not performing the act. Thus refraining is persistence in original non-existence, and attributing that to the agent is impossible. Therefore, the capable has no power over refraining, and the original definition is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The self-evident difference in felt ability between capable and incapable is sufficient to define qadir.
Correction: The author shows that the definition still fails because refraining cannot be attributed to the agent, no matter how intuitively clear the distinction may seem.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given by the author against interpreting refraining as an additional act (i.e., doing the opposite)?
A) It would make the agent incapable of any action
B) It would imply that God performs eternal actions
C) It would make the agent capable only of opposites, not of refraining as such
D) It would contradict the self-evident distinction

Key Takeaway
The attempted defense of the definition of qadir by appealing to self-evidence fails because refraining is not a positive act but persistence in non-existence, which cannot be attributed to the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you believed you were 'refraining' from a sin by doing something else. Did you actually refrain, or did you just replace one act with another? According to this lesson, true refraining is doing nothing at all—how does that challenge your understanding of your own willpower? What would it mean to truly refrain without any replacement action? Can you honestly claim you have that power, or does this expose your complete dependence on Allah?
?
The passage argues that continuous non-existence cannot be attributed to an agent. If you cannot even claim to 'do' refraining, then what remains of your own capacity to choose? Your confidence in being able to 'just stop' a habit—is it based on a false sense of self? How does this lesson push you to rely not on your own supposed ability to refrain, but on Allah's grace to enable you to avoid sin?
?
If the self-evident difference between capable and incapable fails, then how do you actually know you are capable of any action? Your certainty that you can stand up or speak—where does it come from? This lesson undermines intuitive distinctions. Does your daily experience of 'ability' deceive you? What does this mean for your trust in your own judgments about your capacities? Will you continue to presume ability, or let this truth drive you to acknowledge that all power belongs to Allah?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Weak Answer on Ability to Refrain
Refutation of a weak answer regarding the difference between incapable and capable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the view that refraining from an action is equivalent to performing its opposite, and how this refutation establishes that the capable has no power over refraining.

Core Idea
Refraining is not an act; it is simply the continuation of non-existence.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi engages with a weak objection to the Ash'ari position on ability to refrain. The objector claimed that the capable person can sense his ability to refrain, while the incapable cannot, and that this difference is self-evident. The author dismantles this argument by analyzing the nature of 'refraining' itself, showing it cannot be an additional state distinct from mere non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the weak answer
Read the first part of the passage where the weak answer is presented. The objector differentiates the capable from the incapable by their self-awareness of ability to refrain. Note that the answer claims this difference is necessary knowledge, so no further explanation is needed.
2
Identify the author's response
The author labels the answer as weak and sets up a dichotomy: refraining is either an additional state or just the continuation of non-existence. Recognize that the author will argue against the first option.
3
Examine the three reasons
The author gives three arguments against refraining being an additional state. Read each carefully: (1) It conflates refraining with doing the opposite, making the capable capable of action and opposite but not of refraining. (2) Applied to God, it leads to absurdities like an eternal opposite preventing creation. (3) It contradicts self-evident experience that the one who refrains does nothing.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that refraining is just the continuation of original non-existence, and since non-existence cannot be attributed to a capable agent, the capable has no power over refraining. Hence the original definition of 'capable' is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول بعضهم أن يجيب عما يقرب من هذا الكلام . فقال : إن العاجز لا يجد من نفسه أنه إن لم يشأ الفعل فإنه لا يمكنه أن لا يفعل ( وأما القادر على الفعل فإنه يجد من نفسه أنه إن شاء أن لا يفعل فإنه يمكنه أن لا يفعل ) (١) وهذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. ولما كان العلم بهذا الفرق ضرورياً . فحينئذ لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان أنه كيف حصل هذا الفرق ؟ ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الجواب ضعيف . وذلك لأن ترك الفعل إما أن يكون أمراً زائداً على البقاء على العلم الأصلي المستمر ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك . بل هو عبارة عن نفس ذلك العلم المستمر . والأول باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن على هذا التقدير يكون القادر قادراً على فعل الشيء وعلى فعل ضده. ولا يكون البتة قادراً على الفعل وعلى ترك الفعل لأن فعل الضدّ فعل خاص . والفعل الخاص لا يكون تركاً لمسمى الفعل ، وإلا لزم أن يكون أحد النقيضين عين الثاني ، وهو محال . فثبت أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون القادر قادراً البتة على الترك . والثاني : إن الله تعالى كان قادراً على خلق العالم وعلى تركه ، فإذا صار تركه مفسراً بفعل ضده ، فحين كان الله تاركاً للعالم، وجب أن يكون فاعلاً لضده . فلما كان في الأزل تاركاً لفعل العالم ، وجب أن يقال : إنه كان في الأزل فاعلاً لضد العالم ، لكن الفعل الأزلي عند القوم محال . وأيضاً : فعلى هذا التقدير كان ضد العالم أزلياً ، والأزل لا يزول، فوجب أن لا يزول ضد العالم. وإذا امتنع زوال ضده وجب أن يمتنع دخول العالم في الوجود . وذلك باطل . والثالث : إن التارك للفعل يجد من نفسه أنه ما فعل شيئاً ، وأنه لا معنى لكونه تاركاً للفعل إلا أنه لم يأت بذلك الفعل البتة . فالقول بأن ترك الفعل عبارة عن الإتيان بضده قول فاسد ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن ترك الفعل ليس عبارة عن الإتيان بفعل آخر ، بل هو عبارة عن البقاء على العدم الأصلي المستمر. وقد بينا أن إسناد العدم المستمر إلى القادر والفاعل محال فيثبت أن القادر لا قدرة له البتة على الترك وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كان القول بأن القادر هو الذي يصح منه الفعل والترك بحسب الدواعي المختلفة : قولاً باطلاً . والله أعلم .
(۱) من ( س ) .
And some of them attempted to answer something close to this speech. He said: Indeed, the incapable does not find in himself that if he does not will the action, then he cannot avoid doing it. (But as for the capable of the action, he finds in himself that if he wills not to do it, then he can avoid doing it.) (1) And this difference is known by necessity. And since knowledge of this difference is necessary, then we have no need to explain how this difference occurred? And one may say: This answer is weak. That is because refraining from the action is either something additional to remaining upon the original continuous non-existence, or it is not so. Rather, it is an expression of that same continuous non-existence. And the first is false for reasons: The first: Indeed, on this assumption, the capable is capable of the action and of its opposite. But he is not at all capable of the action and of refraining from the action, because the opposite action is a specific action. And the specific action is not a refraining of the named action; otherwise it would necessitate that one of the two opposites is the same as the second, which is impossible. So it is established that on this assumption, the capable is not at all capable of refraining. The second: Indeed, God Almighty was capable of creating the world and of refraining from it. So if His refraining is interpreted by doing its opposite, then when God was refraining from the world, He must have been doing its opposite. Since He was in pre-eternity refraining from the act of the world, it must be said that He was in pre-eternity doing the opposite of the world. But the eternal act according to the people is impossible. Also on this assumption, the opposite of the world would be eternal, and pre-eternity does not cease, so it would be necessary that the opposite of the world does not cease. And if the cessation of its opposite is impossible, then the entry of the world into existence must be impossible. And that is false. The third: Indeed, the one who refrains from the action finds in himself that he did nothing, and that there is no meaning to his being a refraining from the action except that he did not perform that action at all. So the saying that refraining from the action is an expression of performing its opposite is a corrupt saying. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that refraining from the action is not an expression of performing another action, but rather it is an expression of remaining upon the original continuous non-existence. And we have shown that attributing continuous non-existence to the capable and agent is impossible. So it is established that the capable has no power at all over refraining. And if the matter is so, then the saying that the capable is he from whom action and refraining are possible according to different motives is a false saying. And God knows best.
(1) From (S).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a weak answer regarding the difference between the incapable and the capable in relation to the ability to refrain from action. The author first presents the weak answer: the incapable does not find himself able to avoid acting when he does not will the action, while the capable does find himself able to avoid acting when he wills not to act. The answer claims this difference is known necessarily, so no need to explain its cause. The author rejects this as weak and proceeds to analyze the concept of 'refraining' (tark). He argues that refraining is either an additional state beyond the original continuation of non-existence, or it is merely that continuation. The first option is false for three reasons: (1) If refraining is an additional state, then the capable is capable of an action and its opposite, but not of the action and its refraining, because the opposite action is a specific action, not the absence of the action. To reframe refraining as doing the opposite would conflate a pair of contradictories. (2) Applied to God: If His refraining from creating the world were interpreted as doing the opposite, then when He was refraining from creating, He would be creating the opposite of the world. That would make the opposite eternal, preventing the world's creation. (3) The one who refrains finds himself doing nothing; refraining is simply not doing the action, not doing another action. Therefore, refraining is the continuation of original non-existence. But attributing this continuing non-existence to a capable agent is impossible, because non-existence is not an effect of power. Hence the capable has no power to refrain. Consequently, the definition of 'capable' as one from whom both action and refraining are possible according to diverse motives is false.

عاجز

ضد
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refraining from an action is the same as doing its opposite.
Correction: Refraining is merely the continuation of non-existence, not a positive act. Doing the opposite is a distinct action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three reasons given against the claim that refraining is an additional state?
A) It would make the capable capable of an action and its opposite, but not of action and refraining.
B) It would lead to God's eternal opposite, preventing creation.
C) It contradicts the self-evident finding that one who refrains does nothing.
D) It would entail that the capable has power over non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Ability to refrain is not a power over an act; it is simply the absence of action, and only actions are effects of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If refraining from sin is not an act you produce but merely the absence of the sin, does your reliance on your own willpower make sense? How does this lesson shift your dependency onto Allah in moments of temptation?
?
When you say 'I will try to avoid that sin,' are you assuming you have power over refraining? After studying this passage, can you truly claim any ability to avoid sin apart from Allah creating the opposite act? What does this mean for your daily struggle?
?
The source argues that the capable has no power over refraining—only over actions. If you cannot even 'refrain' by your own power, what is left of your pride in your self-control? Will you humble yourself and ask Allah to grant you the righteous acts that replace the refraining?

The Sixth Argument: Necessity of a Motive for Divine Acts
Al-Razi presents a proof requiring a motive for divine acts via three absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that divine actions require a motive, and why three absurd consequences follow if we deny this.

Core Idea
To deny a motive for God's acts undermines praise, prophecy, and speech.

Al-Razi now presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument. He shows that if we allow acts to occur without a motive (dā'ī), three necessary implications arise, all of which are false. This forces us to affirm that every divine act, including creation, miracles, and speech, has a purpose. In this lesson, you will trace each consequence and see why the premise must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a premise, three consequences, and an elaboration.
Focus on the logical flow: if P then Q1, Q2, Q3; Q's are false, so P is false.
2
Identify the three consequences
List the three absurd consequences Al-Razi claims follow from the premise that an act can occur without a motive.
They are about divine praise, miracles, and speech.
3
Examine the elaboration of the first consequence
Read the detailed explanation for why God would not deserve praise if acts need no motive. Note the role of the principle that things remain as they were originally.
This is the longest elaboration; understand the reasoning about beneficence.
4
Reflect on the second and third consequences
Consider the implications for miracles and speech. Why would miracles no longer indicate truthfulness? Why would speech become meaningless?
These consequences strike at the foundations of prophecy and communication.
5
Connect to the overall argument
Al-Razi concludes that since the consequences are false, the original premise must be false. Reflect on how this argument establishes that divine acts require a motive.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: the denial leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنه لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي ، لزم أن لا يكون الله تعالى مستحقاً للحمد والثناء على شيء من أفعاله أصلا ، ويلزم أن لا تدل خلق المعجزات على صدق الداعي أصلا، ويلزم أيضاً : أن لا يدل صدور الكلام من المتكلم على معنى من المعاني أصلاً . وكل هذه اللوازم باطلة فذلك الملزوم أيضاً باطل

أما الأول فلأنه إذا جاز أن يصدر الفعل عن القادر ، لا لداعي ، لامتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى ، وإن خلق الأشياء النافعة إلا أنه خلقها ، لا لداعية أصلاً ، وإذا كان كذلك فحينئذ ما خلقها لداعية الإحسان ، لأن داعية الإحسان داعية بكيفية وعند انتفاء أصل الداعي، لا تكون الداعية المكيفة حاصلة . وإذا ثبت احتمال أنه تعالى ما خلق هذه الأشياء لداعية الإحسان وجب أن لا يستحق على شيء من أفعاله الحمد والثناء . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل

ومما يقوي هذا الكلام : أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان والأصل عدم خلق الأشياء لداعية الإحسان . فإذا حصل الخلق ، وكان التقدير أن حصول الخلق لا يتوقف على حصول الداعي، فيلزم أن تبقى حصول تلك الداعية على العدم الأصلي . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يلزم المطلوب . وأما الثاني فلأنه إذا جاز صدور الفعل عن القادر ، لالداعي ، جاز من الله تعالى أن يخلق المعجزات ، لا لشيء من الدواعي اصلاً ، وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يخرج المعجز عن أن يدل على أنه تعالى إنما خلقه لغرض التصديق ، فحينئذ يخرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً من الصدق . وأما الثالث فلأنه إذا جاز صدور الفعل عن القادر ، لا لداعي ، فلعل المتكلم بهذه الألفاظ وهذه الكلمات فعلها ، وأدخلها في الوجود ، لا لغرض الإفهام والتعريف ، ومع بقاء هذا الاحتمال فإنه يخرج هذا الكلام عن كونه مفيداً ، فيثبت أن القول بجواز صدور الفعل عن القادر ، لا للداعي ، يلزم منه هذه الأباطيل، فوجب أن يكون القول به باطلاً .
The sixth proof: If the action does not depend on the motive, then it would necessitate that God, exalted, is not deserving of praise and commendation for any of His actions at all, and it would necessitate that the creation of miracles does not indicate the truthfulness of the claimant at all, and it would also necessitate that the utterance of speech from a speaker does not indicate any meaning at all. All these consequences are false, so that which entails them is also false.

As for the first: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable [agent] without a motive, then it would be impossible to say that He, exalted, although He created beneficial things, He created them without any motive at all. And if that is the case, then He did not create them for the motive of beneficence, because the motive of beneficence is a qualified motive, and when the root of the motive is absent, the qualified motive does not occur. And if it is established that there is a possibility that He, exalted, did not create these things for the motive of beneficence, then He must not deserve praise and commendation for any of His actions. And it is well known that this is false.

What strengthens this statement is that the principle in every matter is that it remains as it was, and the principle is the absence of creating things for the motive of beneficence. So when creation occurs, and it is assumed that the occurrence of creation does not depend on the occurrence of the motive, then it necessitates that the occurrence of that motive remains in its original non-existence. And on this assumption, the required conclusion follows. 

As for the second: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable [agent] without a motive, then it is permissible for God, exalted, to create miracles without any motive at all. And on this assumption, the miracle would cease to indicate that He, exalted, created it for the purpose of verification. Then the miracle would cease to be evidence of truthfulness.

As for the third: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable [agent] without a motive, then perhaps the speaker uttered these words and sentences and brought them into existence without the purpose of conveying meaning and definition. And with this possibility remaining, this speech would cease to be beneficial. Thus it is established that the statement permitting the occurrence of an act from the capable [agent] without a motive entails these falsehoods, so it must be that this statement is false.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhān) by reasoning to the absurd (luzūm). The author states that if an action did not require a motive (dā'ī), three necessary consequences would follow, all of which are false, so the original premise must be false. The three consequences are: (1) God would not be worthy of praise for any action, (2) miracles would not indicate the truth of the prophet, (3) speech would not convey meaning. The author then elaborates on each. For the first, if God could act without a motive, then even if He creates beneficial things, it could be that He did not do so with the motive of beneficence, so He would not deserve praise. The second and third are similarly explained. The proof is strengthened by noting that the default assumption is that no motive exists until proven otherwise. Thus, the argument concludes that the claim that an act can occur without a motive is false.

Key Terms

الحمد
المعجزات
الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves God has needs like humans.
Correction: The argument proves God's actions are purposeful and wise, not that He is driven by need.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three absurd consequences mentioned in the sixth argument?
A) God would not be deserving of praise
B) Miracles would not indicate truthfulness
C) Speech would not convey meaning
D) God would be unjust

Key Takeaway
God's actions, including creation, miracles, and speech, are necessarily purposeful, as denying this leads to absurdities that undermine religion and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God never acts without purpose. But when you act, how often do you stop to ask yourself: 'Why am I doing this?' If you truly believed your every action is witnessed by a Purposeful Creator, would you still waste time on empty deeds? What will you change today to align your actions with purpose?
?
The argument shows that if God could act without motive, He would not deserve praise. Now think: Do you deserve praise from others for your good deeds? Or do you sometimes do things without genuine intention, seeking only praise itself? How can you purify your motives so that your actions are truly for God alone?
?
Al-Razi says that without a motive, even speech loses meaning. When you speak, do you consider the purpose of your words? How often do you speak carelessly, without seeking to benefit or convey truth? If you knew that every word must have a purpose before God, what would you say differently today?

Conclusion and Transition to Second Stage
Conclusion of the first station and introduction to the second station.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concludes that moral judgments reduce to benefit and harm, and how he sets up the second stage of the argument.

Core Idea
Good and ugly are nothing but benefit and harm; divine actions transcend such motives.

Having presented the deniers' arguments, the author now draws the conclusion: all moral judgments ultimately refer to seeking benefit and avoiding harm. Since such motives cannot apply to God, the deniers' position collapses. He then transitions to a second station where, for the sake of argument, he grants a distinction between benefit/harm and moral qualities to further analyze the 'motive of need'.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the author sums up the first station: all aspects boil down to benefit and harm.
2
Understand the implication
The author states that since motives based on good/ugly cannot be attributed to God, the deniers' argument fails. Make sure you grasp this logical step.
This is a key deductive move in kalam.
3
Transition to the second station
The author sets up a hypothetical scenario where he grants the distinction. Identify what he plans to discuss next: the 'motive of need'.
The passage ends abruptly; we have only the beginning of the second station.

فيثبت مجموع هذه الوجوه التي ذكروها ، لا تخرج عن رعاية المصالح والمفاسد . إما بواسطة واحدة ، أو بوسائط كثيرة . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الحسن والقبح ، لا معنى لهما إلا السعي في جلب المنفعة ودفع المفسدة. ولما حصل الاتفاق على أن ثبوت هذا الداعي في حق الله تعالى محال . كان القول بإثبات الدواعي المبنية على الحسن والقبح محالا في حق الله تعالى . فهذا تقرير الكلام في هذا المقام . أما المقام الثاني : إنا إذا سلمنا حصول المغايرة بين جلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة ، وبين كون الشيء حسناً أو قبيحاً . نقول : قد ذكرنا أن حاصل الكلام في تفسير داعي الحاجة : السعي إلى إيصال الخير والنفع إلى النفس . وتفسير
Thus it is established that all these aspects they mentioned do not go beyond consideration of benefits and harms, either by one intermediary or by many intermediaries. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the good and the ugly have no meaning except striving to bring benefit and repel corruption. And when agreement was reached that establishing this motive with respect to God Almighty is impossible, then the statement affirming motives based on good and ugly is impossible with respect to God Almighty. So this is the exposition of the discourse in this station. As for the second station: If we grant the distinction between bringing benefit and repelling harm, and between a thing being good or ugly, we say: We have mentioned that the outcome of the discourse in explaining the motive of need is: striving to bring good and benefit to the self. Commentary
This passage first summarizes the conclusion of the first station: all the arguments presented reduce to consideration of benefit and harm. Therefore, the terms 'good' and 'ugly' mean nothing more than seeking benefit and avoiding harm. Since it is agreed that such motives cannot be attributed to God, it is impossible that divine actions are based on these moral categories. Then the author transitions to a second station, where he hypothetically accepts the distinction between benefit/harm and moral qualities, and discusses the meaning of 'motive of need' (dā'ī al-ḥājah). The text cuts off, but the point is to continue the analysis. The lesson thus closes the first major argument and sets up the next phase.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the deniers are atheists who reject all morality.
Correction: The deniers here argue that God's actions are not based on human moral categories like good and evil, but rather on benefit and harm; they do not deny morality itself. They uphold a form of consequentialist ethics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that our concepts of good and bad are tied to benefit and harm, while God's actions are beyond such motives. How does this challenge your immediate emotional reactions to hardship? What would change if you truly believed that God's justice is not measured by your personal benefit?
?
You often ask 'why did God allow this suffering?' based on a human sense of fairness. But if God's actions are not driven by the same considerations of good and evil that you use, how should you reframe your prayers and supplications? Will you commit to trusting God's wisdom even when His decree seems 'ugly' by your measure?
?
Think of a time you blamed God for something that 'hurt' you. According to this passage, your judgment of that event as 'bad' came from a comparison with worldly benefit. Since God is free from such motives, what would it mean to abandon that comparison and submit to His absolute wisdom? Are you ready to say 'I know He is just even though I cannot see how'?

The Mu'tazila Proof for Divine Motive of Beneficence
The Mu'tazila argue that God's creation must be motivated by beneficence to others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's rational argument that God's creation must be motivated by beneficence to others, not by need or without purpose.

Core Idea
God's creation cannot be purposeless or self-serving; it must be for the benefit of others.

The Mu'tazila present a logical argument to establish that God's act of creation is motivated by beneficence. They start from the fact that the world is created and God is beyond need. Then they consider possibilities and eliminate those incompatible with divine perfection. This concise proof exemplifies Mu'tazili rationalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors: 'إما... أو' (either...or).
2
Identify the premises
Note the key premises: world is originated (muḥdath), God is not a body, and therefore God has no need (ḥāja).
3
Follow the disjunction
Understand the either/or reasoning about motive: either God acted without any motive (which is a defect) or with a motive. Then the motive is either for Himself or for others.
The argument eliminates the defective options.
4
See the conclusion
Recognize that the only valid motive is beneficence (iḥsān) to others.
This conclusion is presented as decisive (dālīl qāṭiʿ).

قالت المعتزلة : ههنا دليل يدل على صحة هذا القسم . وهو : أنه قد ثبت أن العالم محدث ، وكل محدث فله محدث . فالعالم محدث . ومحدث العالم ليس بجسم ، فوجب أن لا تصح عليه الحاجة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : فاعل العالم إما أن يقال : إنه أحدثه لا لداعية أصلاً ، أو أحدثه لداعية ، والأول عيب ، وهو نقص ، وهو على الله محال ، فثبت أنه فعله لداعية ، وتلك الداعية : إما داعية إيصال النفع إلى نفسه ، أو داعية إيصال النفع إلى غيره، والأول باطل ، لما ثبت أن الحاجة إليه محال ، فيثبت أنه إنما خلق العالم لداعية إيصال النفع إلى غيره، ولا معنى لداعية الإحسان إلا هذا، فيثبت بهذا الدليل القاطع : حصول هذا القسم من الدواعي. هذا منتهى الكلام في هذا الباب
The Mu'tazila said: Here is an evidence indicating the validity of this category. It is that it has been established that the world is originated (muḥdath), and every originated thing has an originator. So the world is originated. The originator of the world is not a body, so it is necessary that need (ḥāja) does not apply to Him. If this is established, we say: The agent of the world – either it is said that He originated it without any motive (dā'iyya) at all, or He originated it with a motive. The first is a defect (ʿayb), and it is a deficiency (naqṣ), and it is impossible for God. So it is established that He did it with a motive. And that motive is either a motive of bringing benefit to Himself or a motive of bringing benefit to others. The first is invalid, since it has been established that need in relation to Him is impossible. So it is established that He only created the world with the motive of bringing benefit to others. And there is no meaning to the motive of beneficence (dā'iyat al-iḥsān) except this. So by this decisive evidence it is established that this category of motives exists. This is the end of the discussion in this chapter.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a proof presented by the Mu'tazila. They argue from the temporality of the world and the nature of God to establish that God's motive in creation is beneficence. The argument proceeds: the world is originated, so it has an originator. That originator is not a body, so He cannot have needs. Therefore, His act of creation cannot be motivated by need. The alternatives are: either He acted without any motive (which is a defect) or with a motive. The motive cannot be for His own benefit (since He is free of need), so it must be for the benefit of others. This is what they call the motive of beneficence. The proof is considered decisive.

Key Terms
المحدث
داعية الإحسان
الحاجة
النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That God could act without any motive or purpose.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that acting without a motive would be a defect, which is impossible for God. Therefore God must have a motive.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why cannot God's motive in creation be the benefit of Himself?
A) Because He is not a body
B) Because He is free from need (ḥāja)
C) Because He is omnipotent
D) Because He is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila prove that God's creation is motivated by beneficence to others, not by need or caprice.

?

?
The proof says God cannot act without a motive or for self-benefit; He acts only for others’ good. Does your life reflect that same pattern? In what relationship are you acting primarily for your own benefit rather than for the other’s good, and what would it cost you to change that?

The Third Argument Against Divine Action for Benefit
Presents the third argument refuting divine action for benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument used by Sunni theologians to refute the claim that God acts for benefit, and how it relies on the impossibility of infinite regress and the necessity of a preponderating motive.

Core Idea
God's acts need no benefit or motive; they are simply by His wisdom.

This lesson presents the third argument in a series refuting the view that God's actions are motivated by benefit. The argument uses a clever dilemma: either the specification of creation to a particular time and place is due to a wisdom, leading to infinite regress, or it is without reason, implying arbitrariness. Both options are impossible for God, thus the premise that God acts for benefit must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'إما أن يكون' (either it is) to set up the dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities for why creation was specified to a particular time and place. Find each one and note the consequence drawn from it.
The first horn leads to infinite regress (تسلسل), the second to absence of motive (داعية مرجحة).
3
Understand why each horn is impossible
Ask yourself: Why is infinite regress impossible? Why is acting without a preponderating motive impossible for God? Connect these to core Islamic beliefs about God's necessity and wisdom.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن تخصيص إحداث العالم بوقت معين من الأوقات المقدرة التي لا أول لها، وبحيز معين من الأحياز المفروضة في الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له، إما أن يكون لأجل حكمة يختص بها ذلك الوقت المعين وذلك الحيز المعين، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فنقول : الأوقات متشابهة، والأحياز متشابهة، فاختصاص ذلك الوقت معين وذلك الحيز المعين بتلك الحكمة المعينة واجب أن يكون لأمر آخر، ويلزم منه التسلسل، وهو محال . وإن كان ذلك التخصيص بذلك الوقت المعين وبذلك الحيز المعين لا لأمر . فحينئذ يكون هذا اعترافاً بأن أفعاله تعالى، قد وقعت من غير داعية مرجحة، وإذا انتفى أصل الداعي، كان انتفاء الداعية المكيفة ـ أعني داعية الأحياز - ألزم . فهذه وجوه مختصرة في إبطال هذا القول . وسيأتي الاستقصاء في ذكر الدلائل على أن تعليل أفعال الله - تعالى - وأحكامه برعاية المصالح والأغراض قول باطل . والله أعلم
The third argument: The specification of the creation of the world to a specific time among the estimated times which have no beginning, and to a specific place among the supposed places in infinite void, is either because of a wisdom specific to that particular time and that particular place, or it is not. If the first, we say: Times are similar, and places are similar, so the specification of that particular time and that particular place with that particular wisdom necessarily must be for another reason, and that entails infinite regress, which is impossible. And if that specification to that particular time and that particular place is not for a reason, then this is an admission that His (exalted) acts occurred without a preponderating motive. And if the principle of motive is negated, the negation of the qualifying motive—I mean the motive of places—is more necessary. These are brief points in refuting this statement. And a thorough treatment will come in mentioning the proofs that the attribution of God's acts and His rulings to the observance of benefits and purposes is a false statement. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that God's actions are motivated by benefit to others (a Mu'tazili position). The argument is structured as a dichotomy. The author begins by stating the issue: the specification of the creation of the world to a particular time and a particular place. This specification must either be due to some wisdom (ḥikma) that is specific to that time and place, or not due to any reason. If it is due to a wisdom, then since all times and places are similar (i.e., there is no inherent difference), the specification of that particular time and place with that particular wisdom would itself require another reason, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. If the specification is not due to any reason at all, then this would be an admission that God's acts occur without any preponderating motive (dāʿiya murjīḥa). But if the principle of a motive is absent altogether, then the absence of a specific qualifying motive (such as the motive of place) is even more necessary. The author concludes that these are brief points refuting that view, and promises a more thorough discussion later, affirming that attributing purposes and benefits to God's actions is false.

Key Terms
تخصيص
حكمة
تسلسل
داعية مرجحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's actions are either arbitrary or motivated by benefit.
Correction: The argument shows that God's actions are neither arbitrary nor for benefit; they are by His wisdom but not for any external purpose.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the specification of a particular time and place for creation is due to a wisdom (ḥikma) specific to them?
A) It shows that God's actions are for benefit.
B) It leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because times and places are similar.
C) It proves that the act is necessary.
D) It affirms the existence of a preponderating motive.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that claiming God acts for benefit leads to either infinite regress or arbitrariness, both impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When hardship strikes, does your heart immediately ask 'Why me?' as if God should have acted for your benefit? How does this argument expose that assumption as false, and what would it take to truly accept that God's actions are not about your comfort?

If God's acts are not motivated by any benefit to you, then your worship and obedience are not transactions. Examine your prayer: are you supplicating as a servant seeking His pleasure, or as a customer expecting a return? Which motive does this lesson undermine, and what will you do about it today?

The argument uses the similarity of times and places to show that no circumstance is inherently more deserving of God's specification. Reflect on a time you felt entitled to God's mercy because of your situation. How does this lesson dismantle that entitlement? Can you now approach God without claiming any right? What concrete step will you take to live that humility?
