God's Knowledge Necessitates What He Knows
Author presents proof that God's knowledge entails necessity of what He knows, refuting mere possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge entails the necessity of what He knows, and why this invalidates the claim that His power operates merely on the basis of possibility.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is the measure of reality, not possibility.

The second argument in this section tackles a key question: does God's knowledge impose necessity on what happens? The author builds on the consensus that God knows all things and then uses the nature of knowledge itself—its requirement of correspondence—to prove that what God knows must occur necessarily. This leads to a direct refutation of the claim that God's power only operates on the basis of permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two parts
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the consensus and then provides the logical proof, concluding with a refutation of the opposing view.
The passage is a continuous argument; pay attention to the flow.
2
Understand the consensus premise
The author starts by stating that all religious groups agree that God knows all things, including which events will occur and which will not. This is the foundational premise.
3
Grasp the logical link between knowledge and necessity
The proof is that knowledge must correspond to reality. If God knows something will occur, then it must occur; otherwise His knowledge would be false. Therefore, occurrence becomes necessary. Similarly for non-occurrence.
4
See how the conclusion refutes the opposing view
The author concludes that God's power affects things either necessarily (if known to occur) or impossibly (if known not to occur). This directly contradicts those who say power only affects things as merely possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : أطبق أهل الملل والنحل على أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات فلا بد ، وأن يعلم أن أي الأشياء يقع ، وأن أيها لا يقع . وكل ما علم الله وقوعه فإنه واجب الوقوع. وكل ما علم عدمه فإنه ممتنع الوقوع. والدليل عليه : أن العلم إنما يكون علماً إذا كان مطابقاً للمعلوم . وإذا كان كذلك فالعلم المتعلق بوجوده إنما يكون علماً متعلقاً بوجوده إذا كان هو موجوداً [وإذا كان كذلك فيكون ذلك العلم علماً متعلقاً بوجوده] مع كون ذلك الشيء معدوماً يقتضي الجمع بين النقيضين وذلك محال . ولما كان كون علمه علماً أمراً واجباً وكان من لوازم كونه علماً وجود معلومه وعدم عدم ذلك المعلوم . لزم من وجوب ذلك العلم وجوب وجود ذلك المعلوم وامتناع عدمه. فيثبت : أن وجود ما علم الله تعالى وجوده يكون واجب الوجود وأن وجود ما علم الله عدمه يكون ممتنع الوجود. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تأثير قدرة الله في بعض الأشياء على سبيل الوجوب وفي غيرها على سبيل الامتناع . وذلك يبطل قول من يقول : إن تأثير قدرة الله في الأشياء على سبيل الصحة لا على سبيل الوجوب
The second proof: that we say: The people of religions and sects are in agreement that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of all known things, so necessarily He knows which things occur and which do not. And everything that God knows its occurrence is necessary of occurrence. And everything that He knows its non-existence is impossible of occurrence. And the evidence for that: knowledge is only knowledge if it corresponds to the known. And if that is the case, then knowledge related to its existence is only knowledge related to its existence if it is existent [and if that is the case, then that knowledge is knowledge related to its existence] while that thing is non-existent entails combining two opposites, and that is impossible. And since His knowledge being knowledge is a necessary matter, and from its necessities are the existence of its known and the non-existence of that known's non-existence, then from the necessity of that knowledge follows the necessity of the existence of that known and the impossibility of its non-existence. So it is established: that the existence of what God, exalted, knows its existence is necessary of existence, and that the existence of what God knows its non-existence is impossible of existence. And this entails that the effect of God's power in some things is on the basis of necessity, and in others on the basis of impossibility. And that invalidates the saying of one who says: the effect of God's power in things is on the basis of validity (ṣiḥḥa), not on the basis of necessity.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) against the view that God's power affects things merely as possible (ṣaḥīḥ), not as necessary. The author begins by citing consensus (iṭbāq) among people of religions and sects that God knows all things. Consequently, He knows which things occur and which do not. Whatever He knows will occur is necessary (wājib al-wuqū‘), and whatever He knows will not occur is impossible (mumtani‘ al-wuqū‘). The proof is: knowledge, to be knowledge, must correspond to the known. If knowledge is related to the existence of something while that thing is non-existent, that would entail a contradiction. Since God's knowledge is necessarily knowledge, it necessitates the existence of its object and the non-existence of its non-existence. Therefore, the known object is necessary in existence if God knows it exists, and impossible if He knows it does not. This implies that God's power (qudra) produces effects either necessarily (wujūb) or impossibly (imtinā‘), not merely as permissible (ṣiḥḥa). Thus, the view that God's power operates only on the basis of permissibility is invalid.

معلوم
وجوب
امتناع
قدرة
صحة
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's knowledge does not impose necessity because God is free and can change His mind.
Correction: The argument shows that knowledge, by its very nature, requires correspondence. If God knows something will occur, it must occur; otherwise His knowledge would be false, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why must what God knows to occur be necessary?
A) Because God's power is limited.
B) Because knowledge requires correspondence to reality.
C) Because the mutakallimun agreed on it.
D) Because God's will overrides all possibility.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge, being necessarily true, requires that what He knows to occur is necessary and what He knows not to occur is impossible, thereby refuting the view that His power only makes things possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of one past failure: can you see it as part of God's necessary decree, and does that give you peace or discomfort? What specific step will you take today to surrender that regret to God's knowledge?
?
This lesson says God's knowledge cannot be mistaken. When you worry about the future, are you acting as if you know better than God? What is one future event you are anxious about, and how can you replace that anxiety with trust in God's perfect knowledge that has already determined it?
?
The author argues that God's power is not merely possible but necessary based on His knowledge. Does your daily life reflect that belief? Examine your actions: do you pray and strive as if outcomes are already fixed by God's knowledge, or do you act as if your efforts alone determine success? What habit can you change to align with this truth?

The Second Proof: God's Knowledge Necessitates Its Objects
Presents the second proof that God's knowledge necessitates existence of its objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's knowledge of an event necessitates its occurrence, and how this implies that divine power operates by necessity rather than mere possibility.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not neutral; it actively determines reality.

This lesson presents a key argument in Islamic theology: the proof that God's exhaustive knowledge necessarily entails the actual occurrence of what He knows. The argument builds on the consensus that God knows all things, then uses the principle that true knowledge must match reality, deducing that what God knows to occur must exist necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from the start of the second argument to the conclusion that divine power acts by obligation. Then read the translation.
Notice how the argument moves from consensus to logical deduction.
2
Identify the key premises
List the premises: God knows all things, knowledge must correspond to its object, and God's knowledge is necessarily true.
These premises lead to the conclusion that what God knows to occur must exist.
3
Understand the contradiction
If God knew something would exist but it did not, that would combine true knowledge with a false object, which is a contradiction. Since God's knowledge is necessary, the object must also be necessary.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
The conclusion that God's power acts by necessity (obligation) in some things and by impossibility in others refutes the view that divine power acts merely by possibility (permissibility).
Think about why this matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على إنضمام الداعي إليه، إلا أنه لا يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول ذلك، فيثبت أنه لا مزيد على هذين الجوابين، إلا أن البحث عن كل واحد منهما قد سبق على الاستقصاء والاستيفاء فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة. وقوله: لو لم نجوز ذلك، لزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين القادر وبين الموجب، مع أن هذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. فهذا الكلام أيضاً قد سبق البحث فيه فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.

الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: أطبق أهل الملل والنحل على أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات، فلا بد وأن يعلم أن أي الأشياء يقع، وأن أيها لا يقع؟ وكل ما علم الله وقوعه، فإنه واجب الوقوع. وكل ما علم عدمه، فإنه ممتنع الوقوع. والدليل عليه: أن العلم إنما يكون علماً، إذا كان مطابقاً للمعلوم. وإذا كان كذلك، فالعلم المتعلق بوجوده، إنما يكون علماً متعلقاً بوجوده، إذا كان هو موجداً، وإذا كان كذلك، فيكون ذلك العلم علماً متعلقاً بوجوده، مع كون ذلك الشيء معدوماً، يقتضي الجمع بين النقيضين. وذلك محال. ولما كان كون علمه علماً أمراً واجباً، وكان من لوازم كونه علماً وجود معلومه، وعدم عدم ذلك المعلوم، لزم من وجوب ذلك العلم وجوب وجود ذلك المعلوم، وامتناع عدمه. فيثبت: أن وجود ما علم الله تعالى وجوده، يكون واجب الوجود، وأن وجود ما علم الله عدمه، يكون ممتنع الوجود. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تأثير قدرة الله، في بعض الأشياء على سبيل الوجوب، وفي غيرها على سبيل الامتناع. وذلك يبطل قول من يقول: إن تأثير قدرة الله في الأشياء على سبيل الصحة، لا على سبيل الوجوب.

فإن قالوا: العلم إنما يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه. فلما كان الشيء في نفسه جائز الوجود، وجب أن يتعلق ذلك العلم به، على هذا الوجه، فلو صار ذلك الشيء بسبب ذلك العلم واجب الوجود، يلزم أن يجتمع في الشيء الواحد كونه جائزاً وواجباً. وذلك محال.

قلنا: لا نسلم أن ذلك محال، وأي امتناع في أن يكون الشيء الواحد جائز الوجود لذاته، وواجب الوجود، لأجل حصول سببه، وهو العلم والقدرة؟
On the joining of the motive to it, except that it does not become necessary to occur upon the attainment of that, so it is established that there is nothing beyond these two answers, except that the investigation of each one of them has already been done exhaustively and comprehensively, so there is no need for repetition. And his statement: If we do not allow that, it would necessitate that no difference remains between the capable (al-qādir) and the necessitator (al-mūjib), while this difference is known necessarily. So this speech also has already been investigated, so there is no need for repetition.

The second proof: We say: The people of religions and sects agree that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of all known things, so it must be that He knows which things occur and which do not. And everything that God knows its occurrence is necessarily occurring. And everything that He knows its non-existence is impossible to occur. And the evidence for it is: Knowledge is only knowledge when it corresponds to the known thing. And if that is the case, then knowledge related to its existence is only knowledge related to its existence if it is that which brings it into existence, and if that is the case, then that knowledge being knowledge related to its existence while that thing is non-existent necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. And since the fact that His knowledge is knowledge is an obligatory matter, and it is a necessary concomitant of it being knowledge that its known exists and that known thing does not lack existence, then from the obligation of that knowledge follows the obligation of the existence of that known thing and the impossibility of its non-existence. Thus it is established that the existence of what God knows to exist is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), and that the existence of what God knows not to exist is impossible of existence (mumtaniʿ al-wujūd). And this requires that the effect of God's power in some things is by way of obligation, and in others by way of impossibility. And that invalidates the statement of those who say that the effect of God's power in things is by way of permissibility, not by way of obligation.

If they say: Knowledge only relates to the known thing as it is. So since the thing is in itself contingent of existence, that knowledge must relate to it in this manner. So if that thing becomes, due to that knowledge, necessary of existence, it would necessitate that in one thing both its being contingent and necessary be combined, which is impossible.

We say: We do not accept that it is impossible. And what impossibility is there that one thing be contingent of existence in itself, and necessary of existence due to the attainment of its cause, which is knowledge and power?
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the necessity of God's knowledge entailing the necessary occurrence of what He knows, followed by an objection and its answer. The author first references previous discussion, then states the second proof. The proof begins with the consensus of all religions and sects that God knows all things, so He must know what will occur and what will not. Then it argues: whatever God knows to occur is necessarily occurring; whatever He knows not to occur is impossible. The evidence is that knowledge must match its object. If knowledge of existence is true, it must be that the thing exists, otherwise it would be a contradiction. Since God's knowledge is necessarily true, its objects must necessarily exist. This implies that God's power acts by necessity, not by mere possibility. The objection claims that a thing could be contingent in itself, and knowledge should conform to that contingency, so it cannot make it necessary. The answer rejects that combining contingency in essence with necessity due to cause is impossible. The author asserts that it is perfectly coherent for something to be contingent in itself but necessitated by its cause (knowledge and power). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
المعلوم (the known)
واجب الوقوع (necessarily occurring)
ممتنع الوقوع (impossible to occur)
القادر (the capable agent)
الموجب (the necessitator)
جائز الوجود (contingent of existence)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
القدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge is merely passive and that it simply reflects possibilities without determining actuality.
Correction: This argument shows that God's knowledge is active: because it is necessarily true, it necessitates the actuality of what it knows.

Checkpoint

B) God's knowledge of occurrence makes the event necessarily occur
C) God's knowledge only knows possibilities, not actualities
D) God's knowledge is dependent on the event's decision

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is determinative: what He knows to occur must exist necessarily, and His power acts by obligation.

?

Objection and Reply: The Compatibility of Contingency and Necessity
Presents an objection to the second argument and the author's reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge should conform to the contingent nature of things, and the reply that a thing can be contingent in itself yet necessary by an external cause.

Core Idea
A thing can be contingent in itself yet necessary by God's knowledge and power.

After presenting the argument that God's knowledge necessitates its objects, the author raises a natural objection: if things are contingent in themselves, how can God's knowledge make them necessary? The reply clarifies that contingency in essence and necessity by cause are not contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage of the objection and the author's reply. Then read the translation.
Notice how the objection uses the principle that knowledge must conform to its object.
2
Analyze the objection
The objectors claim that if a thing is contingent in itself, knowledge must reflect that contingency. Making it necessary would combine contingency and necessity in one thing, which is impossible.
This is a direct challenge to the previous argument.
3
Understand the reply
The author rejects the impossibility claim, arguing that there is no contradiction in something being contingent in itself but necessary due to its cause (knowledge and power).
This distinguishes between essential and accidental attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
This reply preserves both divine determinism and the contingent nature of creation, showing they are compatible.
Think about how this reconciles free will and predestination in some theological frameworks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على إنضمام الداعي إليه، إلا أنه لا يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول ذلك، فيثبت أنه لا مزيد على هذين الجوابين، إلا أن البحث عن كل واحد منهما قد سبق على الاستقصاء والاستيفاء فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة. وقوله: لو لم نجوز ذلك، لزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين القادر وبين الموجب، مع أن هذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. فهذا الكلام أيضاً قد سبق البحث فيه فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة.

الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: أطبق أهل الملل والنحل على أنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات، فلا بد وأن يعلم أن أي الأشياء يقع، وأن أيها لا يقع؟ وكل ما علم الله وقوعه، فإنه واجب الوقوع. وكل ما علم عدمه، فإنه ممتنع الوقوع. والدليل عليه: أن العلم إنما يكون علماً، إذا كان مطابقاً للمعلوم. وإذا كان كذلك، فالعلم المتعلق بوجوده، إنما يكون علماً متعلقاً بوجوده، إذا كان هو موجداً، وإذا كان كذلك، فيكون ذلك العلم علماً متعلقاً بوجوده، مع كون ذلك الشيء معدوماً، يقتضي الجمع بين النقيضين. وذلك محال. ولما كان كون علمه علماً أمراً واجباً، وكان من لوازم كونه علماً وجود معلومه، وعدم عدم ذلك المعلوم، لزم من وجوب ذلك العلم وجوب وجود ذلك المعلوم، وامتناع عدمه. فيثبت: أن وجود ما علم الله تعالى وجوده، يكون واجب الوجود، وأن وجود ما علم الله عدمه، يكون ممتنع الوجود. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون تأثير قدرة الله، في بعض الأشياء على سبيل الوجوب، وفي غيرها على سبيل الامتناع. وذلك يبطل قول من يقول: إن تأثير قدرة الله في الأشياء على سبيل الصحة، لا على سبيل الوجوب.

فإن قالوا: العلم إنما يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه. فلما كان الشيء في نفسه جائز الوجود، وجب أن يتعلق ذلك العلم به، على هذا الوجه، فلو صار ذلك الشيء بسبب ذلك العلم واجب الوجود، يلزم أن يجتمع في الشيء الواحد كونه جائزاً وواجباً. وذلك محال.

قلنا: لا نسلم أن ذلك محال، وأي امتناع في أن يكون الشيء الواحد جائز الوجود لذاته، وواجب الوجود، لأجل حصول سببه، وهو العلم والقدرة؟
On the joining of the motive to it, except that it does not become necessary to occur upon the attainment of that, so it is established that there is nothing beyond these two answers, except that the investigation of each one of them has already been done exhaustively and comprehensively, so there is no need for repetition. And his statement: If we do not allow that, it would necessitate that no difference remains between the capable (al-qādir) and the necessitator (al-mūjib), while this difference is known necessarily. So this speech also has already been investigated, so there is no need for repetition.

The second proof: We say: The people of religions and sects agree that He, exalted, is knowledgeable of all known things, so it must be that He knows which things occur and which do not. And everything that God knows its occurrence is necessarily occurring. And everything that He knows its non-existence is impossible to occur. And the evidence for it is: Knowledge is only knowledge when it corresponds to the known thing. And if that is the case, then knowledge related to its existence is only knowledge related to its existence if it is that which brings it into existence, and if that is the case, then that knowledge being knowledge related to its existence while that thing is non-existent necessitates combining two opposites, which is impossible. And since the fact that His knowledge is knowledge is an obligatory matter, and it is a necessary concomitant of it being knowledge that its known exists and that known thing does not lack existence, then from the obligation of that knowledge follows the obligation of the existence of that known thing and the impossibility of its non-existence. Thus it is established that the existence of what God knows to exist is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), and that the existence of what God knows not to exist is impossible of existence (mumtaniʿ al-wujūd). And this requires that the effect of God's power in some things is by way of obligation, and in others by way of impossibility. And that invalidates the statement of those who say that the effect of God's power in things is by way of permissibility, not by way of obligation.

If they say: Knowledge only relates to the known thing as it is. So since the thing is in itself contingent of existence, that knowledge must relate to it in this manner. So if that thing becomes, due to that knowledge, necessary of existence, it would necessitate that in one thing both its being contingent and necessary be combined, which is impossible.

We say: We do not accept that it is impossible. And what impossibility is there that one thing be contingent of existence in itself, and necessary of existence due to the attainment of its cause, which is knowledge and power?
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the necessity of God's knowledge entailing the necessary occurrence of what He knows, followed by an objection and its answer. The author first references previous discussion, then states the second proof. The proof begins with the consensus of all religions and sects that God knows all things, so He must know what will occur and what will not. Then it argues: whatever God knows to occur is necessarily occurring; whatever He knows not to occur is impossible. The evidence is that knowledge must match its object. If knowledge of existence is true, it must be that the thing exists, otherwise it would be a contradiction. Since God's knowledge is necessarily true, its objects must necessarily exist. This implies that God's power acts by necessity, not by mere possibility. The objection claims that a thing could be contingent in itself, and knowledge should conform to that contingency, so it cannot make it necessary. The answer rejects that combining contingency in essence with necessity due to cause is impossible. The author asserts that it is perfectly coherent for something to be contingent in itself but necessitated by its cause (knowledge and power). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)
المعلوم (the known)
جائز الوجود (contingent of existence)
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
لذاته (in itself)
لأجل حصول سببه (due to its cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God's knowledge makes things necessary, then nothing can be truly contingent or free.
Correction: The reply clarifies that contingency can be an intrinsic property while necessity is external, so both can coexist.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that a thing cannot be both contingent and necessary?
A) He agrees and revises the argument
B) He denies the impossibility, citing the difference between essential contingency and necessity by cause
C) He claims that nothing is contingent in itself
D) He argues that God's knowledge does not cause necessity

Key Takeaway
Contingency in essence and necessity by cause are compatible, preserving both divine determinism and the contingent nature of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that everything in your life—every choice, every outcome—is predetermined by God's knowledge and power, or do you secretly believe that some things happen by chance? What would change in your daily trust in Allah if you fully internalized this teaching?

The objection argued that knowledge must conform to its object. If God's knowledge of your future sins makes them necessary, how do you reconcile that with your moral responsibility? Objection and Answer: Knowledge and the Possible
Objection and answer on knowledge and the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection that divine knowledge would contradict the inherent possibility of created things.

Core Idea
Possibility in essence and necessity from cause can coexist without contradiction.

The objectors claim that knowledge must conform to its object, so if something is possible in itself, knowledge cannot make it necessary. The author responds by distinguishing between a thing's intrinsic possibility and its necessity derived from an external cause—divine knowledge and power. This lesson examines that exchange and the logical resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector uses the principle that knowledge follows the known. Understand the logical dilemma they pose.
2
Read the author's response
The author denies the impossibility and offers a distinction between essence and cause. Find that distinction.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why this answer is valid and how it preserves both divine foreknowledge and creaturely contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : العلم إنما يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه . فلما كان الشيء في نفسه جائز الوجود ، وجب أن يتعلق ذلك العلم به ، على هذا الوجه ، فلو صار ذلك الشيء بسبب ذلك العلم واجب الوجود يلزم أن يجتمع في الشيء الواحد كونه جائزاً أو واجباً . وذلك محال . قلنا : لا نسلم أن ذلك محال ، وأي امتناع في أن يكون الشيء الواحد جائز الوجود لذاته ، وواجب الوجود، لأجل حصول سببه ، وهو العلم والقدرة ؟
If they say: Knowledge is only related to the known as it is. Since the thing in itself is possible of existence, that knowledge must relate to it according to that aspect. So if that thing becomes necessary of existence due to that knowledge, it would necessitate that in one thing both possibility and necessity be combined. That is impossible. We say: We do not agree that it is impossible. What impossibility is there in that one thing be possible of existence in itself and necessary of existence due to its cause, namely knowledge and power?
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objectors argue that knowledge must conform to its object as it is. If an object is inherently possible, knowledge should reflect that possibility. If knowledge were to make it necessary, a single thing would be both possible and necessary, which is absurd. The author responds by denying the absurdity: there is no impossibility in something being possible in itself but necessary due to an external cause (here, divine knowledge and power). Thus the objector’s dilemma is resolved.

المعلوم
جائز الوجود
واجب الوجود
السبب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility and necessity are mutually exclusive in all respects, so a thing cannot be both possible and necessary under any circumstances.
Correction: The author clarifies that a thing can be possible in itself (by its essence) and necessary due to an external cause (like divine knowledge and power). The two modalities apply to different aspects.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge does not compromise creaturely contingency because possibility relates to essence while necessity comes from an external cause.

The Third Proof: Divine Knowledge and Power Necessitate
Third proof analyzing knowledge and power for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God's power is eternal and related to a specific effect, that effect must occur necessarily, not by free choice.

Core Idea
Eternal power with an eternal relation to an effect necessitates that effect.

This lesson presents the third and perhaps most technical proof against free choice. It focuses on the nature of divine knowledge and power, examining every possible scenario. The argument systematically shows that whether knowledge is eternal or originated, and whether power is eternal or originated, the conclusion is the same: the effect cannot be contingent on free choice but must be necessary. Pay close attention to the inference about eternal relation (ta'alluq).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a division of knowledge, then a division of power.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Follow the knowledge branch
Identify the two possibilities for knowledge: eternal or originated. For each, the author draws conclusions about the influencer (mu'aththir). Note how each branch leads away from free choice.
3
Analyze the power branch
When knowledge is originated, the focus shifts to power. The author considers whether power is originated (leading to regress) or eternal. The eternal case is key: eternal power has an eternal relation to the effect, making the effect necessary.
This step is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the influence of God's power is necessary, not contingent, thus God would not be a free-choosing agent on the opposing interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : العلم إما أن يكون قديماً ، وإما أن يكون حادثاً . فإن كان قديماً فإما أن يكون واجباً لذاته، أو ممكناً لذاته . فإن كان واجباً لذاته لزم استغناؤه من المؤثر . وذلك يقتضي نفي القادر والموجب معاً ، وإن كان ممكناً لذاته ، فلا بد له من مؤثر ، وذلك المؤثر يمتنع أن يكون قادراً، وإلا لزم إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل ، وهو محال . ولما بطل أن يكون ذلك المؤثر قادراً، يثبت أنه يجب أن يكون موجباً . وإما إن كان العلم حادثاً، فالمؤثر إن كان موجباً فهو المطلوب وإن كان قادراً . فنقول : تلك القدرة، إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى قدرة أخرى. ولزم التسلسل . وإن كانت قديمة كانت تلك القدرة من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت الذي يحدث فيه ذلك الأثر متعلقة باقتضاء وجود ذلك الأثر في ذلك الوقت وباقتضاء حدوثه فيه . واتفق المتكلمون على أن كل ما ثبت قدمه، فإنه يمتنع عدمه . فعلى هذا ذلك التعلق المستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد، أعني تعلق قدرة الله تعالى باقتضاء حدوث ذلك الحادث المعين في ذلك الوقت، أمر أزلي . والأزل يمتنع زواله، فوجب أن نحكم بأن ذلك المتعلق ممتنع الزوال، فيكون هذا قولاً بأن تأثير قدرة الله في حدوث ذلك الحادث المعين تأثير واقع على سبيل الوجوب، لا على سبيل الجواز . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يكون قادراً مختاراً . على التفسير الذي ذكرتموه .
The third proof: Knowledge is either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then it is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If it is necessary in itself, it follows that it is independent of an influencer. That entails negating both the qadir and the mujib. If it is possible in itself, it must have an influencer, and that influencer cannot be a qadir, otherwise that would necessitate bringing about the existing and obtaining the obtained, which is impossible. Since it is impossible that the influencer be a qadir, it is established that it must be a mujib. And if knowledge is originated, then the influencer, if it is a mujib, that is what is sought; if it is a qadir, we say: that power, if originated, would need another power, leading to infinite regress. If it is eternal, then that power from pre-existence until the time in which the effect occurs is related to requiring the existence of that effect at that time and requiring its occurrence therein. And the mutakallimun agree that whatever is established as eternal is impossible of non-existence. So on this basis, that continuous relation from pre-eternity to eternity — I mean the relation of God’s power to requiring the occurrence of that specific originated thing at that time — is an eternal matter. Pre-eternity cannot cease, so we must judge that that relation is impossible of cessation. This means that the influence of God’s power in causing that specific originated thing is an influence occurring necessarily, not contingently. On this estimation, He would not be a free-choosing qadir, according to the interpretation you have mentioned.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. It systematically examines the possible states of divine knowledge and power. First, it divides knowledge into eternal or originated. Each branch leads to a conclusion that either negates free choice or entails necessity. The key move is the analysis of eternal power: its relation (ta'alluq) to the effect is eternal, and since the eternal cannot cease, the effect is necessitated. Thus the qadir is not free.

العلم
القديم
الحادث
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
المؤثر
القادر
الموجب
التسلسل
التعلق
الاقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if power is eternal, it can still choose freely; the author argues that an eternal relation necessitates the effect.
Correction: The author shows that an eternal relation (ta'alluq) to a specific effect is itself eternal, and since the eternal cannot cease, the effect is necessitated, eliminating free choice.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if an eternal power is related to a specific originated effect from pre-eternity?
A) The power can choose to create the effect or not.
B) The effect must occur necessarily.
C) The power must be originated to allow free choice.
D) The relation is contingent on divine will.

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that an eternal power with an eternal relation to an effect necessitates that effect, thereby refuting free choice within the opponents' framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this argument challenge or confirm your belief, and what practical difference does it make in how you approach your daily decisions?
?
The author argues that if the influencer is a qadir with originated power, it leads to infinite regress. In your own life, when you make a choice, do you ever trace the causes backwards? What does this tell you about the need for a First Cause that is not free in the human sense? How does this affect your awe of God?
?
The passage concludes that God's power influences necessarily, not contingently. Sit with that statement for a moment. If you truly believed that every event is necessitated by God's eternal relation, would you worry less or more about your future? What would change about your trust in Him?

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility of Sihha (Validity) in Free Choice
The author presents the fourth proof refuting valid free choice by temporal analysis of sihha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that refutes the notion of valid free choice by analyzing the temporal impossibility of sihha.

Core Idea
Validity before the effect is impossible because it requires the future to be present in the past.

This passage presents the fourth proof against the claim that a free agent's act can be described as 'valid' (sihha). The author examines what 'validity before the effect' could mean, and shows both possible interpretations lead to contradiction. This is a key argument in the debate on divine determinism and free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the logical flow.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'sihha' and 'qadir'.
2
Understand the initial division
The author divides the possible times of validity: before, during, or after the effect. All three are claimed invalid, with focus on 'before'.
This is a standard kalam technique of exhaustive division.
3
Examine the two interpretations of 'before'
Notice the two interpretations of 'valid before the effect': either the agent can produce the effect in a later time from an earlier time, or it means that when the later time comes the agent will be able to produce it.
The first interpretation is more straightforward; the second is a subtle attempt to salvage validity.
4
See why both are impossible
The first requires the later time to be present in the earlier time, which is impossible. The second only gives power at the later time, so it doesn't establish pre-existent validity.
Thus the claim of sihha collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو حكمنا على القادر بأنه يصدر منه الأثر على سبيل الصحة، لكانت تلك الصحة إما أن تحصل قبل حصول الأثر، أو حال حصوله، أو بعد حصوله، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . فالقول بالصحة باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن تحصل تلك الصحة قبل حصول الأثر . فذلك لأنه لو صح منه التأثير في ذلك الأثر قبل حصول الأثر . فإما أن يكون معناه أنه في الوقت الأول يصح منه أن يوجد ذلك الأثر في الوقت الثاني، أو يكون معناه أنه جاء الوقت الثاني فعند مجيء الوقت الثاني يصح منه إيجاد ذلك الأثر في ذلك الوقت . أما القسم الأول فباطل، لأن إيجاد الشيء في الوقت الثاني مشروط بحضور الوقت الثاني، لكن حضور الوقت الثاني في الوقت الأول محال . والموقوف على المحال محال، فوجب أن يكون حدوث الشيء في الوقت الثاني عند حضور الوقت الأول محال، والمحال لا قدرة عليه، فيثبت أن من المحال أن يقدر في الوقت الأول على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : معنى أنه يصح منه الفعل في الوقت الأول أنه محكوم عليه في الوقت الأول، فإنه إذا انقضى هذا الوقت، وحضر الوقت الثاني فإنه عند حضور الوقت الثاني، يقدر على إيجاد الفعل فيه، إلا أنا نقول : قدرته على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني، إنما تحصل عند حضور الوقت الثاني . فإنه لا يكون قادراً مختاراً . على التفسير الذي ذكرتموه .
The fourth proof: We say: If we judge of the qadir that the effect proceeds from him on the basis of validity (sihha), then that validity must occur either before the occurrence of the effect, or at the time of its occurrence, or after its occurrence, and all three divisions are invalid. So the claim of validity is invalid. As for the division, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible for that validity to occur before the occurrence of the effect: that is because if it were valid for him to influence that effect before its occurrence, then either its meaning is that in the first time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in the second time, or its meaning is that when the second time comes, then at the coming of the second time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence at that time. As for the first division, it is invalid, because bringing a thing into existence in the second time is conditioned by the presence of the second time, but the presence of the second time in the first time is impossible. What is dependent on the impossible is impossible, so it necessarily follows that the occurrence of the thing in the second time at the presence of the first time is impossible, and the impossible is not within power, so it is established that it is impossible for him to have power in the first time to bring about the act in the second time. As for the second division, which is that one says: the meaning that the act is valid from him in the first time is that it is predicated of him in the first time that when this time passes and the second time comes, then at the coming of the second time he is able to bring the act into existence therein — except that we say: his power to bring the act into existence in the second time only arises at the presence of the second time. Thus he would not be a free-choosing qadir, according to the interpretation you have mentioned.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage sets forth the fourth proof. It begins by positing a qadir who acts 'ala sabili al-sihhah (on the basis of validity). The validity is analyzed temporally: it must be before, during, or after the effect. The author then shows that validity before the effect reduces to two possibilities, both of which are absurd. The first is that in time1 the agent can produce the effect in time2, but that requires time2 to be present in time1, which is impossible. The second is that in time1 it is predicated of the agent that when time2 arrives he will be able to produce it, but the power to produce in time2 only arises at time2, not before. Thus the notion of sihha fails, and the qadir cannot be free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine power allows bringing about an effect in the future from eternity, i.e., that power in the present can produce an effect in the future without temporal dependency.
Correction: The author argues that producing an effect in a specific time is conditioned by the presence of that time; the power to produce the effect only arises when the time comes.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, why is the first interpretation of 'validity before the effect' invalid?
A) Because it implies the effect exists before its time.
B) Because it requires the presence of the second time in the first time, which is impossible.
C) Because the agent's power cannot be eternal.
D) Because the effect must be simultaneous with the power.

Key Takeaway
The notion of valid free choice is impossible because any temporal analysis of validity leads to contradiction.

The Same Objection Applies to Will
The author states the identical application of an objection to will as to power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the objection against the attribute of power applies equally to the attribute of will, and why this is significant in the discussion of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The same objection that challenges power also challenges will—both stand or fall together.

In the previous discussion, an objection was raised against the attribute of power (qudra). The author now makes a concise but crucial point: this same objection applies identically to the attribute of will (irada). This brief passage reinforces that the argument is not isolated but extends to another core attribute, and that no separate defense for will is possible if the objection stands.

Guided Path
1
Recall the objection against power
Briefly review the objection raised earlier regarding the attribute of power. This sets the context for the current passage.
The exact nature of the objection is not repeated here, so you may need to refer back to the previous lesson.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the phrasing: 'as it applies to power, it applies exactly to will.'
The word 'بعينه' emphasizes identical application.
3
Understand the parallel
The author states that the objection applies exactly to will. This means that whatever argument was made against power holds for will without modification.
There is no new objection or separate treatment for will.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author makes this point. It shows that the critique is systematic and not limited to one attribute. If the objection is valid, it undermines both power and will.
This passage serves as a concluding remark, tying the discussion together.

The Objection Applies Equally to Will
Showing that the objection to power applies equally to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophical objection raised against divine power equally applies to divine will, and why this poses a problem for the opponent's position.

Core Idea
The same dilemma that challenges power also entraps will.

In this passage, the author concludes an argument about divine power, showing that if God's power were necessitating, then God would not be a powerful chooser. More importantly, he immediately points out that the same objection applies to divine will, preventing the opponent from escaping the dilemma by appealing to will. This brief but crucial move shifts the burden back to the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that the passage starts with a consequence and then mentions 'al-irāda'.
2
Understand the consequence of necessitation
The passage states that if God's power necessarily brings about its effect, then God would not be a powerful chooser. Reflect on why this follows.
3
Recognize the transfer of the objection to will
The author explicitly says the same question applies to will. Consider how this extends the argument.
4
Connect to the broader theological debate
This passage shows a key move in kalām: an objection against one attribute cannot be avoided by simply appealing to another attribute.

زواله ، فوجب أن نحكم بأن ذلك المتعلق ممتنع الزوال ، فيكون هذا قولاً بأن تأثير قدرة الله في حدوث ذلك الحادث المعين تأثير واقع على سبيل الوجوب لا على سبيل الجواز . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يكون قادراً مختاراً على التفسير الذي ذكرتموه . الإرادة . واعلم أن هذا السؤال ، كما أنه وارد في القدرة فهو بعينه وارد في الإرادة .
Its removal, so it is necessary that we judge that that object is impossible of removal, so this is a saying that the effect of God's power in the occurrence of that specific event is an effect occurring by way of necessity, not by way of possibility. And on this estimation, He would not be a powerful chooser (qādiran mukhtāran) according to the interpretation you mentioned. The will. And know that this question, just as it applies to power, it applies identically to will.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage concludes a preceding argument about divine power and then transitions to the topic of will. It first states a consequence: if God's power necessarily brings about its effect (i.e., on the basis of necessity rather than possibility), then God would not be a free agent (qādir mukhtār) in the sense the opponents understand. The author then explicitly notes that the same objection that was raised against power is equally valid against divine will (irāda). The phrase 'al-irāda' stands alone as a heading indicating the shift in discussion. The argument is that if power requires a preponderator to avoid necessitation, then will, which is supposed to be the preponderator, faces the same dilemma: it would either be necessary or need another preponderator, leading to regress.

The objection that power would necessitate its effect also applies to will, so appealing to will does not solve the problem.

The Fourth Proof: The Threefold Division and the First Case of Validity Before the Act
Sanusi refutes the first interpretation of validity before the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first case of validity before the act leads to a logical impossibility due to the condition of temporal presence.

Core Idea
Validity before the act cannot mean power over a future act because the future is not yet present.

The fourth proof of the text tackles the concept of validity (siha) in divine action. The author first breaks down the possible temporal locations of validity: before, during, or after the act. He then shows each leads to contradiction. In this lesson, we examine the first sub-case of validity before the act, which involves the impossibility of exercising power over an act in a time that has not yet arrived.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the fourth proof
Read the Arabic passage from 'الحجة الرابعة' to the end of the first sub-case. Note the threefold division and the initial refutation.
The text uses a conditional syllogism.
2
Identify the three divisions
Locate where the author states that validity must occur before, during, or after the effect. Understand this as the logical framework.
3
Focus on the first sub-case of 'before'
The author argues that if validity before the act means power to bring the act into existence in a second time, this is impossible because the second time is not present in the first time.
Think about why dependence on an impossible condition makes the act itself impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion of this sub-case
Since the act would be impossible at the first time, the powerful agent cannot be said to have power over it at that moment.
This establishes that validity before the act in this sense is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو حكمنا على القادر بأنه يصدر منه الأثر على سبيل الصحة ، لكانت تلك الصحة إما أن تحصل قبل حصول الأثر ، أو حال حصوله أو بعد حصوله ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . فالقول بالصحة باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن تحصل تلك الصحة قبل حصول الأثر . فذلك لأنه لو صح منه التأثير في ذلك الأثر قبل حصول الأثر . فإما أن يكون معناه أنه في الوقت الأول يصح منه أن يوجد ذلك الأثر في الوقت الثاني ، أو يكون معناه أنه جاء الوقت الثاني فعند مجيء الوقت الثاني يصح منه إيجاد ذلك الأثر في ذلك الوقت . أما القسم الأول فباطل ، لأن إيجاد الشيء في الوقت الثاني مشروط بحضور الوقت الثاني ، لكن حضور الوقت الثاني في الوقت الأول محال . والموقوف على المحال محال ، فوجب أن يكون حدوث الشيء في الوقت الثاني عند حضور الوقت الأول محال ، والمحال لا قدرة عليه ، فيثبت أن من المحال أن يقدر في الوقت الأول على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : معنى أنه يصح منه الفعل في الوقت الأول أنه محكوم عليه في الوقت الأول ، فإنه إذا انقضى هذا الوقت ، وحضر الوقت الثاني فإنه عند حضور الوقت الثاني ، يقدر على إيجاد الفعل فيه ، إلا أنا نقول : قدرته على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني ، إنما تحصل عند حضور الوقت الثاني ( وهذا يرجع حاصله إلى أن القدرة على الفعل إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل ) وهذا يقدح في قولنا : إن صحة الفعل إنما تحصل حال حضور الفعل . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن حال وجود الفعل يكون عدمه محالاً ، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال ، وما كان محالاً لعينه ولذاته امتنع كون القادر قادراً عليه . فيثبت أن الجمع بين صحة أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، وبين كونه موجوداً جمع بين النقيضين . وذلك محال . فيثبت أن حصول هذه الصحة حال حصول الفعل محال . وأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل بعد وجود الفعل ، فهذا قول معلوم البطلان بالضرورة والبديهة ، ولم يقل به أحد العقلاء . فثبت أن القادر بمعنى كونه محال يصح منه أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، لو حصل لحصلت هذه الصحة . إما قبل وجود الفعل ، وإما مع وجوده وأما بعده وثبت أن الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فكان القول بحصول هذه الصحة باطلاً
The fourth proof: that we say: if we judge of the powerful (al-qādir) that the effect (al-athar) issues from him on the basis of validity (ṣiḥḥa), then that validity would either occur before the occurrence of the effect, or during its occurrence, or after its occurrence, and the three divisions are false. So the statement of validity is false. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible that that validity occurs before the occurrence of the effect: that is because if it were valid for him to effect that effect before the occurrence of the effect, then either its meaning is that in the first time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in the second time, or its meaning is that the second time has come, so at the coming of the second time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in that time. As for the first division, it is false, because bringing something into existence in the second time is conditioned by the presence of the second time, but the presence of the second time in the first time is impossible. And what depends on the impossible is impossible, so it is necessary that the occurrence of the thing in the second time at the presence of the first time is impossible, and the impossible cannot be subject to power, so it is established that it is impossible for him to have power in the first time to bring the act into existence in the second time. And as for the second division [the first interpretation of the before-act case]: and it is that it is said: the meaning of 'it is valid for him to do the act in the first time' is that it is judged of him in the first time that when this time elapses and the second time arrives, then at the presence of the second time, he is able to bring the act into existence in it, except that we say: his power to bring the act into existence in the second time only occurs at the presence of the second time (and this amounts to the result that power over the act only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence), and this contradicts our saying that the validity of the act only occurs at the moment of the act's presence. And as for the second division [the case of validity during the act]: and it is that this validity only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence, then we say: this is also impossible. That is because at the moment of the act's existence, its non-existence is impossible, because the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible, and what is impossible in itself and by its essence prevents the powerful from being powerful over it. So it is established that the conjunction between the validity that it exists and that it does not exist, and its being existent, is a conjunction of contradictories. And that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of this validity at the moment of the act's occurrence is impossible. And as for the third division: and it is that this validity only occurs after the existence of the act, then this is a statement known to be false by necessity and intuition, and no rational person has said it. So it is established that the powerful in the sense of 'it is possible for him to exist and not exist' – if it occurred, this validity would occur: either before the existence of the act, or with its existence, or after it. And it is established that the three divisions are false, so the statement of the occurrence of this validity is false.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) that refutes the notion that divine action occurs 'on the basis of validity' (ṣiḥḥa), i.e., possibility of both existence and non-existence. The author argues that if we attribute validity to a powerful agent's action, that validity must occur in one of three moments: before the act, during the act, or after the act. Each is shown to be impossible. First, validity before the act: this can only mean either (a) the agent is valid to produce the act later, or (b) the validity is that he will be able at the later time. (a) fails because later time's presence is impossible now, so the supposed power is over an impossible thing. (b) reduces to power only at the time of the act, contradicting the claim that validity exists beforehand. Second, validity during the act: if validity exists simultaneously with the act, then the act's non-existence would be impossible (since it exists), but validity implies possibility of non-existence; thus a contradiction. Third, validity after the act: obviously false and no one asserts it. Since all three divisions are false, the concept of validity in God's action is false. The conclusion: the only coherent view is that divine power produces its effect necessarily, which then requires a volitional agent that is not 'free' in the sense of mere possibility.

قادر
أثر
محال
قدرة
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that validity before the act simply means God's power is eternal and can bring about any future event.
Correction: The passage shows that if validity before the act means power over the act at that earlier moment, it fails because the act's time is not present. This refutes a naive understanding of eternal power.

Checkpoint
Why does the first interpretation of validity before the act lead to impossibility?
A) Because the act cannot exist in two times.
B) Because the second time is not present in the first time, making the act impossible then.
C) Because God cannot change His mind.
D) Because the act is not yet created.

Key Takeaway
Validity before the act, if it means power over a future act from the present, is impossible because the future time is not yet present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power over your future, do you imagine it as a potential waiting to be activated, or as fully actual now? How does this lesson's argument that power over a future act from the present is impossible change your perspective?
?
Is there a hope or worry about tomorrow that you are treating as if God can only handle it when the time comes? What would it mean to believe that He is already fully able to act, not just in the future but at this very moment?
?
If God's power is not bound by the flow of time, then your next moment is already directly in His hands. How does that reality confront the way you plan, worry, or trust? Will you live today with that certainty?

The Fourth Proof: Refuting Validity Before the Act (Second Case) and During the Act
Sanusi refutes the second interpretation of validity before the act and the case of validity during the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of validity before the act (reduction to power at the moment of the act) and the paradox of validity during the act.

Core Idea
Validity before the act cannot mean just a future ability, as it reduces to power only at the act's moment; validity during the act leads to contradiction.

Having refuted the first interpretation of validity before the act, the author now examines the second interpretation: that validity before the act means the agent will be able at the future time. This, he shows, merely pushes the validity to the moment of the act, contradicting its alleged occurrence beforehand. Then he tackles the possibility of validity during the act, which leads to a logical contradiction between the act's existence and the possibility of its non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second sub-case of before-act
Read from 'وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال' to the end of the during-act case. Identify the two distinct arguments.
The second sub-case is a different interpretation of 'before'.
2
Analyze the second interpretation
This interpretation says that validity before act means it is judged that when the second time comes, he will be able. But this means his power only exists at the second time, so validity does not actually exist before the act.
The author says this contradicts the claim that validity occurs before the act.
3
Understand the during-act case
If validity occurs simultaneously with the act, then at that moment the act exists. But validity implies the act could also not exist. These are contradictory.
The union of existence and non-existence is impossible.
4
Note the conclusion for these two cases
Both interpretations of before-act and during-act are shown to be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو حكمنا على القادر بأنه يصدر منه الأثر على سبيل الصحة ، لكانت تلك الصحة إما أن تحصل قبل حصول الأثر ، أو حال حصوله أو بعد حصوله ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . فالقول بالصحة باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن تحصل تلك الصحة قبل حصول الأثر . فذلك لأنه لو صح منه التأثير في ذلك الأثر قبل حصول الأثر . فإما أن يكون معناه أنه في الوقت الأول يصح منه أن يوجد ذلك الأثر في الوقت الثاني ، أو يكون معناه أنه جاء الوقت الثاني فعند مجيء الوقت الثاني يصح منه إيجاد ذلك الأثر في ذلك الوقت . أما القسم الأول فباطل ، لأن إيجاد الشيء في الوقت الثاني مشروط بحضور الوقت الثاني ، لكن حضور الوقت الثاني في الوقت الأول محال . والموقوف على المحال محال ، فوجب أن يكون حدوث الشيء في الوقت الثاني عند حضور الوقت الأول محال ، والمحال لا قدرة عليه ، فيثبت أن من المحال أن يقدر في الوقت الأول على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : معنى أنه يصح منه الفعل في الوقت الأول أنه محكوم عليه في الوقت الأول ، فإنه إذا انقضى هذا الوقت ، وحضر الوقت الثاني فإنه عند حضور الوقت الثاني ، يقدر على إيجاد الفعل فيه ، إلا أنا نقول : قدرته على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني ، إنما تحصل عند حضور الوقت الثاني ( وهذا يرجع حاصله إلى أن القدرة على الفعل إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل ) وهذا يقدح في قولنا : إن صحة الفعل إنما تحصل حال حضور الفعل . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن حال وجود الفعل يكون عدمه محالاً ، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال ، وما كان محالاً لعينه ولذاته امتنع كون القادر قادراً عليه . فيثبت أن الجمع بين صحة أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، وبين كونه موجوداً جمع بين النقيضين . وذلك محال . فيثبت أن حصول هذه الصحة حال حصول الفعل محال . وأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل بعد وجود الفعل ، فهذا قول معلوم البطلان بالضرورة والبديهة ، ولم يقل به أحد العقلاء . فثبت أن القادر بمعنى كونه محال يصح منه أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، لو حصل لحصلت هذه الصحة . إما قبل وجود الفعل ، وإما مع وجوده وأما بعده وثبت أن الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فكان القول بحصول هذه الصحة باطلاً
The fourth proof: that we say: if we judge of the powerful (al-qādir) that the effect (al-athar) issues from him on the basis of validity (ṣiḥḥa), then that validity would either occur before the occurrence of the effect, or during its occurrence, or after its occurrence, and the three divisions are false. So the statement of validity is false. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible that that validity occurs before the occurrence of the effect: that is because if it were valid for him to effect that effect before the occurrence of the effect, then either its meaning is that in the first time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in the second time, or its meaning is that the second time has come, so at the coming of the second time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in that time. As for the first division, it is false, because bringing something into existence in the second time is conditioned by the presence of the second time, but the presence of the second time in the first time is impossible. And what depends on the impossible is impossible, so it is necessary that the occurrence of the thing in the second time at the presence of the first time is impossible, and the impossible cannot be subject to power, so it is established that it is impossible for him to have power in the first time to bring the act into existence in the second time. And as for the second division [the first interpretation of the before-act case]: and it is that it is said: the meaning of 'it is valid for him to do the act in the first time' is that it is judged of him in the first time that when this time elapses and the second time arrives, then at the presence of the second time, he is able to bring the act into existence in it, except that we say: his power to bring the act into existence in the second time only occurs at the presence of the second time (and this amounts to the result that power over the act only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence), and this contradicts our saying that the validity of the act only occurs at the moment of the act's presence. And as for the second division [the case of validity during the act]: and it is that this validity only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence, then we say: this is also impossible. That is because at the moment of the act's existence, its non-existence is impossible, because the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible, and what is impossible in itself and by its essence prevents the powerful from being powerful over it. So it is established that the conjunction between the validity that it exists and that it does not exist, and its being existent, is a conjunction of contradictories. And that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of this validity at the moment of the act's occurrence is impossible. And as for the third division: and it is that this validity only occurs after the existence of the act, then this is a statement known to be false by necessity and intuition, and no rational person has said it. So it is established that the powerful in the sense of 'it is possible for him to exist and not exist' – if it occurred, this validity would occur: either before the existence of the act, or with its existence, or after it. And it is established that the three divisions are false, so the statement of the occurrence of this validity is false.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) that refutes the notion that divine action occurs 'on the basis of validity' (ṣiḥḥa), i.e., possibility of both existence and non-existence. The author argues that if we attribute validity to a powerful agent's action, that validity must occur in one of three moments: before the act, during the act, or after the act. Each is shown to be impossible. First, validity before the act: this can only mean either (a) the agent is valid to produce the act later, or (b) the validity is that he will be able at the later time. (a) fails because later time's presence is impossible now, so the supposed power is over an impossible thing. (b) reduces to power only at the time of the act, contradicting the claim that validity exists beforehand. Second, validity during the act: if validity exists simultaneously with the act, then the act's non-existence would be impossible (since it exists), but validity implies possibility of non-existence; thus a contradiction. Third, validity after the act: obviously false and no one asserts it. Since all three divisions are false, the concept of validity in God's action is false. The conclusion: the only coherent view is that divine power produces its effect necessarily, which then requires a volitional agent that is not 'free' in the sense of mere possibility.

Key Terms
صحة
قادر
قدرة
إيجاد
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that validity during the act means the act could have been otherwise, which seems plausible.
Correction: But if the act exists, its non-existence is impossible at that moment, so the 'could have been otherwise' does not apply to the existing act; it applies only before it exists.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is validity during the act impossible?
A) Because the act cannot be valid after it occurs.
B) Because existence and non-existence cannot be simultaneously possible.
C) Because power is only before the act.
D) Because the act is not created yet.

Key Takeaway
Validity during the act is impossible because it would require both existence and non-existence simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that God's power cannot be constrained by time, does your heart truly accept that He is all-powerful over every moment, or do you sometimes imagine His power as limited like human ability? What would change in your reliance on Him if you fully internalized this?
?
The passage shows that assuming validity during the act leads to logical impossibility. Are there areas in your faith where you have allowed a contradiction to linger without resolving it? Which belief needs closer examination to be consistent with tawhid?
?
If the very concept of God's power requires us to think carefully about time and possibility, how much more should we guard our tongues and hearts when speaking about His attributes? Will you commit to studying 'ilm al-kalam more deeply so that you don't fall into invalid conceptions of Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Refuting Validity After the Act and Conclusion
Sanusi refutes the third division (after-act) and concludes the fourth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why validity after the act is patently false and how the entire proof establishes that divine action cannot be described as 'valid' in the sense of mere possibility.

Core Idea
Validity after the act is universally rejected; thus all three divisions are false, proving that divine action is necessary, not contingent.

The third division—that validity occurs after the act—is dismissed as obviously false and never asserted by any rational person. With all three divisions refuted, the author concludes that the concept of validity (siha) in divine action is false. This has profound implications for how we understand God's power and action: God's act is not 'one among possibilities' but is determined by His wisdom and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the after-act case and conclusion
Read from 'وأما القسم الثالث' to the end of the passage. Note how brief the refutation of the third division is.
2
Understand why after-act is impossible
Validity after the act would mean the act could be valid or not after it has already occurred, which is nonsensical. The text says it is known by necessity to be false.
3
Review the overall conclusion
The author restates that all three temporal locations are impossible, so the claim that divine action occurs with validity is false.
This means that divine power must produce its effect in a necessary manner, which then requires an additional factor like volition to explain choice.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof impacts the understanding of God's freedom. If validity (contingency) is impossible in divine action, then God's act is not 'free' in the sense of being equally possible to do or not do; it follows from His nature or will necessarily.

الحجة الرابعة : أن نقول : لو حكمنا على القادر بأنه يصدر منه الأثر على سبيل الصحة ، لكانت تلك الصحة إما أن تحصل قبل حصول الأثر ، أو حال حصوله أو بعد حصوله ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . فالقول بالصحة باطل . أما الحصر فظاهر . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن تحصل تلك الصحة قبل حصول الأثر . فذلك لأنه لو صح منه التأثير في ذلك الأثر قبل حصول الأثر . فإما أن يكون معناه أنه في الوقت الأول يصح منه أن يوجد ذلك الأثر في الوقت الثاني ، أو يكون معناه أنه جاء الوقت الثاني فعند مجيء الوقت الثاني يصح منه إيجاد ذلك الأثر في ذلك الوقت . أما القسم الأول فباطل ، لأن إيجاد الشيء في الوقت الثاني مشروط بحضور الوقت الثاني ، لكن حضور الوقت الثاني في الوقت الأول محال . والموقوف على المحال محال ، فوجب أن يكون حدوث الشيء في الوقت الثاني عند حضور الوقت الأول محال ، والمحال لا قدرة عليه ، فيثبت أن من المحال أن يقدر في الوقت الأول على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن يقال : معنى أنه يصح منه الفعل في الوقت الأول أنه محكوم عليه في الوقت الأول ، فإنه إذا انقضى هذا الوقت ، وحضر الوقت الثاني فإنه عند حضور الوقت الثاني ، يقدر على إيجاد الفعل فيه ، إلا أنا نقول : قدرته على إيجاد الفعل في الوقت الثاني ، إنما تحصل عند حضور الوقت الثاني ( وهذا يرجع حاصله إلى أن القدرة على الفعل إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل ) وهذا يقدح في قولنا : إن صحة الفعل إنما تحصل حال حضور الفعل . وأما القسم الثاني . وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل حال حصول الفعل فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن حال وجود الفعل يكون عدمه محالاً ، لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال ، وما كان محالاً لعينه ولذاته امتنع كون القادر قادراً عليه . فيثبت أن الجمع بين صحة أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، وبين كونه موجوداً جمع بين النقيضين . وذلك محال . فيثبت أن حصول هذه الصحة حال حصول الفعل محال . وأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن هذه الصحة إنما تحصل بعد وجود الفعل ، فهذا قول معلوم البطلان بالضرورة والبديهة ، ولم يقل به أحد العقلاء . فثبت أن القادر بمعنى كونه محال يصح منه أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، لو حصل لحصلت هذه الصحة . إما قبل وجود الفعل ، وإما مع وجوده وأما بعده وثبت أن الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فكان القول بحصول هذه الصحة باطلاً
The fourth proof: that we say: if we judge of the powerful (al-qādir) that the effect (al-athar) issues from him on the basis of validity (ṣiḥḥa), then that validity would either occur before the occurrence of the effect, or during its occurrence, or after its occurrence, and the three divisions are false. So the statement of validity is false. As for the enumeration, it is apparent. As for the demonstration that it is impossible that that validity occurs before the occurrence of the effect: that is because if it were valid for him to effect that effect before the occurrence of the effect, then either its meaning is that in the first time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in the second time, or its meaning is that the second time has come, so at the coming of the second time it is valid for him to bring that effect into existence in that time. As for the first division, it is false, because bringing something into existence in the second time is conditioned by the presence of the second time, but the presence of the second time in the first time is impossible. And what depends on the impossible is impossible, so it is necessary that the occurrence of the thing in the second time at the presence of the first time is impossible, and the impossible cannot be subject to power, so it is established that it is impossible for him to have power in the first time to bring the act into existence in the second time. And as for the second division [the first interpretation of the before-act case]: and it is that it is said: the meaning of 'it is valid for him to do the act in the first time' is that it is judged of him in the first time that when this time elapses and the second time arrives, then at the presence of the second time, he is able to bring the act into existence in it, except that we say: his power to bring the act into existence in the second time only occurs at the presence of the second time (and this amounts to the result that power over the act only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence), and this contradicts our saying that the validity of the act only occurs at the moment of the act's presence. And as for the second division [the case of validity during the act]: and it is that this validity only occurs at the moment of the act's occurrence, then we say: this is also impossible. That is because at the moment of the act's existence, its non-existence is impossible, because the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible, and what is impossible in itself and by its essence prevents the powerful from being powerful over it. So it is established that the conjunction between the validity that it exists and that it does not exist, and its being existent, is a conjunction of contradictories. And that is impossible. So it is established that the occurrence of this validity at the moment of the act's occurrence is impossible. And as for the third division: and it is that this validity only occurs after the existence of the act, then this is a statement known to be false by necessity and intuition, and no rational person has said it. So it is established that the powerful in the sense of 'it is possible for him to exist and not exist' – if it occurred, this validity would occur: either before the existence of the act, or with its existence, or after it. And it is established that the three divisions are false, so the statement of the occurrence of this validity is false.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) that refutes the notion that divine action occurs 'on the basis of validity' (ṣiḥḥa), i.e., possibility of both existence and non-existence. The author argues that if we attribute validity to a powerful agent's action, that validity must occur in one of three moments: before the act, during the act, or after the act. Each is shown to be impossible. First, validity before the act: this can only mean either (a) the agent is valid to produce the act later, or (b) the validity is that he will be able at the later time. (a) fails because later time's presence is impossible now, so the supposed power is over an impossible thing. (b) reduces to power only at the time of the act, contradicting the claim that validity exists beforehand. Second, validity during the act: if validity exists simultaneously with the act, then the act's non-existence would be impossible (since it exists), but validity implies possibility of non-existence; thus a contradiction. Third, validity after the act: obviously false and no one asserts it. Since all three divisions are false, the concept of validity in God's action is false. The conclusion: the only coherent view is that divine power produces its effect necessarily, which then requires a volitional agent that is not 'free' in the sense of mere possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine action is simply a free choice among equally possible alternatives, i.e., that God could have done otherwise in the same circumstances.
Correction: This proof shows that such 'validity' (contingency) leads to temporal contradictions. Instead, divine action is necessary given God's attributes and will, though the specific act may be contingent relative to external factors.

Checkpoint
Why is the third division (validity after the act) considered false?
A) Because it requires infinite time.
B) Because it is self-evidently impossible and no rational person has claimed it.
C) Because it contradicts God's omnipotence.
D) Because the act has already occurred.

Key Takeaway
All possible timings for validity in divine action are impossible, so the concept of validity is false; divine action is necessary, not contingent.

How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what must change in your du'a?

Reflect on a specific situation where you felt God was 'too late' or 'too early'. How does the necessity of divine action reframe your understanding of that event?
?
