The Sixth Proof: Power Cannot Be Over Non-Existence
Razi proves that power cannot relate to non-existence, using two rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that divine power cannot be directed towards non-existence, based on the nature of power as an effective attribute and the impossibility of forming the already formed.

Core Idea
Power is an effective attribute; non-existence is pure negation—they cannot be linked.

This passage presents Razi's sixth proof against the idea that God has power over abandonment (tark). Razi gives two compelling reasons why power can only relate to existence, not to non-existence. Understanding this argument deepens our grasp of the nature of divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence due to page cut-off.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage presents two reasons (وجهان) why original non-existence cannot be an object of power. Find each one and note how they differ.
The first reason deals with the nature of power and non-existence; the second deals with the persistence of non-existence.
3
Understand the first reason
Power is an effective attribute (صفة مؤثرة); non-existence is pure negation (نفي محض). Attaching pure negation to an effective attribute is contradictory.
4
Understand the second reason
If non-existence persists and is attributed to an effect, it would mean 'forming the formed' (تحصيل الحاصل), which is impossible. This reinforces that power cannot be over non-existence.
This reason uses the principle that an effect cannot be repeated after it already exists.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The two reasons together prove that power can only relate to existence. Reflect on why this conclusion is important for understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن كون القادر قادراً على الترك محال . لأن المراد ههنا بالترك بقاؤه على عدمه الأصلي. والعدم الأصلي محال أن يكون مقدوراً لوجهين : الأول : أن القدرة صفة مؤثرة ، والعدم نفي محض . فإسناد النفي المحض إلى القدرة التي هي صفة مؤثرة محال . والثاني : إن العدم حال بقائه ، لا يمكن إسناده إلى المؤثر، لأنا لو أسندنا الباقي إلى المؤثر ، لزم تكوين الكائن وتحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال . فيثبت بهذين الوجهين أن إسناد العلم المستمر إلى القادر : محال . وإذا ثبت هذا امتنع كون القادر قادراً على الترك . وإذا ثبت أن القادر لا تعلق له إلا بجانب الوجود ، وأما جانب العدم فلا تعلق له به ، وإذا كان القادر لا صلاحية له إلا في التأثير في جانب الوجود بعينه , فحينئذ لا
The sixth proof: That the powerful being is powerful over abandonment (tark) is impossible. Because what is meant here by abandonment is its remaining in its original non-existence. And the original non-existence being an object of power is impossible for two reasons: First: that power is an effective attribute, and non-existence is pure negation. So attributing pure negation to power, which is an effective attribute, is impossible. Second: that non-existence, while it persists, cannot be attributed to the effect, because if we attribute the persistent to the effect, it would entail the formation of the formed and the obtaining of the obtained, which is impossible. So by these two points it is established that the attribution of continuous non-existence to the powerful is impossible. And when this is established, it is impossible for the powerful to be powerful over abandonment. And when it is established that the powerful has no attachment except to the side of existence, and as for the side of non-existence, it has no attachment to it, and when the powerful has no suitability except for affecting the side of existence specifically, then it does not...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof, arguing that it is impossible for a powerful being (al-qadir) to have power over abandonment (tark), i.e., over refraining from action, understood as remaining in original non-existence. The author gives two reasons. First, power (qudra) is an effective attribute (sifa mu'aththira), while non-existence (adam) is pure negation (nafy mahd); attributing pure negation to an effective attribute is impossible because an effect requires positive reality. Second, if non-existence persists, it cannot be attributed to an effect because that would be like 'forming the already formed' (tahsil al-hasil) which is impossible. Therefore, power can only relate to existence, not to non-existence. This refutes the Ash'ari view that God is powerful over both action and its omission, or at least qualifies it.

Key Terms
قادر
ترك
عدم أصلي
قدرة
صفة مؤثرة
نفي محض
تكوين الكائن
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power includes the ability to not create (i.e., to leave things in non-existence).
Correction: Razi argues that non-existence itself has no positive reality and cannot be an object of power because power is an effective attribute that requires a positive effect.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi consider it impossible for power to be over non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is pure negation and power is an effective attribute.
B) Because non-existence does not exist in time.
C) Because power is only for what is possible.
D) Because God only creates good.

Key Takeaway
Divine power can only be directed towards existence, because power is effective and non-existence has no positive reality to receive effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God's power includes the ability to 'do nothing' or to leave things in non-existence? How does this passage challenge that assumption? What does it mean for your trust in God's actions that His power is only directed toward existence?
?
If non-existence is pure negation, what does that say about the reality of 'nothingness'? How does this understanding affect the way you perceive God's constant act of creation?
?
Razi argues that attributing persistent non-existence to power is absurd because it would lead to 'obtaining the obtained'. In what areas of your life do you mistakenly assume that God could leave something unrealized when He has already promised existence? How can this truth deepen your reliance on Him?

Refutation of the Claim That the Capable Can Refrain from Action
Sanusi refutes an objection about the difference between capable and incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intuitive difference between a capable agent and an incapable one does not prove an ability to refrain from action, and how the refutation relies on the distinction between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
The felt difference only shows ability to act when motive is present, not ability to refrain.

This passage addresses an objection that might arise after the previous discussion on necessitating causes. Some claim that we intuitively know that the capable can refrain from acting while the incapable cannot, and this necessary knowledge suffices to prove divine free will. Sanusi presents this objection and then refutes it by analyzing what the felt difference actually implies. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on understanding the flow of the objection and the refutation without analyzing details yet.
Notice the structure: objection (قال) followed by refutation (ولقائل أن يقول...).
2
Identify the objection and the weak answer
Locate where the objector presents the intuitive difference between the capable (القادر) and the incapable (العاجز). The objector claims this difference is known necessarily and proves the capable can refrain.
The weak answer is that we have no need to explain the difference because it is self-evident.
3
Understand the refutation's core argument
The refutation argues that when a motive (الداعي) to act occurs, the act necessarily follows from the combination of capacity (القدرة) and that specific motive. If the combination is absent, the act does not occur—but this does not imply an ability to refrain when the motive is present.
Key: The act is caused by capacity + motive; absence of either causes non-action, not a separate power of refraining.
4
Grasp the distinction between existence and non-existence
The refutation concludes that the felt difference between capable and incapable pertains only to the side of existence (جانب الوجود): the capable produces the act when motive is present, the incapable does not. It does not pertain to non-existence (جانب العدم), so it does not prove an ability to refrain.
This distinction is crucial: the difference is about whether the act occurs, not about whether one can choose to not act.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this refutation matters for divine attributes. It prevents a flawed argument for free will that would compromise God's necessitating causality.
This connects to the larger theme of causality and divine attributes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبقى بينه وبين الموجب فرق . وتمام تقرير هذا الكلام قد سبق في أول الباب الأول
وحاول بعضهم أن يجيب عن هذا الكلام . فقال : نحن نعلم بالضرورة
أن العاجز لا يجد من نفسه أنه إن شاء لا يفعل أمكنه أن لا يفعل . أما القادر
على الفعل فإنه يجد من نفسه أنه إذا شاء أن لا يفعل فإنه يمكنه أن لا يفعل
وهذا الفرق معلوم بالضرورة. ولما كان هذا الفرق معلوماً بالضرورة ، لم
يكن بنا حاجة إلى بيان كيفية هذا الفرق. ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الجواب ضعيف . وبيانه : أنا نجد من أنفسنا أنه إذا حصل الداعي إلى الفعل ، فإنه يترتب على مجموع القدرة مع تلك الداعية الخاصة المعينة حصول ذلك الفعل المعين وإذا لم يوجد ذلك المجموع . إما لأنه فقد الداعية ، أو لم يقدر بالقدرة فحينئذ يبقى ذلك العلم المستمر ، كما كان . والفرق الذي نجده من أنفسنا بين القادر وبين العاجز : عائد إلى جانب الوجود ، وأن القادر موصوف بصفة إذا حصلت له الداعية المعينة إلى الإيجاد، فإنه يحصل الفعل . والعاجز ليس كذلك . فهذه التفرقة حاصلة بين القادر وبين العاجز . إلا أن هذه التفرقة عائدة إلى جانب الوجود ، لا إلى جانب العدم. فيثبت : أن الفرق الذي يجده
كل عاقل من نفسه، لا يقتضي كون القادر ، قادراً على الترك .
There remains a difference between him and the necessitating cause. The complete establishment of this discussion has already preceded at the beginning of the first chapter. And some have attempted to answer this statement. They said: We know necessarily that the incapable does not find within himself that if he wills not to do, he could not do. As for the one capable of the act, he finds within himself that if he wills not to do, then it is possible for him not to do. This difference is known necessarily. Since this difference is known necessarily, we have no need to explain how this difference occurs. But it may be said: This answer is weak. Its explanation: We find within ourselves that when the incitement to the act occurs, then the occurrence of that particular act depends on the combined capacity and that specific incitement. And if that combination does not exist—either because the incitement is absent or because one does not possess the capacity—then that continuous knowledge remains as it was. The difference we find within ourselves between the capable and the incapable pertains to the side of existence, namely that the capable is described by a quality such that when the specific incitement to bring about the act occurs to him, the act occurs. The incapable is not like that. So this differentiation occurs between the capable and the incapable. But this differentiation pertains to the side of existence, not to the side of non-existence. Thus it is established that the difference which every rational person finds within himself does not entail that the capable is capable of refraining from the act.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection regarding the difference between a capable agent (al-qādir) and an incapable one (al-ʿājiz). The objector claims that we intuitively know that the incapable cannot avoid inaction when he wills it, while the capable can avoid action when he wills it, and this necessary knowledge suffices to prove that the capable can refrain. The author first states that one may answer this objection weakly, then proceeds to refute it. The refutation argues that when a motive (dāʿī) to act arises, the act necessarily follows from the combination of capacity (qudra) and that specific motive. If either is absent, the act does not occur, but no ability to refrain is implied. The difference between capable and incapable is only on the side of existence: the capable produces an act when the motive is present, while the incapable cannot. This difference does not entail an ability to refrain from action when the motive is present. Thus the objection fails.

الفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the intuitive difference between being able to act and not proves that the capable has free will to refrain from acting.
Correction: The refutation shows that this difference only indicates that the act occurs when motive and capacity combine, not that the capable can choose to refrain when the motive is present.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does the felt difference between the capable and the incapable pertain to?
A) The side of existence, i.e., whether the act occurs when the motive is present
B) The side of non-existence, i.e., whether one can refrain
C) Both existence and non-existence equally
D) The certainty of the motive

Key Takeaway
The intuitive difference between capable and incapable only concerns whether the act occurs when the motive is present, not an ability to refrain from action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you tell yourself 'I could have chosen otherwise'? How does this lesson expose that excuse as relying on a feeling that only points to the presence of motive, not real freedom? What does this mean for how you repent?
?
If your ability to act is merely a capacity that must be triggered by a motive from Allah, how does that change your sense of responsibility for your intentions? Are you truly the source of your good deeds, or a channel? What should you ask Allah for on the prayer mat?
?
This lesson denies that you have the power to refrain when the motive to act is present. Do you feel that your soul resists this? Why? Could this resistance be a form of pride in your own will? How can you humbly rely on Allah for both the motive and the capacity to obey?

First Religious Proof: God is Not a Necessary Cause
Sanusi presents the first religious proof that God is not a necessary cause by refuting both conditional and unconditional causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first religious argument that God is a free agent, not a necessary cause, by seeing how both conditional and unconditional causation are impossible.

Core Idea
God cannot be a necessary cause because both conditional and unconditional causation lead to absurdity.

Before examining Islamic proofs for God's free will, we consider an argument from the people of creeds: if God were a necessary cause, His causation would either be conditional or unconditional. Both options fail, so God must be a free agent. This passage lays out the first such proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up a conditional and then splits it into two divisions.
The passage is incomplete due to page break but the core argument is clear.
2
Understand the two divisions
Identify the two possibilities: unconditional causation (غير موقوف على شرط) and conditional causation (موقوف على شرط). The argument claims both are impossible.
3
Trace the refutations
For unconditional: it would require the effect to be simultaneous with God, leading to eternity of the world or temporality of God. For conditional: the condition is either eternal (leads to eternity of world) or temporal (leads to infinite regress). Both are false.
The regress argument is cut off but the conclusion is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أرباب الملل والأديان : فقد احتجوا على كونه تعالى قادراً مختاراً بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات لكان إيجابه لمعلولاته ، إما أن يكون غير موقوف على شرط ، وإما أن يكون موقوفاً على شرط . والقسمان باطلان .
فالقول بكونه موجباً بالذات باطل ، إذا قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الإيجاب غير موقوف على شرط . وذلك لأنه لو كان كذلك لزم أن يكون موجبه معه ، إذ لو حصل ذلك المعلول في بعض الأوقات دون البعض ، مع أنه لم يتميز ذلك الوقت عن سائر الأوقات بشرط ، وخاصية . فحينئذ يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن من غير مرجح وهو محال . ولو كان معلوله معه لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، أو من حدوث العالم حدوثه ، لكنا قد دللنا على أن الله تعالى قديم ، وأن العالم حادث ، فيثبت أنه يمتنع أن يقال : أن الله تعالى يوجب العالم من غير شرط . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الإيجاب مشروطاً بشرط . ذلك لأن الشرط إما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون حادثاً . فإن كان قديماً لزم من قدم المؤثر ، وقدم الشرط ، قدم العالم ، على ما قررناه ، وأنه باطل
وإن كان حادثاً كان الكلام في كيفية حدوثه كالكلام في كيفية حدوث الحادث الأول ، ويفضي هذا إلى التسلسل . وذلك التسلسل . إما أن ...
And as for the people of creeds and religions, they have argued for His exalted being being powerful and free by aspects: The first proof: That if He were a necessary cause in essence, then His causing of His effects would either not be conditional upon a condition, or it would be conditional upon a condition. Both divisions are false. For the statement that He is a necessary cause in essence is false, because we say: It is impossible for that causing to be unconditional. That is because if it were so, then His effect would have to be simultaneous with Him. For if that effect occurs at some times without others, while that time is not distinguished from other times by a condition or particularity, then preference of one side of the possible without a preponderator would follow, which is impossible. And if His effect were simultaneous with Him, then from the eternity of the effect the eternity of the world would follow, or from the temporality of the world His temporality would follow. But we have shown that God is eternal and the world is temporal. So it is established that it is impossible to say that God causes the world without a condition. And we said: It is impossible for that causing to be conditional upon a condition. That is because the condition is either eternal or temporal. If it is eternal, then from the eternity of the effective cause and the eternity of the condition, the eternity of the world follows, according to what we have established, and that is false. And if it is temporal, then the discussion concerning its coming to be is like the discussion concerning the coming to be of the first temporal thing, and this leads to infinite regress. That regress...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument from the people of creeds and religions (arbāb al-milal wa l-adyān) that God is powerful and free (qādir mukhtār), not a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt). The argument is a classification and refutation. It divides the hypothesis that God is a necessary cause into two possibilities: either His causation is unconditional (ghayr mawqūf ʿalā sharṭ) or conditional (mawqūf ʿalā sharṭ). Both are shown to be false. The first division is refuted: unconditional causation would require the effect to be simultaneous with God, but since effects appear at specific times, that would imply temporal distinction without a preponderator, which is impossible. Moreover, if the effect were eternal (like the world), that would imply the world is eternal, contradicting its temporality. The second division is refuted: if the condition is eternal, the effect would be eternal, again false; if temporal, it leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The argument is cut off at the mention of regress, but the refutation of both divisions is clear, establishing that God cannot be a necessary cause.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
شرط
معلول
قديم
حادث
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine necessary causation can be either conditional or unconditional, and one of these might be possible.
Correction: The argument shows both are impossible, thereby disproving necessary causation entirely.

Checkpoint
What follows if God's causation is unconditional, according to the first religious proof?
A) The effect must be simultaneous with God, leading to co-eternity of the world or temporality of God.
B) The effect must occur at a later time, proving God's freedom.
C) The condition must be eternal, leading to infinite regress.
D) The condition must be temporal, leading to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The first religious proof shows that God cannot be a necessary cause because both conditional and unconditional causation lead to impossible consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you sometimes expect God to answer automatically, as if He were a necessary cause? How does this argument challenge you to trust in His free will instead of treating Him as a mechanism?
?
Think of a recent difficulty in your life. Did you feel entitled to a specific outcome from God? How does the truth that God is not a necessary cause free you from demanding and open you to accepting His wisdom?
?
This proof uses logic to defend God's freedom. Are you willing to use your intellect to strengthen your faith, or do you avoid such arguments? What holds you back from engaging with proofs like this one?

First Proof: God is Free, Not a Necessary Agent
Mutakallimun prove God's free will by showing necessary agency leads to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's proof that God is a free agent (qādir mukhtār) by refuting the assumption that He is a necessary agent (mūjib bi-dhāt).

Core Idea
If God were a necessary agent, His causality would be either unconditional or conditional — both are impossible.

The mutakallimun argue that attributing necessary agency to God leads to contradictions. They first divide the possibilities of divine causation and then refute each one. This lesson will walk through their disjunctive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure with divisions (qismān).
The passage opens with 'if He were necessary by essence...'.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
The proof splits the possibility of necessary agency into two cases: unconditional or conditional causation. Understand why these are the only options.
Mutakallimun use exhaustive division.
3
Examine the unconditional case
Follow the argument that unconditional causation leads to either simultaneity of cause and effect or preponderance without a reason. Both are impossible given God's eternity and the world's temporality.
Consider how this refutes the first branch.
4
Examine the conditional case
The condition must be either eternal or temporal. If eternal, leads to pre-eternity of world; if temporal, leads to infinite regress. Both are refuted.
The regress arguments are assumed from earlier proofs.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since both branches lead to contradiction, the original assumption that God is a necessary agent is false. Therefore, God is a free agent.
This proof establishes a key divine attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أرباب الملل والأديان : فقد احتجوا على كونه تعالى قادراً مختاراً الحجة الأولى : أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات لكان إيجابه لمعلولاته، إما أن يكون غير موقوف على شرط، وإما أن يكون موقوفاً على شرط. والقسمان باطلان. فالقول بكونه موجباً بالذات باطل، إذا قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الإيجاب غير موقوف على شرط. وذلك لأنه لو كان كذلك لزم أن يكون موجبه معه، إذ لو حصل ذلك المعلول في بعض الأوقات دون البعض، مع أنه لم يتميز ذلك الوقت عن سائر الأوقات بشرط، وخاصية. فحينئذ يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن من غير مرجح وهو محال. ولو كان معلوله معه لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، أو من حدوث العالم حدوثه، لكنا قد دللنا على أن الله تعالى قديم، وأن العالم حادث، فيثبت أنه يمتنع أن يقال: أن الله تعالى يوجب العالم من غير شرط. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الإيجاب مشروطاً بشرط. ذلك لأن الشرط إما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون حادثاً. فإن كان قديماً لزم من قدم المؤثر، وقدم الشرط، قدم العالم، على ما قررناه، وأنه باطل. وإن كان حادثاً كان الكلام في كيفية حدوثه كالكلام في كيفية حدوث الحادث الأول، ويفضي هذا إلى التسلسل. وذلك التسلسل. إما أن يقع دفعة، أو يقع بحيث يكون كل واحد منهما مسبوقاً بغيره، لا إلى أول. والقسم الأول. وهو التسلسل الذي يقع دفعة قد أبطلناء في إثبات واجب الوجود. والقسم الثاني. وهو القول بوجود حوادث يكون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، لا إلى أول، فهو قول بحوادث لا أول لها. وقد أبطلناه، فيثبت أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات، لكان إما أن يكون تأثيره في وجود المعلولات غير موقوف على شرط، وإما أن يكون موقوفاً على شرط. وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القطع بامتناع كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات.
As for the people of religions and faiths, they have argued for His being powerful and free. The first proof: That if He were necessary by essence, then His causation of His effects would either be not dependent on a condition, or dependent on a condition. Both divisions are invalid. So the statement that He is necessary by essence is invalid, because we say: It is impossible for that causation to be not dependent on a condition. That is because if it were so, then it would be necessary that its effect be with it, for if that effect occurred at some times without others, even though that time was not distinguished from other times by a condition and a property, then there would follow a preponderance of one side of the possible without a preponderator, which is impossible. And if its effect were with it, then from its pre-eternity the pre-eternity of the world would follow, or from the temporality of the world its temporality would follow. But we have established that God is pre-eternal and that the world is temporal, so it is established that it is impossible to say that God causes the world without a condition. And we say: It is impossible that that causation be conditioned by a condition. That is because the condition is either pre-eternal or temporal. If it is pre-eternal, then from the pre-eternity of the effecter and the pre-eternity of the condition, the pre-eternity of the world follows, according to what we have established, and that is invalid. And if it is temporal, then the discussion about how it occurs is like the discussion about how the first temporal event occurs, and this leads to infinite regress. That regress either occurs at once, or occurs such that each is preceded by another without a first. The first type, which is the regress that occurs at once, we have already refuted in establishing the Necessary Existent. The second type, which is the statement of the existence of temporal events each of which is preceded by another without a first, is a statement of temporal events without a beginning. And we have refuted it. So it is established that if He were necessary by essence, then either His effect in the existence of the effects would be not dependent on a condition, or it would be dependent on a condition. And the invalidity of both divisions has been established, so it is necessary to conclude the impossibility of His being necessary by essence.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This is a proof from the people of religion (أرباب الملل والأديان) demonstrating that God is a powerful and free agent (قادراً مختاراً), not a necessary agent (موجب بالذات). The proof is structured as a disjunctive syllogism: If God were a necessary agent, then His causation of His effects would be either unconditional (غير موقوف على شرط) or conditional (موقوف على شرط). Both are invalid. The unconditional case leads to the effect being simultaneous with the cause, which would mean either the world is eternal (if God is eternal) or God is temporal (if the world is temporal), but we know God is eternal and the world is temporal, so it's impossible. The conditional case leads to an infinite regress because the condition itself would require another condition, and so on, which is also impossible. Therefore, the assumption that God is a necessary agent is false, and He must be free. The argument carefully eliminates both possibilities.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
قادر مختار
شرط
تسلسل
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof denies any causality in God's action, but it only denies necessary causation, not voluntary causation.
Correction: The proof shows that God's causation cannot be necessary like natural causation; it is free and voluntary.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if God were a necessary agent, what would His causation be?
A) Either conditional or unconditional.
B) Either eternal or temporal.
C) Either by essence or by accident.
D) Either direct or indirect.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun prove that God is a free agent by eliminating the possibility of necessary agency through a disjunctive syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you sometimes expect God to act in a certain way as if He were bound by a rule? How would your prayer change if you truly believed that nothing compels God—that He is absolutely free to answer or not, in His own wisdom? What would that mean for your patience and trust?
?
This proof shows that if God were a necessary agent, His actions would be locked—either always or under fixed conditions. Do you ever live as if God's mercy or anger is automatic, rather than the free act of a Personal Being? If He is free, what does that demand from you in your gratitude and repentance?
?
The proof eliminates both unconditional and conditional necessity from God. Are there areas in your life where you have placed God in a box, assuming He must act in a particular way based on your actions? If you truly believed God is not bound by any necessity, how would that change your fear of Him and your hope in Him?

The First Proof Against God Being Mu'jib bi al-Dhat
The author refutes two types of infinite regress to prove God cannot be mu'jib bi al-dhat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God cannot be a necessitating cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat) because it leads to impossible consequences such as infinite regress or the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
A necessitating cause produces eternal effects — but the world is not eternal, so God is not a necessitating cause.

This passage presents a key argument from Islamic theology that God acts by choice, not by necessity. The author refutes two forms of infinite regress to show that if God were a necessitating cause, His creation would have to be eternal, which is false. This argument is purely rational and foundational for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow.
The passage builds on earlier proofs that infinite regress is impossible.
2
Identify the two types of tasalsul
The passage mentions two forms of infinite regress: one that occurs instantaneously (simultaneous chain) and one with no beginning (temporal chain). Make sure you understand each.
Both types have been refuted in previous arguments about the Necessary Being.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Next, the author presents a conditional: if God were mu'jib bi al-dhat, then His causation would be either unconditional or conditional. Both lead to contradictions.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See why both options are false
If unconditional, the effect would be eternal (coexistent with the cause). If conditional, the condition would require another cause, leading to regress. Both are impossible.
The refutation of regress is used again.
5
Conclude the impossibility
Since both possibilities are false, the premise that God is mu'jib bi al-dhat must be false. God is therefore a voluntary agent (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar).
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقع دفعة، أو يقع بحيث يكون كل واحد منهما مسبوقاً بغيره، لا إلى أول. والقسم الأول: وهو التسلسل الذي يقع دفعة قد أبطلناه في إثبات واجب الوجود. والقسم الثاني: وهو القول بوجود حوادث يكون كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بغيره، لا إلى أول، فهو قول بحوادث لا أول لها. وقد أبطلناه، فيثبت أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات، لكان إما أن يكون تأثيره في وجود المعلولات غير موقوف على شرط، وإما أن يكون موقوفاً على شرط. وثبت فساد القسمين، فوجب القطع بامتناع كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات.
It occurs instantaneously, or it occurs such that each one of them is preceded by another, without an end. The first division: the serial sequence (tasalsul) that occurs instantaneously we have refuted in proving the Necessary of Existence. The second division: the claim that there are events each of which is preceded by another, without an end, it is a claim of events with no beginning. And we have refuted it. It is thereby established that if the Exalted were necessary by essence (mu'jib bi al-dhat), then either His effect in the existence of the effects would not be conditional upon a condition, or it would be conditional upon a condition. The corruption of both divisions has been established, so it is necessary to judge the impossibility of the Exalted being necessary by essence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a necessarily efficient cause (mu'jib bi al-dhat). The author begins by recalling two types of infinite regress (tasalsul) that have been refuted earlier: one that occurs instantaneously (e.g., a chain of simultaneous events) and one of events without a beginning (an infinite past sequence). Having established the impossibility of both, he argues as follows: if God were mu'jib bi al-dhat, His causation would be either unconditional or conditional. If unconditional, the effect would be eternal (coexistent with the cause), leading to the eternity of the world. If conditional, the condition must be either eternal or temporal; if eternal, the effect would again be eternal; if temporal, the condition itself would require a cause and lead to an infinite regress of conditions. Both possibilities are false, so God cannot be mu'jib bi al-dhat. The conclusion is that God must act by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar).

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
تسلسل
حوادث لا أول لها
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof assumes that the world is created rather than eternal, making it a circular argument.
Correction: The proof is purely rational: it shows that the assumption of God being mu'jib bi al-dhat leads to contradictions (either eternity of the world or infinite regress), which are independently proven false. It does not assume the world's non-eternity; rather, it uses the impossibility of infinite regress (established earlier) as a premise.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the refutation of the two types of tasalsul?
A) The world is eternal.
B) God cannot be mu'jib bi al-dhat.
C) Conditions are impossible for God.
D) Events have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a necessitating cause because that would imply either an eternal world or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible; hence, God acts by free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though God's actions toward you are automatic — like a cause that must produce a certain effect — or do you truly believe that He chooses how to respond to your state? Will you repent today for treating God as a mere mechanism rather than a personal, willing Lord?
?
If God were compelled by His essence, your prayers would be meaningless. But this lesson proves He acts by free choice. When was the last time your dua genuinely felt like it could change what God does? How would your prayer life transform if you fully internalized that God wills every interaction with you?
?
The lesson argues that if God's effect were unconditional, the world would be eternal. Yet you experience time and change. How does the reality of a created, temporal world make you more aware that God's will is truly free and not forced? Will you praise Him today for the gift of a world that is not automatic but a product of His deliberate choice?

Reply to the Objection: Eternal Cause and Temporal Effect
The author replies to the objection about eternal causality, showing it leads to an infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how assuming an eternal necessary cause leads to an infinite regress of temporal events, thereby refuting the objection that an eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect.

Core Idea
If an eternal cause can act later, every moment demands an earlier effect, creating an infinite regress.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that an eternal being cannot cause a temporal world because the cause must be simultaneous with its effect. By granting the premise but analyzing its implications, he shows that allowing delayed causation actually leads to a logical absurdity: an infinite chain of temporal events with no first beginning. This demonstrates why the assumption of a necessary cause (موجب بالذات) must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims an eternal cause (God) cannot produce a temporal effect because the cause must coexist with its effect. Here, the author grants this for the past but examines the future.
The author’s strategy is to accept the premise provisionally and derive its consequences.
2
Follow the hypothetical reasoning
Assume that pre-eternity was an obstacle to creation, but that obstacle is removed after the initial moment. Then the world should originate at some later time, giving it a specific first moment.
3
Shift the time earlier
If we imagine the world occurring a hundred years earlier, it would still be temporal, not eternal. So no matter how far back we go, it remains temporal.
This shows that delaying does not make it eternal.
4
Recognize the infinite regress
For any time before the world’s origin, the cause existed and the obstacle was already removed earlier. Thus, the world should have occurred at that earlier time. This applies to every previous moment, creating an infinite regress of temporal events.
5
Conclude the absurdity
This infinite regress is impossible. It arises from assuming God is a necessary cause (موجب بالذات). Therefore, that assumption must be false.
The conclusion refutes the objection and supports the Islamic doctrine that God creates by will, not necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في الجواب عن السؤال المذكور أن نقول : هب أن كونه أزلياً يمنع من كون الموجب موجباً له في الأزل ، إلا أن هذا المانع زائل فيهما لا يزال ، فوجب أن يصدر العالم عنه فيما لا يزال . وإذا كان كذلك كان لحدوثه أول معين . ولا شك أنه لو حدث قبل ذلك الوقت بمائة سنة كان حادثاً أيضاً ، فلا يصير بسبب هذا التقدير من الزيادة أزلياً. وعلى هذا فلا وقت إلا وكانت العلة الموجبة لوجود العالم موجودة قبله ، وإن كان هذا فلا وقت إلا وكانت العلة الموجبة لوجود العالم موجبة قبله وكان الأزل الذي هو العائق عن صدوره عنه زائلا قبله ، فيلزم أن يقال : أنه لا وقت إلا ويجب أن يكون حادثاً قبله . وهذا المحال إنما لزم من فرض كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات
The second aspect: in answer to the aforementioned question, we say: Grant that His being eternal prevents the necessary cause from causing it in pre-eternity, except that this obstacle is removed forever thereafter, so it is necessary that the world originate from Him forever thereafter. If that is so, then its temporal occurrence has a specific beginning. There is no doubt that if it had occurred a hundred years before that time, it would also be temporal, so it does not become eternal due to this increase in estimation. Hence, there is no time except that the necessary cause of the world's existence existed before it, and if this is so, there is no time except that the necessary cause of the world's existence was causing it before it, and the pre-eternity which was the impediment to its issuance from Him had ceased before it. Thus it follows that it must be said: there is no time except that it must be temporal before it. This impossibility arises only from assuming that He, exalted, is a necessary cause.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a reply (الوجه الثاني) to an objection. The objection likely claimed that if God is eternal, He cannot cause a temporal world because the cause must be simultaneous with its effect. The author responds: even if we grant that eternality prevents causation in the past (in the eternal beginning), this obstacle is removed in later times. So the world should come into existence at some later time. But then that time would be a specific first moment. If we imagine it happening a hundred years earlier, it would still be temporal, not eternal. Thus, for any time before the world, the cause (the eternal being) was already present and the obstacle of being in the past was removed earlier. Therefore, for every time, the world should have occurred earlier, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events with no first beginning. This absurdity arises from the assumption that God is a necessary cause (موجب بالذات). Hence, the assumption is false.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
أزل
لا أول
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is eternal, He can only produce an eternal effect, so the world must be eternal.
Correction: The argument does not require that; it shows that even if the cause can act later, it leads to an infinite regress, which is more problematic.

Checkpoint

A) The world must be eternal
B) The world must have a first beginning
C) Every temporal event must have a prior temporal event, leading to an infinite regress
D) The cause must be temporal

Key Takeaway
Assuming an eternal necessary cause forces an infinite regress of temporal events, proving that God creates by will, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that assuming necessity leads to absurdity. In your life, where do you find yourself assuming that God's actions are bound by your own logic? Can you humble yourself to accept that His will is absolutely free, even when you don't understand His timing?
?
This passage confronts the intellectual arrogance of assuming we can fully comprehend God's nature. Are you willing to let go of your need for a logically tidy universe and submit to a God who acts by His will alone?

Second Argument: From the Similarity of Bodies to a Free Agent
The author presents the second kalam argument for God being a free agent based on the need for a differentiator for similar bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument that proves God is a free agent by systematically eliminating all other possible sources of differentiation among similar bodies.

Core Idea
Only a free agent can explain why similar bodies have different attributes.

The mutakallimun developed this argument to prove that God acts by choice, not necessity. It begins with the observation that all bodies are identical in essence, yet they differ in attributes and locations. This difference requires a differentiator, and the argument systematically eliminates all other possibilities, leaving only a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: bodies are similar
Read the opening lines where the author states that all bodies are identical in essence. This is a foundational claim for the argument.
The similarity is in the complete essence (تمام الماهية).
2
Recognize that specific attributes are contingent
Notice that each body's particular attribute and location must be contingent (جائز) because if they were necessary, they'd be the same for all bodies due to similarity.
The rule: the ruling of a thing equals the ruling of its like.
3
Analyze the four possible sources of differentiation
The author identifies four possible differentiators: the body's own essence, a place, an accident, or something else. Follow how each is refuted.
4
Conclude that the differentiator must be a free agent
After eliminating all other options, the only remaining possibility is a free agent. Understand why a necessary cause would fail.
A necessary cause would relate equally to all bodies, leading to uniformity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للمتكلمين في إثبات كونه تعالى فاعلا بالاختيار ، لا موجباً بالذات : هي أن نقول : قد دللنا على أن الأجسام بأسرها متماثلة في تمام الماهية ، فاختصاص كل واحد منها بصفته المعينة وحيزة المعين ، لا بد وأن يكون من الجائزات. لما ثبت أن حكم الشيء يساوي حكم مثله ، وكل جائز فلا بد له من مرجح . وذلك المرجح إما أن يكون ذات الجسم أو غيره . وذلك الغير إما أن يكون محلا للجسم ، أو ما يكون حالاً فيه ، أو ما لا يكون محلا له ، أو لا حالاً فيه ، والأقسام الأربعة باطلة . أما القسم الأول . وهو أن يكون المقتضي لتلك الأحوال والصفات ذات الجسم. فهذا باطل . لأن ذوات الأجسام متماثلة ، وصفاتها مختلفة، وما به الاشتراك لا يكون علة لما به الامتياز . والقسم الثاني باطل . لأنا دللنا على أن الجسم ذات قائمة بنفسها ، فيمتنع يكون لها محل . والقسم الثالث أيضاً باطل . لأن الأجسام كما أنها مختلفة في هذه الصفات المحسوسة فهي أيضاً مختلفة في الصفات المقتضية لهذه الصفات المحسوسة فهي لا بد وأن تكون لصفات أخرى . ويلزم التسلسل . ثم ذلك التسلسل إن وقع دفعة واحدة فهو باطل. كما بيناه ، وإن وقع بحيث يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ، لا إلى أول . فقد أبطلناه أيضاً . والقسم الرابع أيضاً باطل . لأن ذلك المقتضى إن كان جسماً أو جسمانياً، عاد التقسيم فيه، وهو أنه كيف اختص ذلك الجسم بالصفة التي لأجلها صار مقتضياً لهذه الأحكام ، وإن لم يكن جسماً ولا جسمانياً فهو إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً ، ولا جائز أن يكون موجباً ، لأنه لما لم يكن جسماً ولا جسمانياً لم يكن مختصاً بشيء من الأجسام بالقرب منه أو بالبعد منه ، وإذا كانت نسبته إلى الكل واحدة ، وكانت الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في قبول هذه الصفات ، فحينئذ لا يكون حصول الصفة المعينة في بعض الأجسام أولى من حصولها في سائر الأجسام ، لأن القوابل بأسرها متساوية في القبول والمؤثر ، والنسبة إلى الكل على السوية . فوجب التشابه المذكور ، وحيث لم يحصل علمنا أن القول بالموجب باطل . وإذا بطل هذا يثبت أن فاعل العالم فاعل مختار ، لا علة موجبة بالذات ، واعلم أن مدار هذه الحجة ومدار الحجة التي قبلها على القول ببطلان حوادث لا أول لها
The second argument of the mutakallimun in proving that He, exalted, is a free agent, not a necessary cause: it is that we say: We have demonstrated that all bodies are similar in the entirety of their essence. So the specification of each one of them with its particular attribute and its particular location must be among the contingent things, because it is established that the ruling of a thing equals the ruling of its like, and every contingent must have a differentiator. That differentiator is either the essence of the body or something else. That something else is either a place for the body, or an accident in it, or something that is neither a place for it nor an accident in it. The four divisions are invalid. As for the first division—that the determinant of those states and attributes is the essence of the body—this is invalid because the essences of bodies are similar, while their attributes are different, and that which is common cannot be the cause of that which distinguishes. The second division is invalid because we have demonstrated that the body is a substance subsisting by itself, so it is impossible for it to have a place. The third division is also invalid because bodies, just as they differ in these sensible attributes, also differ in the attributes that necessitate these sensible attributes, so they must be for other attributes, and a regress follows. Then that regress, if it occurs all at once, is invalid, as we have explained; and if it occurs such that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event without a first, we have also refuted that. The fourth division is also invalid, for if that determinant is a body or a bodily thing, the division recurs with respect to it—namely, how that body came to be specified by the attribute due to which it became a determinant of these rulings? And if it is not a body nor a bodily thing, then it is either necessary or free. It is not possible for it to be necessary, because when it is not a body nor a bodily thing, it is not particularized with respect to any bodies by nearness to it or distance from it. When its relation to all is one, and all bodies are equal in receiving these attributes, then the occurrence of a specific attribute in some bodies is not more likely than its occurrence in the rest of the bodies, because the recipients are all equal in receptivity, and the effect has a uniform relation to all. Hence the aforementioned similarity would be necessary, and since it does not obtain, we know that the doctrine of the necessary cause is false. When this is refuted, it is established that the agent of the world is a free agent, not a necessary cause. And know that the pivot of this argument and the argument before it is the doctrine of the nullification of temporal events with no first beginning.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is the second argument (الحجة الثانية) of the mutakallimun to prove that God is a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار), not a necessary cause (موجب بالذات). The argument proceeds by analyzing the differentiation of bodies. Bodies are all identical in essence (تماثل في الماهية). Yet each has specific attributes and spatial locations. These specificities are contingent (جائزات) because if they were necessary, they would be the same for all bodies due to similarity. Every contingent needs a differentiator (مرجح). This differentiator is either the body's own essence or something else. The something else is either a place for the body, or an accident in it, or something that is neither a place nor an accident. These four divisions are all invalid. First, the body's own essence cannot be the differentiator because essences are similar and what is common cannot cause distinction. Second, the body cannot have a place because it is a substance (ذات قائمة بنفسها). Third, if the differentiator is an accident, then those accidents themselves require differentiators, leading to an infinite regress, which is rejected whether simultaneous or sequential. Fourth, if the differentiator is neither a body nor a bodily thing, it must be either necessary or free. It cannot be necessary because if it is non-bodily, it has no special relation to any body; its relation to all bodies is equal. Since all bodies are equally receptive, the differentiator would produce the same attributes in all, but we observe differences, so it must be a free agent. Therefore, the agent of the world is a free chooser. The argument also notes that it depends on the rejection of an infinite regress of temporal events.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
موجب بالذات
مرجح
تماثل الأجسام
جائز
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary cause (موجب بالذات) could be the differentiator that produces differences among bodies.
Correction: The argument shows that if the cause were necessary and non-bodily, it would relate uniformly to all bodies, so all bodies would have the same attributes. Since they differ, the cause must be free.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the differentiator of the attributes of bodies to be the body itself?
A) Because bodies are different in attributes
B) Because bodies are similar in essence, and what is common cannot be the cause of difference
C) Because bodies are not causes
D) Because the body is not a free agent

Key Takeaway
The differences among similar bodies prove that God is a free agent, not a necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though every detail of your life—your location, your attributes, your circumstances—is directly chosen by a free Agent, or do you secretly attribute them to impersonal forces like luck, genetics, or environment? What would change if you were fully convinced that God personally selected each particular aspect of your existence?
?
When you face a trial that seems unfair or random, do you trust that a free, wise Agent chose it for you, or do you fall into resentment as if it were meaningless chance? Examine your heart: are there areas where you treat God as a necessary cause—expecting uniform outcomes—rather than as a free agent who may choose differently?
?
If you truly believed that all similar people (like you) have different fates not because of anything in themselves but because of God's free choice, would you stop comparing yourself to others? Would you accept your unique situation as a direct and personal gift from the One who chooses freely? Why do you still struggle with envy or dissatisfaction?

The Unity of Effect Argument
The author argues by reductio that the world's cause cannot be necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the unity of effect demonstrates that the world's cause must be a free agent.

Core Idea
A necessary cause can only have one immediate effect, but the world has many, so the cause must be freely willing.

The author presents a powerful reductio argument. If the world's cause were necessary, it would produce only one effect, which would then produce another, leading to a chain where everything is linked. But we see unrelated things like drops of water and dust particles, so the assumption that the cause is necessary fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the argument is structured as a dichotomy.
The word 'الخصر' may be a copyist error for 'الأول' – the first of the two divisions.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author splits the assumption into two cases: the effect is one, or it is more than one. Both are claimed false.
3
Understand why one effect is impossible
If the effect is one, that effect itself must cause another single effect, and so on, creating a single chain linking everything. But we observe unrelated things like water drops and dust particles, contradicting this.
This is the core of the reductio.
4
See the conclusion
Since both divisions are impossible, the premise that the cause is necessary must be false. Therefore, the cause is free.
This argument is a classic example of kalam reasoning.
5
Reflect on the principle 'from one only one'
The argument relies on the principle that a necessary cause produces only one immediate effect. Reflect on why this principle is plausible.
The principle is debated; the author assumes it for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان المؤثر في العالم موجباً بالذات ، لكان إما أن يكون معلوله واحداً ، وإما أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بالموجب باطل . أما الخصر فظاهر . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله شيئاً واحداً . ذلك لأنه لما وجب أن يكون معلول الواحد واحداً فقط . فمعلول ذلك الواحد أيضاً يجب أن يكون واحداً . وهلم جرا إلى آخر المراتب . فيلزم أن لا يوجد في هذا العالم شيئاً من قطرات الماء ، وذرات الهباءات ، إلا ويكون أحدهما علة للآخر ، والآخر معلولا له ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل .
The third argument: If the effect in the world were a necessary cause, then its effect would be either one or more than one. Both divisions are invalid, so the doctrine of the necessary cause is false. As for the first part, it is obvious. We only say: It is impossible that its effect be one thing. That is because when it is necessary that the effect of the one be only one, then the effect of that one must also be one, and so on to the end of the ranks. Consequently, there would not exist in this world anything from drops of water and atoms of dust except that one of them would be the cause of the other and the other its effect, and it is known that this is false.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the third argument (الحجة الثالثة) that the world's agent must be a free agent. It argues by reductio: if the agent were a necessary cause, its effect would be one single thing. Because from one necessary cause only one effect proceeds. Then that effect would also be a cause for another single effect, and so on in a linear chain. Consequently, everything in the world would be linked as cause and effect in a single chain. But we see water drops and dust particles that are not linked in such a chain. This is false, so the assumption that the agent is a necessary cause is false. Therefore, the agent is free.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
معلول
واحد
كثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One cause can produce many effects directly without a chain.
Correction: From a necessary cause, only one effect proceeds directly; many effects require a free cause.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of assuming the world's agent is a necessary cause?
A) The world would be eternal
B) Everything in the world would be part of a single chain of causation from one cause
C) There would be no causation
D) The world would be non-existent

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that a necessary cause would force a single chain of causation, contradicting the observed diversity in the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world were the product of a necessary cause, you would be nothing but a link in an endless chain—no true choice, no freedom. Do you live as if you are merely a product of causes, or as one who stands before a free Creator? What would change in your daily decisions if you truly believed that your existence is a direct gift from the will of God, not from impersonal necessity?
?
The argument proves that God is free—but do you treat Him that way? Do you pray to Him as if He is bound by your expectations, or as the absolute sovereign who can act independently? In what situation have you assumed God 'must' act a certain way, forgetting His absolute freedom?
?
If every drop of water and atom of dust is not causally determined by a single necessary chain, then your small, ordinary moments are not predetermined either. What small, seemingly 'necessary' habit in your life is actually an opportunity to choose freely for God? Will you embrace that freedom starting today?

The Eighth Proof: Refutation of Causal Relationship
Argument that the existence-as-cause makes the recipient part of the agent or independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni theologian's argument that an attribute of a possible being cannot act as an efficient cause without leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
An attribute cannot be an independent cause; otherwise it becomes self-subsistent.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument against the philosophers' view that an attribute of a possible being can act as an efficient cause. Using a dilemma, he shows that either the recipient becomes part of the agent or the attribute becomes independent, both of which are unacceptable. Let's examine the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author starts by quoting the philosophers' principle.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents a dilemma: either the existence acts with the participation of the essence or without. Identify both horns and the consequences.
This is a typical method in kalam arguments.
3
Trace the contradiction
Follow how the second horn leads to the existence becoming self-subsistent, contradicting the assumption that it is an attribute.
This is the heart of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : مذهبهم أن ما كان غنياً في فعله عن الغير، كان غنياً في ذاته عن ذلك الغير. فنقول : كون ذلك الوجود علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى إما أن يكون بمشاركة من تلك الماهية الممكنة أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فقد صار القابل جزءاً من الفاعل، وهو عندكم محال . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ ذلك الوجود غني في ذلك التأثير عن تلك الماهية، فوجب كونه غنياً في نفسه عن تلك الماهية، فذلك الوجود أمر قائم بنفسه، غني عن تلك الماهية . وقد فرضناه صفة لتلك الماهية . هذا خلف
The Eighth Proof: Their doctrine is that whatever is independent in its act from another is independent in its essence from that other. So we say: That existence's being a cause for the form of the outermost sphere is either with participation of that possible essence or not. If the first, then the recipient has become a part of the agent, and according to you that is impossible. If the second, then that existence is independent in that effect from that essence, so it must be independent in itself from that essence. Thus that existence is a self-subsistent thing, independent of that essence. But we had assumed it to be an attribute of that essence. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) against the philosophers' view that the First Cause emanates multiple things. The author, a Sunni theologian, argues that if the existence (which they attribute to an essence) is a cause for the form of the outermost sphere, then either it acts with the participation of the possible essence or without. If with participation, the recipient becomes part of the agent, which is impossible. If without, then that existence is independent in its effect and thus independent in itself, but it was assumed to be an attribute—so a contradiction arises.

Key Terms
علة
ماهية
وجود
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about God's independence or about the First Cause itself.
Correction: The argument is specifically about the attribute (existence) of a possible being, not about God. It refutes the notion that an attribute of a contingent can be a cause.

Checkpoint
What does the Eighth Proof argue must happen if the existence is a cause without participation of the essence?
A) The existence becomes self-subsistent and independent of the essence.
B) The existence becomes identical with the essence.
C) The existence ceases to exist.
D) The essence becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The existence attributed to a possible essence cannot be a cause without either making the recipient part of the agent or becoming independent itself, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on something created as if it has independent power, forgetting that only Allah is truly independent? How can you train yourself to see all causes as dependent?
?
The philosophers assumed that independence in act implies independence in essence. Do you ever assume that because you can do something without help, you don't need Allah? How does this argument correct that?
?
If an attribute cannot be a cause without becoming self-subsistent, what does that tell you about the relationship between your actions and your own self? Are you your actions, or are they attributes of your essence that have no power of their own?

The Sixth Refutation: Eternal Attributes Are Unavoidable
Author counters the sixth proof by invoking consensus on God's eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the consensus on God's eternal attributes to refute the claim that eternal entities are impossible.

Core Idea
The proof against eternal entities backfires on the objectors themselves.

This lesson presents the author's response to the sixth proof, which argued that positing eternal entities is impossible. The author cleverly turns the tables by pointing to the universal agreement among religions that God has eternal attributes distinct from His essence. Thus, the very objects of the proof—eternal entities—are affirmed by the objectors' own theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author states the proof concisely, then immediately counters.
2
Identify the objection and response
The proof claims 'positing eternal entities is impossible' (محال). The response invokes consensus (اتفاق) on God's eternal attributes and the doctrine that they are not identical to essence.
The terms 'عالم' and 'قادر' are the attributes mentioned.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this argument refute the proof? Because the objectors themselves must accept that God's attributes are eternal, so they are committed to the very thing they declared impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة السادسة : إن القول بإثبات القدماء محال . فنقول : إن أرباب الملل والأديان اتفقوا على أنه تعالى ، كان عالماً قادراً في الأزل ، وكونه عالماً قادراً ليس عين ذاته المخصوصة ، على ما سيأتي تفصيل ذلك . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان القول بإثبات القدماء لازماً عليهم . فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم
As for the sixth proof, that the statement of establishing eternal entities is impossible. We say: The people of creeds and religions agreed that He, exalted, was knowing and powerful in pre-eternity, and His being knowing and powerful is not identical to His specific essence, as will come in detail. If this is the case, then the statement of establishing eternal entities is necessary upon them. So this completes the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the sixth proof (الحجة السادسة). The philosophers apparently argued that positing any eternal entities besides God (possibly the divine attributes or other eternal principles) is impossible (محال). The author counters by appealing to the consensus of people of religions (ملل وأديان) that God was knowing and powerful in pre-eternity, and that these attributes are not identical to His essence (a key Ash'ari doctrine). Therefore, if the philosophers accept these attributes as eternal, they are committed to the very thing they declare impossible: eternal entities distinct from God. The author then concludes the chapter (هذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب).

Key Takeaway

Response to the Sixth Objection: Eternal Attributes and Pre-Eternal Entities
Refutes the impossibility of affirming pre-eternal entities by citing divine knowledge and power.

Learning Objective

The consensus on God's eternal attributes necessitates affirming pre-eternal realities.

The sixth objection claims that positing any pre-eternal entity besides God is impossible. The author counters with a powerful point: all religious traditions agree that God is eternally knowing and powerful, and these attributes are not identical to His essence. The Proof from Perfection for God's Knowledge
Al-Razi presents the proof from perfection for God's knowledge with two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's knowledge based on the perfection of creation, including its two premises.

Core Idea
The perfection of creation proves that its Creator must be knowing.

This lesson presents one of the classic kalām arguments for God's knowledge, known as the proof from soundness and perfection (dalīl al-iḥkām wa al-itqān). Al-Rāzī states the argument concisely and unpacks its two premises: that God's acts are perfect, and that perfect acts require knowledge. He then supports the second premise with everyday examples. As you read, notice how the proof connects observable order in the universe to a necessary divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim and two premises
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Look for the overall argument and the two explicit premises Al-Razi outlines.
The passage begins by stating the claim, then says 'we need here to establish two premises'.
2
Study the first premise: God's acts are sound and perfect
Al-Razi defines 'sound and perfect' as being in accordance with benefit and interest. He mentions many examples but omits them because his book already contains them.
The first premise is about the quality of divine actions, not just their existence.
3
Study the second premise: Perfect acts require knowledge
Al-Razi uses examples like weaving and calligraphy to show that ignorance prevents perfection. He notes that some theologians consider this connection to be necessary knowledge.
Notice that the proof relies on a general principle derived from everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
حكاية ودلائل المتكلمين في كونه تعالى عالماً
اعلم أن القائلين بأن العالم محدث، وأن إله العالم قادر مختار احتجوا بدليل الإحكام والإتقان على كونه تعالى عالماً بالمعلومات. وتقريره: أن قالوا: إن أفعال الله محكمة متقنة، وكل من كان فعله محكماً متقناً وجب كونه عالماً فيلزم كونه تعالى عالماً بالأشياء
فنفتقر ههنا إلى تقرير مقدمتين:
إحداهما: قولنا: أفعال الله محكمة متقنة. والمراد من كونها محكمة متقنة: كونها موافقة لوجوه المصلحة والمنفعة. وهذا إنما يظهر ببيان حكمة الله تعالى في تخليق السموات والكواكب، وفي تخليق العناصر الأربعة، وفي تخليق الآثار العلوية والمعادن والنبات والحيوان والإنسان، وأعجبها شرح أبدان الناس، ولما كان هذا الكتاب الكبير الشريف مملوءاً من هذا النوع من العلوم، لا جرم لم يكن في إيراد هذا النوع ههنا مزيد فائدة. وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي قولنا: وكل من كان فعله محكماً متقناً، فإنه يجب أن يكون عالماً. فتقريره بالأمثلة الكثيرة: فإن الجاهل بنسج الديباج لا يمكنه أن يأتي هذا العمل على وجه الإتقان، والجاهل بالخط لا يمكنه أن يأتي بالخط على وجه الإتقان. ولا سبب لذلك إلا عدم العلم، فيثبت أن عدم العلم بالشيء ينافي الإتيان به على وجه الإحكام والإتقان
ومن المتكلمين من يدعي العلم الضروري بأن فاعل المحكم المتقن يجب عالماً بذلك الفعل، ويذكر تلك الأمثلة لأجل التنبيه على أن العلم بهذه المقدمة علم ضروري. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل
Chapter Two: The Account and Proofs of the Theologians Concerning His Being Exalted is Knowing. Know that those who affirm that the world is temporally originated and that the God of the world is powerful and free agent argued by the proof of soundness (iḥkām) and perfection (itqān) that He Exalted is knowing of the known things. Its formulation: They said: Indeed, the acts of God are sound and perfect, and everyone whose act is sound and perfect must be knowing, so it follows that He Exalted is knowing of things. So we need here to establish two premises: The first: Our statement: The acts of God are sound and perfect. What is meant by their being sound and perfect is their being in accordance with the aspects of benefit and interest. This becomes apparent only by an exposition of the wisdom of God Exalted in the creation of the heavens and the planets, in the creation of the four elements, in the creation of the celestial effects, minerals, plants, animals, and humans, and the most wondrous of them is the constitution of human bodies. And since this great noble book is filled with this type of knowledge, there is no additional benefit in presenting this type here. As for the second premise: Our statement: And everyone whose act is sound and perfect must be knowing. Its establishment is by many examples: Indeed, one ignorant of weaving silk brocade cannot produce this work in a perfect manner, and one ignorant of calligraphy cannot produce calligraphy in a perfect manner. There is no cause for that except the absence of knowledge, so it is established that the absence of knowledge of something precludes performing it in a manner of soundness and perfection. And among the theologians are those who claim necessary knowledge that the agent of the sound, perfect act must be knowing of that act, and they mention those examples for the purpose of alerting that knowledge of this premise is necessary knowledge. This completes the discussion on establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's knowledge (ʿilm) based on the perfection and soundness (iḥkām and itqān) of creation. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, first states the claim: those who affirm the world's temporal origination and God's power and free will argue that God's acts are perfect, and whoever performs perfect acts must be knowing, so God is knowing of things. He then explains that two premises are needed. The first premise is that God's acts are sound and perfect, meaning they conform to benefit and interest. Al-Razi notes that this is demonstrated by the wisdom in creation but says his book is already full of such examples, so he omits them. The second premise is that anyone whose act is perfect must be knowing. He supports this with examples: an ignorant weaver cannot produce perfect silk; an ignorant calligrapher cannot produce perfect writing. The cause of imperfection is ignorance, so lack of knowledge prevents perfection. Some theologians consider this connection a necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge, and the examples serve only as reminders. The passage concludes the exposition of this proof.

Key Terms
الإحكام
الإتقان
المصلحة
المنفعة
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires enumerating all examples of divine perfection to be valid.
Correction: Al-Rāzī explicitly states that his book already contains many such examples, so repeating them here would not add benefit. The proof stands on the general principle, not on exhaustive listing.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise in the proof from perfection (itqān)?
A) The acts of God are sound and perfect.
B) Anyone whose act is perfect must be knowing.
C) The ignorant cannot produce perfect work.
D) Knowledge of this premise is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The perfection of creation rationally necessitates that its Creator possesses knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change in your daily life if you genuinely attributed every perfect function to God's knowledge?
?
Al-Rāzī argues that a perfect act must come from someone who knows what they are doing. How does that failure highlight the necessity of God's perfect knowledge, and what can you learn from it?
?
If knowledge of God's perfection is, as some theologians claim, 'necessary knowledge' (ḍarūrī), then why do you sometimes deny or forget it? What distractions or attachments blind you to the obvious signs of divine wisdom in creation? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to consciously acknowledge God's knowledge in a specific situation.

Completion of the Proof from Perfection: The Creator Must Be Knowing
The author concludes the proof that the maker of the perfect world must be knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfection and precision in creation logically require the creator to possess knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfect creation implies a knowing creator; ignorance cannot produce precision.

We have arrived at the final part of the proof from perfection. The author argues that the flawless order we observe could only come from an agent with knowledge. This passage ties together the entire argument and notes that some theologians consider this conclusion intuitive.

Guided Path

Read the passage and translation

Notice how the author states the premise directly.
2
Identify the key premise

3

This shows the strength of the argument in their view.
4

The passage concludes 'this completes the speech in establishing this proof.' See how all parts of the argument come together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالخط على وجه الإتقان. ولا سبب لذلك إلا عدم العلم، فيثبت أن عدم العلم بالشيء ينافي الإتيان به على وجه الإحكام والإتقان. ومن المتكلمين من يدعي العلم الضروري بأن فاعل المحكم المتقن يجب عالماً بذلك الفعل، ويذكر تلك الأمثلة لأجل التنبيه على أن العلم بهذه المقدمة علم ضروري. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل

So this completes the speech in establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the final part of a proof for God's knowledge. The argument states that the perfection and precision observed in creation cannot arise from an agent lacking knowledge; therefore, the creator must be knowing. The author notes that some theologians consider this knowledge intuitive (ضروري). The passage concludes the exposition of this proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

If you have never paused to reflect on the perfection of the world around you, what does that say about your awareness of God? Commit to spending one minute later today observing one thing in creation and letting it lead you to that intuition.
