The First Objection: Intermediaries in the Proof for God's Attributes
The objection argues that the proof for God's attributes does not exclude intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the kalam proof for God's power and knowledge, which argues that the proof does not exclude the possibility of intermediaries.

Core Idea
A proof must exclude all plausible alternatives; the objection claims it fails to do so.

In this passage, the author presents a sophisticated objection to the standard theological proof for God's attributes. The objection points out a logical gap: even if the proof establishes a powerful, knowing creator, it does not rule out that this creator itself depends on a higher cause or is mediated through intermediaries. The author acknowledges the force of this objection and then prepares to respond.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage for the objection once, then the translation. Note how the objection is framed as a 'question' (سؤال).
The objection is not a refutation but a request for further proof.
2
Identify the logical gap
The objection states that the proof for a powerful, knowing creator does not eliminate the possibility that this creator itself is an effect or requires intermediaries. Find the key phrase 'لا يمتنع في بديهة العقل' (not impossible in reason).
3
Understand the consequence
If the possibility remains, the proof is insufficient. The author calls this a 'clear question' and notes that few theologians have addressed it.
This shows the author's intellectual honesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول: هو أن دلائل المتكلمين في إثبات القادرية والعالمية، بتقدير صحتها لا تدل على المقصود، لأنه لا يمتنع في بديهة العقل أن يقال: إن واجب الوجود لذاته علة الموجود، وذلك الموجود عالم لذاته وقادر لذاته، وخالق للعالم، فإنه لما ثبت أن العالم محدث وجب افتقاره إلى محدث وفاعل ولزم أن يكون ذلك الفاعل قادراً عالماً. ولا يبعد أن يكون ذلك العالم القادر معلولا لذاته أو واجب الوجود لذاته. إما من غير واسطة، أو بواسطة واحدة أو بوسائط كثيرة. وإذا كان ما ذكرناه محتملاً، سقط الاستدلال بهذه الوجوه المذكورة على كون واجب الوجود لذاته عالماً قادراً، فهذا سؤال مبين، وما رأيت أحداً من المتكلمين دار حوله. والغفلة عنه في مثل هذه المطالب العالية من العجائب. واعلم أن الذي يمكننا ذكره في دفع هذا السؤال وجهان: الأول: إنا قد ذكرنا في مقدمة هذا الكتاب أن المطالب الإلهية عالية والعقول البشرية ضعيفة، فيجب أن يكتفى فيها بالأخذ بالأخلق والأولى. وعند هذا نقول: لما ثبت حدوث العالم وثبت افتقاره إلى فاعل ومحدث، وجب الاعتراف بهذا الفاعل والمحدث. فأما إثبات الوسائط فلم يدل على وجودها دليل فوجب الأخذ بالمتيقن، وطرح المشكوك المشتبه، فهذا أحد الطريقين. الثاني: لما دللنا على أنه تعالى قادر على الإيجاد والتكوين، فحينئذ نقيم الدلالة على أنه يستحيل في الوجود أن يكون أكثر من مؤثر واحد. وعند هذا يسقط القول بإثبات الوسائط. فهذا ما يمكن ذكره في هذا المقام
The first question: that the proofs of the theologians in establishing the attributes of power and knowledge, even if they are sound, do not indicate the intended [conclusion], because it is not impossible in the clear reason to say that the Necessary Being in essence is the cause of existence, and that existence is knowing in essence and powerful in essence, and creator of the world, because when it is established that the world is originated, it necessitates its need for an originator and an agent, and it is necessary that that agent be powerful and knowing. And it is not far-fetched that that knowing, powerful being be an effect in essence or necessary of existence in essence, either without intermediary, or with one intermediary, or with many intermediaries. And if what we have mentioned is possible, the inference by these mentioned proofs that the Necessary Being in essence is knowing and powerful falls. So this is a clear question, and I have not seen any theologian who has addressed it. And negligence of it in such lofty inquiries is astonishing. Know that what we can mention to repel this question are two ways: The first: we have mentioned in the introduction of this book that divine inquiries are lofty and human intellects are weak, so one must suffice with adopting the most fitting and preferable. And with this we say: when the temporality of the world is established and its need for an agent and originator is established, it is necessary to acknowledge that agent and originator. As for establishing intermediaries, no proof for their existence is provided, so it is necessary to take the certain and discard the doubtful and ambiguous. This is one of the two ways. The second: after we have proven that He, exalted, is powerful over bringing into existence and creating, then we establish the proof that it is impossible in existence to have more than one effective agent. And with this the claim of establishing intermediaries falls. So this is what can be mentioned in this context.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the proof for God's being knowing and powerful. The objection states that the proof does not preclude the possibility that the knowing and powerful creator is itself an effect or has intermediaries. The author responds with two ways: first, a methodological principle of accepting only what is certain and discarding the doubtful; second, a proof that there cannot be more than one effective agent, which negates the need for intermediaries.

Key Takeaway

Two Responses to the Intermediaries Objection
The author provides two responses to the intermediaries objection: a methodological and a substantive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two responses to the intermediaries objection: a methodological principle of accepting only what is certain, and a proof for the impossibility of multiple effective agents.

Core Idea
Certainty comes from accepting only the certain; unity of agency rules out any intermediaries.

Having presented a formidable objection, the author now offers two distinct responses. The first response is methodological: given the lofty nature of divine inquiries and human intellectual weakness, we should accept only what is certain and discard what is doubtful. The second response is substantive: by proving that only one effective agent can exist, any intermediaries become impossible. Both responses aim to neutralize the objection without conceding its force.

Guided Path
1
Read both responses
Read the Arabic passage for the two responses and the translation. Note the author labels them as 'two ways' (وجهان).
The first response appeals to epistemic caution; the second uses a proof for divine unity.
2
Analyze the first response
The first response relies on a methodological principle: in lofty matters, take the most fitting and preferable (الأخلق والأولى). Identify the key principle and how it applies to the objection.
The author says to take the certain (existence of an originator) and discard the doubtful (intermediaries).
3
Analyze the second response
The second response argues that there cannot be more than one effective agent (مؤثر واحد). Find the reasoning: once we prove God is powerful, we can prove that multiple agents are impossible, negating intermediaries.
This is a stronger, more direct logical refutation.
4
Compare the responses
Notice that the first response is a fallback (taking the certain), while the second is a positive argument. Discuss which is more compelling.

Second Objection: Defining Perfection and Precision
The objection questions the meaning of 'perfection' and whether it implies knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the proof from perfection, which challenges the definition of perfection and argues that it does not necessarily imply a knowledgeable creator.

Core Idea
Perfection is ambiguous; it doesn't automatically prove divine knowledge.

The proof from perfection claims that the precise arrangement of the universe proves its Creator is all-knowing. But what exactly do we mean by 'perfection'? This objection presses that question, showing that the term is vague and that neither of its possible meanings successfully establishes the required knowledge. The passage unpacks this challenge, forcing us to refine our understanding of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation to understand the argument.
Notice the dialectical style: the objector poses questions.
2
Identify the two meanings of perfection
The passage divides perfection into two possibilities: benefit in some respects and benefit in all respects. Find where this distinction is made.
This is a classic dichotomy (taqsim).
3
Understand the critique of the first meaning
If perfection means benefit in some respects, even an ignorant person can produce such acts. So it doesn't prove knowledge. Follow the reasoning.
4
Understand the critique of the second meaning
If perfection means benefit in all respects, we cannot know if the world is perfectly arranged because we lack comprehensive knowledge of all possible arrangements.
The objection uses examples like the sun and human bodies.
5
Reflect on the overall impact
Consider how this objection weakens the proof from perfection. What does it say about our ability to infer divine attributes from creation?
This is a classic skeptical objection in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: أن نقول ما المراد بالإحكام والإتقان؟ فإن أريد به وقوع هذه الأشياء على وفق المصلحة والمنفعة، فهذا معقول، وإن عنيتم به معنى آخر، فلا بد من بيانه. ثم نقول: إما أن يريدوا به كونه واقعاً على وفق المصلحة بوجه ما، وإما أن يريدوا به كونه واقعاً على وفق المصلحة من كل الوجوه. فإن أردتم الأول، فهذا القدر لا يدل على كون الفاعل عالماً، فإن الفعل الذي يأتي به الجاهل قد يكون مطابقاً للمصلحة من بعض الوجوه. وإن عنيتم به كونه مطابقاً للمصلحة من كل الوجوه، فلم قلتم إن الأمر كذلك؟ فإنا لا نعرف أن الشمس لو كانت أكبر ما هي الآن أو أصغر كانت المنافع أكمل أو أقل، ولا نعرف أحوال الأفلاك في هذا الباب، ولا نعرف أيضاً أن أبدان الناس لو كانت واقعة على غير هذا الوجه والشكل كيف كانت تكون أحوالها؟ وبالجملة: فمن الذي يمكنه إقامة الدلالة على أنه يمتنع وجود وضع أكمل عما هو الآن مع أن الأوضاع المغايرة لهذا الوضع أمور غير متناهية؟ ومن الذي أحاط عقله بكل تلك الأقسام، ووقف عقله على كل ما في كل واحد منها من المنافع والمضار؟
The second question: we say what is meant by perfection (إحكام) and precision (إتقان)? If what is meant by it is that these things occur in accordance with benefit and advantage, then this is reasonable. And if you mean by it another meaning, then it must be clarified. Then we say: either you intend by it that it occurs in accordance with benefit in some respect, or you intend that it occurs in accordance with benefit in all respects. If you intend the first, then this amount does not indicate that the agent is knowledgeable, for an act performed by an ignorant person may be in accordance with benefit in some respects. And if you intend that it is in accordance with benefit in all respects, then why do you say that it is so? For we do not know whether if the sun were larger or smaller than it is now, the benefits would be more or less, nor do we know the states of the celestial spheres in this matter, nor do we know whether if human bodies were in a different form and shape, how their conditions would be. In short, who can establish the proof that it is impossible to have a more perfect situation than what exists now, given that the different arrangements contrary to this arrangement are infinite? And who has encompassed with his intellect all those categories and understood all that is in each of them of benefits and harms?
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the proof from perfection. It challenges the definition of 'perfection' and 'precision', arguing that if they mean merely benefit in some respects, even an ignorant person can produce such actions. If they mean benefit in all respects, then we cannot know that the world is perfectly arranged, as we lack comprehensive knowledge of all possible arrangements. Thus, the objection undermines the proof that the agent must be knowing.

Key Terms
الإحكام
الإتقان
المصلحة
المنفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'perfection' is a clear and self-evident attribute that obviously indicates a knowing creator.
Correction: The objection shows that the term 'perfection' is ambiguous and that neither of its common interpretations automatically leads to the conclusion of a knowing agent.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, if 'perfection' means benefit in some respects, what does it imply about the agent?
A) The agent must be all-knowing
B) The agent may be ignorant
C) The agent must be powerful
D) The agent must be eternal

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges the proof from perfection by showing that 'perfection' is ambiguous and that neither of its possible meanings successfully demonstrates that the creator is knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage forces you to admit that you do not know if the sun could be better sized or if your body could be more perfectly shaped. Third Objection: Ignorant Agents and Perfect Acts
The objector presents the first aspect of the third objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the third objection against the proof from perfection, which claims that an ignorant person can also produce a perfect action.

Core Idea
Ignorance does not prevent an agent from producing a perfect act.

We now turn to the third objection against the argument from perfection. After conceding the definition of perfection, the objector asks why perfect action must imply knowledge. The first aspect points to examples of ignorant agents who still produce flawless works. This challenges the necessity of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the question being posed and the beginning of the answer.
The text cuts off after mentioning the first aspect, so only one point is given.
2
Identify the key question and first aspect
The objector asks: why must perfect action require knowledge? Then states the first aspect: it is undisputed that an ignorant person can produce a perfect action.
The phrase 'الجاهل بالصفة' may mean 'ignorant of the attribute' or 'ignorant by description'.
3
Reflect on the implication
If an ignorant person can produce a perfect act, then the connection between perfection and knowledge is broken. This weakens the original proof.
Think of examples like an untrained person creating a beautiful artwork by accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third question: we have conceded the discussion about the interpretation of perfection and precision. So why do you say that everyone whose action is perfect and precise must be knowledgeable? And that which indicates that the matter is not so are [several] aspects: The first: that there is no dispute that the ignorant person with the attribute [or: by description] may produce a perfect action.

The Second Objection: What Does 'Perfection' Really Mean?
The author poses the second objection questioning the connection between a thing's perfection and the creator's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the argument from design: that the concept of 'perfection' in creation is ambiguous and cannot prove the creator's knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfection in creation is ambiguous; we cannot know it is perfect in all respects.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful objection against using the perfection and precision of the world as evidence for divine knowledge. The objection forces us to clarify what 'perfection' means. Is it conformity to benefit in some respects or in all? The author argues that under neither interpretation can we conclude that the creator is all-knowing. Let's explore this careful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic text carefully. Notice how the objection is structured as a question followed by a dilemma.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'perfection'
The objection distinguishes two possible meanings of 'perfection': benefit in some respects vs. benefit in all respects. Find where these are introduced.
3
Analyze the objection's argument
For each meaning, see why the objection says it fails to prove knowledge. For the first, ignorant can produce such acts. For the second, we cannot know if it's true.
4
Reflect on the epistemological challenge
Consider the final point: we lack comprehensive knowledge of all possible arrangements. This undermines claims about absolute perfection.
This is the core of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن نقول ما المراد بالإحكام والإتقان ؟ فإن أريد به وقوع هذه الأشياء على وفق المصلحة والمنفعة ، فهذا معقول ، وإن عنيتم به معنی آخر ، فلا بد من بيانه ثم نقول : إما أن يريدوا به كونه واقعاً على وفق المصلحة بوجه ما وإما أن يريدوا به كونه واقعاً على وفق المصلحة من كل الوجوه. فإن أردتم الأول ، فهذا القدر ، لا يدل على كون الفاعل عالماً فإن الفعل الذي يأتي به الجاهل ، قد يكون مطابقاً للمصلحة من بعض الوجوه . وإن عنيتم به كونه مطابقاً للمصلحة من كل الوجوه . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ فإنا لا نعرف أن الشمس لو كانت أكبر ما هي الآن أو أصغر كانت المنافع أكمل أو أقل ، ولا نعرف أحوال الأفلاك في هذا الباب، ولا نعرف أيضاً : أن أبدان الناس لو كانت واقعة على غير هذا الوجه والشكل ، كيف كانت تكون أحوالها ؟ وبالجملة : فمن الذي يمكنه إقامة الدلالة على أنه يمتنع وجود وضع أكمل عما هو الآن مع أن الأوضاع المغايرة لهذا الوضع أمور غير متناهية ؟ ومن الذي أحاط عقله بكل تلك الأقسام ، ووقف عقله على كل ما في كل واحد منها من المنافع والمضار ؟
The second question: we say: what is meant by perfection and precision? If what is meant is that these things occur in accordance with benefit and advantage, this is intelligible. But if you mean another meaning, then you must explain it. Then we say: Either you mean by it that it occurs in accordance with benefit in some respect, or you mean by it that it occurs in accordance with benefit in all respects. If you intend the first, then this amount does not indicate that the agent is knowing, for the act that an ignorant person does may correspond to benefit in some respects. If you intend that it corresponds to benefit in all respects, then why do you say that it is so? For we do not know whether, if the sun were larger or smaller than it is now, the benefits would be more complete or less, nor do we know the states of the celestial spheres in this regard, nor do we know whether, if human bodies were in a different form and shape, what their states would be. In summary: who can establish the proof that it is impossible for a more perfect arrangement to exist than what is now, given that the arrangements different from this one are infinite? And whose intellect has encompassed all those categories and grasped all the benefits and harms in each one?
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is the second objection, questioning the claim that the perfection and precision (ʻiḥkām and itqān) of creation proves the creator's knowledge. The objection forces a clarification of what is meant by 'perfection'. If it means conformity to benefit in some respects, ignorance persons can produce such acts. If it means conformity in all respects, we cannot possibly verify that this is the case, since we do not have comprehensive knowledge of all possible arrangements and their benefits. The objection argues that we cannot claim the present arrangement is the best possible, because alternative possibilities are infinite and our knowledge is limited.

Key Terms
إحكام
إتقان
مصلحة
منفعة
جاهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection denies that creation has any perfection at all.
Correction: The objection does not deny that acts can be perfectly arranged; it denies that we can know they are perfectly arranged in all respects.

Checkpoint
What is the objection's point about the first interpretation of 'perfection' (conformity to benefit in some respects)?
A) It proves the agent is knowledgeable
B) It is unintelligible
C) It does not indicate that the agent is knowing
D) It only applies to divine acts

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'perfection' is ambiguous; under neither interpretation can we conclusively prove the creator's knowledge from the perfection of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial, do you trust that Allah's arrangement is best, even though you cannot see the benefit? Which trial tests your trust the most right now?
?
The passage argues that we often claim to know what is best without having full knowledge. Where in your life have you assumed you knew what was better for you than what Allah decreed? How can you surrender that area to Him today?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah's knowledge is perfect, how would your daily worries change? Commit to a specific dua today asking Allah to grant you contentment with His decree.

Three Objections to Perfection Implying Knowledge
The author enumerates three objections against the necessity of knowledge for perfect acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational objections to the claim that a perfect and precise act necessitates a knowing agent, and how these objections challenge the inference from perfection to knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfect acts can occur without knowledge, as shown by rare accidents, animals, and human skill.

This passage presents three objections against the theological principle that a perfectly ordered act must come from a knowing agent. Drawing on everyday examples—occasional accidental precision, animal constructions, and human skill acquisition—the author argues that the link between perfection and knowledge is not universal. In this lesson, you will examine each objection and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic source once, then the translation. Note that the passage presents three numbered objections (wujūh) against the claim that perfect acts require a knowing agent. The third objection is incomplete due to a break in the text.
The passage is from a larger work where the author is responding to objections; here he presents them.
2
Analyze the first objection: the like-judgment principle
The first objection uses the example of a rare perfect act from an ignorant person (e.g., a line of poetry from a non-poet) and the rational principle that 'the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like' to argue that if a small perfect act can come from ignorance, then larger perfect acts could also come from ignorance.
This argument relies on an analogy between small and large acts.
3
Examine the second objection: animal constructions
The second objection cites bees, spiders, and ants that produce highly precise structures without apparent knowledge. If precision implied knowledge, animals would be more knowledgeable than humans—an absurd conclusion.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Consider the third objection: skill habituation
The third objection (incomplete) describes how humans, after practice, perform crafts perfectly without consciously attending to each detail. This suggests that perfect acts can proceed from a state (malaka) that is not full conscious knowledge.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument likely continues to conclude that such acts do not require a knowing agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : نزلنا عن البحث عن تفسير الإحكام والإتقان . فلم قلتم : إن كل من كان فعله محكماً متقناً . فإنه يجب أن يكون عالماً ؟ والذي يدل على أن الأمر ليس كذلك وجوه : الأول : أنه لا نزاع أن الجاهل بالصفة ، قد صدر عنه الفعل المحكم المتقن على سبيل الإتقان مرة واحدة . فالعاجز عن نظم الشعر قد ينطق على سبيل الإتقان بمصراع من الشعر والجاهل بالخط، قد يكتب حرفاً واحداً على الإحكام والإتقان ; بالاتفاق والندرة وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : قد ثبت في العلوم العقلية : أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله فلما ثبت أن العمل القليل قد يوجد من الجاهل ، وجب أن يكون حكم أمثاله وأشباهه كذلك . وهذا يدل على أن صدور الفعل المحكم المتقن من الجاهل : جائز . والثاني : وهو أنا نشاهد أن النحل يبني البيوت المسدسة من غير مسطرة ولا فرجار، على أحسن الوجوه، بل العقلاء الحصفاء الكاملون ، لو أرادوا بناء البيوت المسدسة من الشمع مثل ما يبنيه النحل ، يعجزون عنه والعنكبوت أيضاً . إذا أرادت إصلاح بيتها ، فإنها تأتي بأعمال عجيبة في ذلك البناء . والنملة إذا خبات في جحرها حبات الحنطة فإنها تقلق كل حبة إلى نصفين، لأجل أنه إذا أصابتها الرطوبة ، فإنها لا تنبت . وعجائب أفعال الحيوانات مذكورة في الكتب . فهذه أفعال محكمة متقنة . فإن دل الفعل المحكم المتقن على علم الفاعل ، وجب القول بأنها أكثر علماً من الإنسان لأن ما في هذه الأنواع من الأفعال من وجوه الإحكام والإتقان ، أكثر مما في أفعال الناس . وذلك بعيد جداً الوجه الثالث : وهو أنا ترى أن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يتعلم صنعة الكتابة أو ضرب الطنبور أو صنعة أخرى، فإنه في أول الأمر يحتاج إلى أن يستحضر في ذهنه صورة حرف حرف ، وصورة نقرة نقرة ، ومتى كان الإنسان باقياً في هذه الدرجة ، فإنه يكون مقصراً في تلك الصناعة ، ثم إذا واظب على تلك الحرفة على تلك الحرفة مدة مديدة ، وتمرن فيها حصلت له حالة عجيبة تسمى بالملكة في إصطلاح الحكماء ، بأن يصير بحيث يكتب على أحسن الوجوه من غير أن يحتاج إلى استحضار حرف حرف في الخيال ، بل ربما كان مشغول القلب بمهم ، وهو يكتب على أحسن الوجوه . فههنا الفعل المحكم المتقن الواقع على
The third question: we have descended from the discussion about the interpretation of perfection and precision. So why do you say that everyone whose act is perfect and precise must be knowing? What indicates that the matter is not so are several points: First: There is no dispute that an ignorant person, by description, may once produce a perfect and precise act in a precise manner. For the one incapable of composing poetry may speak a line of poetry precisely, and the ignorant of handwriting may write a single letter with perfection and precision, by agreement and rarity. And if this is established, we say: It is established in the rational sciences that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. So since it is established that a small act can be from an ignorant person, then the judgment of its likes and similar things must be the same. This indicates that the occurrence of a perfect and precise act from an ignorant person is possible. Second: We observe that bees build hexagonal houses without ruler or compass, in the best manner—indeed, the most prudent and perfect rational beings, if they wanted to build hexagonal houses of wax like bees build, would be incapable of it. Also the spider, when it wants to repair its web, performs amazing actions in that building. And the ant, when it stores wheat grains in its hole, splits each grain into two halves so that if moisture affects them, they do not sprout. The wonders of animals' actions are mentioned in books. These are perfect and precise acts. If the perfect and precise act indicates the knowledge of the agent, then one would have to say that they are more knowledgeable than humans, because the aspects of perfection and precision in these types of acts are more than in human acts. And that is very far-fetched. Third: We see that when a human wants to learn the craft of writing or playing the tambour or another craft, he initially needs to bring to mind in his imagination the image of each letter and each note. As long as a person remains at this level, he is deficient in that craft. Then, if he persists in that craft for a long period and practices, he acquires a wondrous state called 'malaka' (disposition) in the terminology of the philosophers, such that he writes in the best manner without needing to bring each letter to mind in imagination; indeed, he may be occupied in thought with something important while writing in the best manner. So here the perfect and precise act that occurs upon...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which provides three arguments (wujūh) against the claim that a perfect and precise act necessarily comes from a knowing agent. The first argument uses the analogy of a small perfect act (e.g., a line of poetry from a non-poet) to argue that occasional perfect acts can come from ignorance; by the principle that like cases have like judgments, larger perfect acts could similarly occur without knowledge. The second argument cites animals (bees, spiders, ants) that produce highly precise constructions without apparent knowledge—indeed, often surpassing humans—which would absurdly imply animals are more knowledgeable than humans if precision implied knowledge. The third argument (incomplete) points to human skill acquisition: after practice, a person performs a craft perfectly without consciously attending to each detail, showing that perfect acts can proceed from a state (malaka) that is not full conscious knowledge of each part.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection denies that any perfect act requires knowledge.
Correction: The objection does not deny that some perfect acts require knowledge; it argues that not all do, so the inference from perfection to knowledge is not universally valid.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument (the example of animals), if perfect acts implied knowledge, what would follow?

B) Animals are more knowledgeable than humans
C) Humans are more knowledgeable than animals
D) Knowledge is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Perfect and precise acts do not always necessitate a knowing agent, as shown by rare accidents, animal constructions, and human habituation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a perfect act does not always require conscious knowledge, examine one area of your life where you perform religious or moral acts 'automatically.' Are those acts truly pleasing to Allah if your heart is absent? Commit to bringing conscious awareness to one such act tomorrow.
?
The ant splits grains with precision to prevent sprouting, yet it does not 'know' in the way you do. How many of your daily decisions are driven by mere habit rather than deliberate submission to Allah's will? This lesson exposes the danger of trusting flawless execution without knowledge. Where have you substituted mere ritual for real knowing, and what will you do about it today?
?
The passage shows that even the most polished performance can come from ignorance. When you recite Quran with perfect tajweed or pray in congregation, do you rely on a 'disposition' (malaka) that has become empty of understanding? What would it take to transform your performance into a conscious act of worship, so that your knowledge and your action become one?

The First Response: The Question Is Unavoidable
Razi presents the mutakallimun's first response: the question applies to any alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first response to why the world was created at a specific time and place despite the equality of times and spaces.

Core Idea
If every possible choice invites the same question, the question is invalid.

This lesson introduces the first of two responses given by the mutakallimun to the problem of specification. They argue that any alternative time or space would also be subject to the same objection, making the objection irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the mutakallimun set up the problem before stating their response.
The passage begins by affirming the equality of times and spaces.
2
Identify the premise and the objection
The mutakallimun claim the world was specified to a particular time and place. The objection asks: why this one? Understand that this objection assumes a need for a cause.
3
Grasp the first response
The response is that any other time or space would face the same question. Therefore the question is invalid because it applies to all possibilities.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the question cannot be meaningfully answered, so it is dismissed.
4
Reflect on the logic
Think about why this response is considered valid by the mutakallimun. It shifts the burden: the objector cannot single out this time/place without also questioning any alternative.
This response does not rely on any attribute of God, only logical consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: وهو أن الأحياز متساوية والأوقات متساوية. ثم إن المتكلمين قالوا: إنه اختص حدوث العالم بوقت معين دون سائر الأوقات، واختص حصول ذات العالم بحيز معين دون سائر الأحياز، فلما قيل لهم: ولم اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات؟ ولم حصل العالم في ذلك الحيز دون سائر الأحياز؟ مع أن الأوقات بأسرها متماثلة، والأحياز بأسرها متماثلة. أجابوا عنه بوجهين: الأول: إنه لو حصل حدوث العالم في وقت آخر غير هذا الوقت، لكان هذا السؤال المذكور باقياً، ولو حصل العالم في حيز آخر سوى هذا الحيز المعين، لكان السؤال المذكور باقياً، وكل سؤال يبقى على كل التقديرات، فإنه ساقط. والثاني: إنهم قالوا: القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر من غير مرجح. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: أجسام العالم يمكن تركيبها وتأليفها على وجوه كثيرة مختلفة غير متناهية. وأحد تلك الوجوه هو هذا الوجه الواقع، والفاعل القادر كان قادراً على هذا الوجه وعلى سائر الوجوه، إلا أن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح أصلاً، فصدر عن القادر هذا الوجه المعين دون سائر الوجوه بمجرد كونه قادراً. فإن مذهبكم: أن القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح، أو نقول: إن القادر لما كان قادراً على هذا الوجه وعلى سائر الوجوه، لم يجز أن يقال: ولم وقع هذا الوجه دون سائر الوجوه؟ لأن هذا السؤال عائد على كل التقديرات، فكان ساقطاً. وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر حينئذ أن مجرد كون الفاعل قادراً كان في وقوع هذه الأشياء على هذه الأحوال المخصوصة.
And the fifth aspect: that spaces are equal and times are equal. Then the mutakallimun said: The occurrence of the world was specified to a particular time to the exclusion of other times, and the obtaining of the substance of the world was specified to a particular space to the exclusion of other spaces. So when it was said to them: Why was the occurrence of the world specified to that time to the exclusion of other times? And why did the world obtain in that space to the exclusion of other spaces, while times are all similar and spaces are all similar? They answered with two aspects: The first: that if the occurrence of the world had taken place at a time other than this time, this mentioned question would have remained; and if the world had obtained in a space other than this specific space, the mentioned question would have remained. And every question that remains in all suppositions is invalid. The second: that they said: The free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can prefer one of the two similar things over the other without a cause (tarjīḥ aḥad al-mithlayn ʿalā al-ākhar min ghayr murajjiḥ). If you have understood this, we say: The bodies of the world can be composed and arranged in many different, infinite ways. And one of those ways is this actual way, and the capable agent (al-fāʿil al-qādir) was capable of this way and of all other ways, except that the capable one can prefer one of the two similar things over the other, with no cause at all. So from the capable one issued this specific way to the exclusion of other ways, by virtue of his being capable. For your doctrine is that the free agent can prefer one of the two similar things over the other, with no cause. Or we say: Since the capable one was capable of this way and of all other ways, it is not permissible to say: Why did this way occur in this manner to the exclusion of other ways? Because this question goes back to all suppositions, so it is invalid. And when this is established, it then becomes clear that the mere fact that the agent is capable was effective in the occurrence of these things in these particular states.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, concerning the equality of times and spaces. The mutakallimun affirm that the world was created at a specific time and place despite the similarity of all times and spaces. The first response to why this specific choice is that any alternative would invite the same question, so the question is invalid as it applies to all possibilities. The second response introduces the key Ash'ari principle: the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can prefer one of two similar things over the other without any cause (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ). Razi then applies this to the composition of the world's bodies, which could be arranged in infinite ways. The actual arrangement occurred by the mere fact that the agent is capable; no further cause is needed. The passage concludes that the agent's sheer capability is sufficient for the occurrence of things in their specific states.

Key Terms
الأحياز
الأوقات
المتكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first response, why is the question 'Why was the world created at this time and place?' considered invalid?
A) Because times and spaces are not actually equal.
B) Because any alternative time or place would also be subject to the same question.
C) Because the world must be created at the best time and place.
D) Because the question was asked by disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The first response argues that any alternative time or space would raise the same question, so the question itself is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever complained to God about something in your life, only to realize that any alternative you can imagine would also be open to the same complaint? How does knowing that the question 'why this?' is invalid change your response?
?
Think of a situation where you demanded a reason from God. If He were to point out that the same question applies to every possible scenario, would you be able to accept that your demand is logically unsound? What would it take for you to trust His wisdom instead?
?
The mutakallimun argue that a question that never goes away is invalid. But what if the question is 'why does evil exist?' Is this question invalid because it persists in every possible world? Or is there a deeper answer? Reflect on where you draw the line between rational questioning and rebellious questioning in your own heart.

The Second Response: The Free Agent Need No Cause
Razi presents the second response: the free agent can prefer one similar over another without a cause, and applies it to the world's composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that the free agent can prefer one of two similar things without a cause (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ) and how Razi applies it to the composition of the world.

Core Idea
The free agent's sheer capability is sufficient for choice between equals.

This lesson delves into the second and more profound response of the mutakallimun: the principle of tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ. Razi explains that the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can choose without a cause, and he applies this to the infinite possible arrangements of the world's bodies. The lesson also shows how this principle renders the question of 'why this arrangement?' invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the shift: the second response introduces a direct attribute of the agent. The passage then applies it to the composition of the world.
The phrase 'إذا عرفت هذا' connects back to the first response.
2
Identify the key principle
The mutakallimun state: 'al-qādir al-mukhtār can prefer one of the two similar things over the other without a cause.' Understand that this is the core of the Ash'ari doctrine on divine freedom.
The term 'murajjiḥ' means a cause or factor that tips the balance.
3
See how Razi applies the principle
Razi notes that the world's bodies can be arranged in infinite ways. The actual arrangement is one of these. The capable agent was able to produce any arrangement. By the principle, the agent chose this arrangement without any cause other than being capable.
This application shows the principle's explanatory power for contingency in creation.
4
Notice the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the question 'why this arrangement?' is also invalid because any other arrangement would face the same question. Thus, the first response is reused but grounded in the agent's capability.
Razi here combines both responses: the logical invalidity of the question and the theological principle of free choice.

الوجه الخامس: وهو أن الأحياز متساوية والأوقات متساوية. ثم إن المتكلمين قالوا: إنه اختص حدوث العالم بوقت معين دون سائر الأوقات، واختص حصول ذات العالم بحيز معين دون سائر الأحياز، فلما قيل لهم: ولم اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات؟ ولم حصل العالم في ذلك الحيز دون سائر الأحياز؟ مع أن الأوقات بأسرها متماثلة، والأحياز بأسرها متماثلة. أجابوا عنه بوجهين: الأول: إنه لو حصل حدوث العالم في وقت آخر غير هذا الوقت، لكان هذا السؤال المذكور باقياً، ولو حصل العالم في حيز آخر سوى هذا الحيز المعين، لكان السؤال المذكور باقياً، وكل سؤال يبقى على كل التقديرات، فإنه ساقط. والثاني: إنهم قالوا: القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر من غير مرجح. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: أجسام العالم يمكن تركيبها وتأليفها على وجوه كثيرة مختلفة غير متناهية. وأحد تلك الوجوه هو هذا الوجه الواقع، والفاعل القادر كان قادراً على هذا الوجه وعلى سائر الوجوه، إلا أن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح أصلاً، فصدر عن القادر هذا الوجه المعين دون سائر الوجوه بمجرد كونه قادراً. فإن مذهبكم: أن القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح، أو نقول: إن القادر لما كان قادراً على هذا الوجه وعلى سائر الوجوه، لم يجز أن يقال: ولم وقع هذا الوجه دون سائر الوجوه؟ لأن هذا السؤال عائد على كل التقديرات، فكان ساقطاً. وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر حينئذ أن مجرد كون الفاعل قادراً كان في وقوع هذه الأشياء على هذه الأحوال المخصوصة.
And the fifth aspect: that spaces are equal and times are equal. Then the mutakallimun said: The occurrence of the world was specified to a particular time to the exclusion of other times, and the obtaining of the substance of the world was specified to a particular space to the exclusion of other spaces. So when it was said to them: Why was the occurrence of the world specified to that time to the exclusion of other times? And why did the world obtain in that space to the exclusion of other spaces, while times are all similar and spaces are all similar? They answered with two aspects: The first: that if the occurrence of the world had taken place at a time other than this time, this mentioned question would have remained; and if the world had obtained in a space other than this specific space, the mentioned question would have remained. And every question that remains in all suppositions is invalid. The second: that they said: The free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can prefer one of the two similar things over the other without a cause (tarjīḥ aḥad al-mithlayn ʿalā al-ākhar min ghayr murajjiḥ). If you have understood this, we say: The bodies of the world can be composed and arranged in many different, infinite ways. And one of those ways is this actual way, and the capable agent (al-fāʿil al-qādir) was capable of this way and of all other ways, except that the capable one can prefer one of the two similar things over the other, with no cause at all. So from the capable one issued this specific way to the exclusion of other ways, by virtue of his being capable. For your doctrine is that the free agent can prefer one of the two similar things over the other, with no cause. Or we say: Since the capable one was capable of this way and of all other ways, it is not permissible to say: Why did this way occur in this manner to the exclusion of other ways? Because this question goes back to all suppositions, so it is invalid. And when this is established, it then becomes clear that the mere fact that the agent is capable was effective in the occurrence of these things in these particular states.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, concerning the equality of times and spaces. The mutakallimun affirm that the world was created at a specific time and place despite the similarity of all times and spaces. The first response to why this specific choice is that any alternative would invite the same question, so the question is invalid as it applies to all possibilities. The second response introduces the key Ash'ari principle: the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can prefer one of two similar things over the other without any cause (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ). Razi then applies this to the composition of the world's bodies, which could be arranged in infinite ways. The actual arrangement occurred by the mere fact that the agent is capable; no further cause is needed. The passage concludes that the agent's sheer capability is sufficient for the occurrence of things in their specific states.

Key Terms
القادر المختار
ترجيح أحد المثلين
مرجح
الفاعل القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjiḥ' means the agent acts arbitrarily or without reason.
Correction: In Ash'ari thought, this principle is a necessary property of a free agent. The agent's will is self-sufficient and not determined by external causes. It is not arbitrariness but a sign of absolute freedom.

Checkpoint
What is the principle invoked in the second response?
A) The world must be created in the best possible way.
B) The free agent can prefer one of two similar things without a cause.
C) Times and spaces are not equal.
D) The world must be created at the earliest time.

Key Takeaway
The second response establishes that the free agent can choose between equal alternatives without any cause, and that the actual arrangement of the world is a direct result of the agent's sheer capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or blessing in your life that seems inexplicable, do you truly accept that God's choice is sufficient without needing a visible cause, or do you find yourself demanding a 'why' from Him? How can you train your heart to rest in His sheer capability?
?
The lesson teaches that God's preference between two similar possibilities is not random but flows from His sheer power and freedom. When you are confronted with God's decrees that you do not understand, do you trust that His choice—even without a reason you can see—is the best? Or do you seek to justify His actions according to your own reasoning?
?
If the free agent's capability alone is enough to explain the actual state of the world, then every detail of your existence—your circumstances, trials, and blessings—is a direct selection by God. Does this thought bring you peace or discomfort? What would change in your daily life if you lived with full conviction that nothing happens without His intentional choice, even if you cannot see the reason?

The Free Agent's Specific Choice: Summary of the Argument
Razi summarizes that individual forms are explained by the free agent's choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's summary argument that the specific forms of individuals are explained by the free agent's ability to prefer one possibility over another without a cause.

Core Idea
The free agent's specific choice explains why anything is this way rather than any other possible way.

Razi brings his discussion of divine attributes to a concise summary. He uses the example of human individuals to show that the only answer to 'why this particular form?' is the free agent's capacity to choose without a cause. This principle then extends to all divine acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the summarising passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Notice it is a compact argument that moves from observation to question to answer.
The passage is cut off at the end; we take it as given.
2
Identify the problem
The passage starts with a fact: every individual has a specific size, constitution, and nature, despite many other possibilities. Recognize this as the observation that demands explanation.
The question 'Why this particular way?' is the core puzzle.
3
Find the answer
Razi states the only answer is that the capable agent (al-qādir) can bring about a thing with a specific quality while all other qualities are possible. This is the key doctrine.
The phrase 'خاصية القادر المختار' (property of the free agent) is central.
4
Understand the property: ترجيح المثلين على الآخر لا لمرجح
The specific property is preferring one of two similar things over the other with no cause. This is the definition of free choice (ikhtiyar) in kalam.
This distinguishes the free agent from natural causes.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical extension
Razi ends by asking: if we accept this for human forms, why not extend it to other divine acts? Consider how this principle underpins all divine voluntary actions.
The passage is cut off, but the implication is clear: the same logic applies to all divine acts of specification.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الكلام: إن كل شخص من أشخاص الناس مخصوص بمقدار معين، مع أنه كان يمكن وقوعه أعظم مما وقع عليه الآن، أو أصغر مما وقع عليه الآن. ولذلك اختص بكون معين، وخلقة معينة، وطبيعة معينة، مع أن سائر الصور والأحوال كانت جائزة عليه. فإذا قلنا: ولم وقع هذا الوجه المعين دون سائر الوجوه الجائزة؟ لم يكن لنا عنه جواب، إلا أن نقول: القادر بمجرد كونه قادراً يمكنه إيقاع الشيء على صفة خاصة، مع جواز سائر الصفات؛ لأن خاصية القادر المختار ترجيح المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح. فإذا جوزنا هذا، فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال أن القادر المختار يمكنه إيقاع هذه.
The summary of the discourse: that every individual among the people is specified to a particular amount, while it was possible for him to occur greater than what he now occurs upon, or smaller than what he now occurs upon. And therefore he was specified to a particular being, a particular constitution, and a particular nature, while all other forms and states were possible for him. So if we say: Why did this particular way occur to the exclusion of the other possible ways? We would have no answer about it except to say: The capable one (al-qādir), by virtue of his being capable, can bring about the thing with a specific quality, while all other qualities are possible; because the specific property of the free agent (khāṣṣiyyat al-qādir al-mukhtār) is preferring one of the two similar things over the other, with no cause. So if we have allowed this, why is it also not allowed to say that the free agent can bring about these [things]?
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the overall argument. It takes the example of individual human beings: each person has a specific size, shape, constitution, and nature, even though many other possibilities were equally possible. The question 'why this particular form?' can only be answered by appealing to the capability of the free agent (al-qādir) to bring about a thing with a specific quality while all other qualities are equally possible, because the specific property of the free agent (khāṣṣiyyat al-qādir al-mukhtār) is precisely the ability to prefer one of two similar things over the other without a cause. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: if this principle is accepted for human forms, why not also accept that the free agent can bring about these specific qualities? The text is cut off at this point.

Misconception: One might think that the specific form is due to accident or natural necessity.
Correction: Razi insists it is due to the free agent's indifference and choice, not any external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi emphasizes that God's preference is without cause. Does this idea unsettle you because it seems arbitrary, or does it fill you with awe that God's will is utterly independent and sovereign? Which emotion dominates, and how can you transform that into deeper submission to His will?

The Free Agent's Ability to Prefer Without a Reason
The author argues that the free agent can choose between equals without a reason, explaining the world's specific form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the free agent's ability to choose between equals without a reason is a necessary basis for explaining the specific forms of the world and refutes objections about the need for a cause.

Core Idea
The free agent's choice among equals needs no external reason—only its power.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection: why did the world take this specific form rather than an infinite number of other possible forms? He argues that the question itself is invalid because it applies equally to any form. The only explanation is the free agent's power to prefer without a reason. This principle is then extended to human individuals and even to the question of whether knowledge is necessary for creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very direct style of argumentation.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that the free agent can prefer one of two equals over the other without a reason (tarijah bila murajjih). This is the foundational principle.
This is stated at the very beginning.
3
Apply to the world's bodies
See how the author applies this principle: the world could have infinite forms, but the agent chose the actual one without a reason.
The author uses the phrase 'مجرد كونه قادراً' (merely by being powerful).
4
Understand the refutation of the question
The author shows that the question 'Why this way?' is invalid because it would apply to any specific way. Consider why this refutation works.
The author calls the question 'sāqiṭ' (invalid).
5
Extend to humans and the rhetorical challenge
Notice how the same reasoning applies to individual humans, and then the author challenges the opponent: if we accept this, why not also accept that the agent can create without knowledge?
This is a rhetorical extension to test the opponent's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنهم قالوا : القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر من غير مرجح . إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : أجسام العالم يمكن تركيبها وتأليفها على وجوه كثيرة مختلفة ، غير متناهية . وأحد تلك الوجوه هو هذا الوجه الواقع ، والفاعل القادر كان قادراً على هذا الوجه ، وعلى سائر الوجوه ، إلا أن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر ، لا لمرجح أصلا ، فصدر عن القادر ، هذا الوجه المعين دون سائر الوجوه ، بمجرد كونه قادراً . فإن مذهبكم : أن القادر المختار يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر ، لا لمرجح ، أو نقول : إن القادر لما كان قادراً على هذا الوجه ، وعلى سائر الوجوه ، لم يجز أن يقال : ولم وقع هذا الوجه على هذا الوجه دون سائر الوجوه ؟ لأن هذا السؤال عائد على كل التقديرات ، فكان ساقطاً ، وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر حينئذ أن مجرد كون الفاعل قادراً كان في وقوع هذه الأشياء على هذه الأحوال المخصوصة .

وحاصل الكلام : إن كل شخص من أشخاص الناس مخصوص بمقدار معين ، مع أنه كان يمكن وقوعه أعظم مما وقع عليه الآن ، أو أصغر مما وقع عليه الآن. ولذلك اختص بكون معين، وخلقة معينة ، وطبيعية معينة ، مع أن سائر الصور والأحوال كانت جائزة عليه. فإذا قلنا : ولم وقع هذا الوجه المعين دون سائر الوجوه الجائزة ؟ لم يكن لنا عنه جواب ، إلا أن نقول : القادر بمجرد كونه قادراً يمكنه إيقاع الشيء على صفة خاصة ، مع جواز سائر الصفات ؛ لأن خاصية القادر المختار ترجيح المثلين على الآخر، لا لمرجح فإذا جوزنا هذا . فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال أن القادر المختار يمكنه إيقاع هذه الأجسام على هذه الصفات المطابقة للمصالح ، بمجرد كونه قادراً من غير أن يعتبر في ذلك الفاعل القادر كونه عالماً ؟ والله أعلم
And the second: They said: The powerful free agent can prefer one of two equals over the other without a reason. If you understand this, then we say: The bodies of the world can be composed and formed in many different, infinite ways. And one of those ways is this actual way. And the powerful agent was powerful over this way and over the rest of the ways, except that the powerful agent can prefer one of two equals over the other without any reason at all. So this specific way issued from the agent without the rest of the ways, merely by virtue of his being powerful. For your doctrine is that the powerful free agent can prefer one of two equals over the other, without a reason. Or we say: Since the agent was powerful over this way and over the rest of the ways, it is not permissible to say: Why did this way occur in this way rather than the rest of the ways? Because this question recurs on all assumptions, so it is invalid. And if this is established, it then appears that mere being of the agent as powerful was in the occurrence of these things in these specific states.

The summary of the discourse: Every individual person is specified with a particular measure, even though it could have occurred greater than what it actually is, or smaller than what it actually is. Therefore it is specified with a particular being, a particular constitution, and a particular nature, even though other forms and states were possible for it. So if we say: Why did this specific way occur rather than the other possible ways? We have no answer except to say: The powerful agent, merely by being powerful, can bring about a thing in a specific quality, while other qualities are possible; because the property of the powerful free agent is to prefer one of two equals over the other without a reason. So if we allow this, then why may we not also say that the powerful free agent can bring about these bodies with these qualities suitable for benefits, merely by virtue of his being powerful, without considering in that powerful agent his being knowledgeable? And God knows best.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection about the necessity of a reason for the agent's choice. The author argues that the free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) can prefer one of two equally possible options without any external reason (tarijah bila murajjih). He applies this to the bodies of the world: they could be formed in infinite ways, but only one specific arrangement actually exists. The agent, being powerful, simply chose this arrangement without a reason. If one asks why this way rather than another, the question is invalid because it applies equally to all possibilities—every specific arrangement would face the same question. Thus the mere fact that the agent is powerful suffices to explain the occurrence of things in their specific states. The author then extends this to individual humans: each person has a specific size, form, and nature, though many other possibilities were open. The only explanation is the agent's power and free choice. Finally, he poses a rhetorical question: if we accept that the free agent can choose without a reason for preference among possibilities, then why can't we also say that the agent brings about bodies with qualities that match benefits without needing knowledge? This challenges the opponent to provide a proof that knowledge is necessary.

Key Terms
قادر مختار
ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر
لا لمرجح
إيقاع الشيء على صفة خاصة
خاصية القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies God acts without wisdom or purpose.
Correction: The argument only denies the need for an external cause for preference among equally possible alternatives; the agent's wisdom may still be present and not contradicted.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the question 'Why did this specific way occur?' fail when applied to the free agent's action?
A) Because the agent always acts with a reason that we cannot know.
B) Because the question is senseless when no other specific way is possible.
C) Because the question would equally apply to any specific way, so it is invalid.
D) Because the agent is not truly free if there is a reason.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's power alone suffices to explain why one specific possibility among many was actualized; no external reason or cause is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice between equally good options, do you feel a need to justify it with a reason? How does this passage challenge your assumption that every choice requires a cause?
?
The passage says each person's specific measure—size, nature, constitution—comes solely from the agent's power. Are you truly content with your specific measure? How does this truth affect your gratitude or complaint against Allah?
?
If Allah's power alone suffices to bring about the entire world, why do you still search for physical causes and natural explanations? What does this reveal about your reliance on secondary causes versus reliance on Allah?

Does Conjecture Suffice for Precise Actions?
The author examines the objection that conjecture may suffice for producing precise actions, challenging the necessity of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that conjecture might suffice for producing precise actions and why this challenges the necessity of divine knowledge in the design argument.

Core Idea
A writer's precise work based on conjecture suggests the world's precision might also come from conjecture, not certain knowledge.

The author presents a powerful objection: even if we grant that precise actions require awareness (shuʿūr wa idrāk), why must that awareness be certain knowledge (ʿilm)? Could conjecture (ẓann) be enough? Using the example of a skilled writer who produces precise writing without certain knowledge of every detail, the objector argues that the world's precision could similarly arise from conjecture. This lesson unpacks that objection and the ensuing reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: the question, the example, the conclusion, and the counter-argument.
The passage is dense with technical terms; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the objection's structure
Note how the objector first concedes that precise actions need some awareness, then asks why conjecture cannot replace knowledge. The writer example is the key evidence.
The example shows that precise crafts can be produced based on strong conjecture (ẓann ghālib).
3
Analyze the reply
The reply from the opponent says conjecturers can err. But the objector counters: the world contains many defects, so perhaps the creator sometimes errs. This shows the inference from precision to knowledge is not airtight.
Understand that this is a dialectical move; the objector is not asserting this view, only showing it is possible.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how this objection undermines the classical design argument unless the possibility of conjecture is ruled out. This is a classic kalam debate.
The next lesson may address how the mutakallimun respond to this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : هب أنا سلمنا أن فاعل الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة يجب أن يكون له شعور وإدراك . فلم لا يكفي في صدور الأفعال المحكمة عنه كونه ظاناً ، ولا يشترط فيه كونه عالماً ؟ والدليل على الاحتمال المذكور لازم : ذلك ، لأن أصحاب الحرف والصناعات إنما يأتون بتلك الأفاعيل بناء على الظنون والحسبانات وإلا فالأتي صنعة الكتابة ، كيف يمكنه تحصيل العلم اليقيني بأحوال رأس القلم . ويأحوال سطح الكافد. وبأحوال كمية المداه.. وكيفيته . وبأحوال حركات الأصابع بحسب ما لها من الكمية والكيفية ؟ وكل هذه الأحوال مجهولة عند ذلك الكاتب ، إلا أنه يظن ظناً غالباً أن هذه الآلات والأدوات موافقة لتحصيل الصنعة المطلوبة ، فيستعمل تلك الآلات في تلك الصنائع بناء على الظنون والحسبانات ، فتحصل تلك الصنائع ، فيثبت أن كل ما نشاهده في العالم من الصنائع المحكمة المتقنة . فإنه إنما يحصل بناء على الظنون والحسبانات ، ولا حاجة في تحصيل شيء منها إلى العلم الجازم الموصوف بالقطع واليقين. وإذا ثبت هذا فقد ظهر أن الاستدلال بالفعل المحكم للمتقن على كون فاعله عالماً في غاية الصعوبة .

فإن قالوا : إن صاحب الظن قد يخطىء وقد يغلط ، وقد يعجز عن تحصيل مطلوبه فنقول : لا نزاع في أن الأمر كما ذكرتم ، إلا أنا ترى الوجوه الكثيرة من النقائص والآفات والتشويهات الكثيرة في الخلقة حاصلة في تركيبات هذا العالم ، فلعل هذه الأحوال إنما حصلت لأن فاعلها. إنما يركبها بناء على الظن والتخمين ، فتارة تقع على الوجه الصواب الموافق للحكمة والمصلحة ، وتارة تقع على الوجه المضطرب الفاسد وإذا كان الاحتمال قائماً فعليكم أن تدلوا على أنه غير محتمل ، وعلى أنه باطل ، حتى يتم دليلكم في الاستدلال بالفعل المحكم المتقن على كون الفاعل عالماً
The fourth question: Suppose we affirm that the doer of precise and perfect actions must have awareness and perception. So why does not his being a conjecturer suffice for the occurrence of precise actions from him, and his being knowledgeable is not required? And the proof for the mentioned possibility is necessary: that, because the people of crafts and industries only perform those actions based on conjectures and estimates. For instance, the one who does the craft of writing, how can he attain certain knowledge of the states of the pen's tip, and of the states of the surface of the paper [؟], and of the states of the quantity of the ink [؟] and its quality, and of the states of the movements of the fingers according to what they have of quantity and quality? All these states are unknown to that writer, except that he conjectures a strong conjecture that these instruments and tools are suitable for attaining the desired craft. So he uses those instruments in those crafts based on conjectures and estimates, and those crafts are realized. So it is established that everything we observe in the world of precise and perfect crafts is only realized based on conjectures and estimates, and there is no need in attaining any of it for the certain knowledge described with decisiveness and certainty. And if this is established, it has become clear that the inference from the precise and perfect action to its doer being knowledgeable is extremely difficult.

If they say: The one with conjecture may err and may mistake, and may be unable to attain his desired end. We say: There is no dispute that the matter is as you mentioned, except that we see many aspects of deficiencies, defects, and many disfigurements in creation occurring in the compositions of this world. Perhaps these states only occurred because their doer composes them based on conjecture and guesswork, sometimes occurring in the correct way consistent with wisdom and benefit, and sometimes occurring in the disordered and corrupt way. And if the possibility stands, you must demonstrate that it is not possible and that it is false, so that your proof is complete in inferring from the precise and perfect action that the doer is knowledgeable.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth question (al-suʾāl al-rābiʿ) in a series of objections against the argument that precise actions require knowledge. The objector concedes that the doer of precise and perfect actions must have awareness, but asks why conjecture (ẓann) cannot suffice instead of certain knowledge (ʿilm). The author supports this possibility by pointing to human artisans: they produce precise crafts (e.g., writing) based on conjecture about the tools and their use, without certain knowledge of every detail. Thus, precise actions can issue from conjecture. This supposedly undermines the inference from the world's precision to the Creator's knowledge. The author then presents a response from the opponent: one who relies on conjecture may err and fail. The author replies that this is true, but we see many defects and imperfections in the world, which might be due to the agent acting on conjecture and guesswork—sometimes succeeding, sometimes failing. Therefore, the possibility remains that the world's order results from conjecture, and the opponent must prove that this possibility is false for his argument to stand. This is a classic kalam objection challenging the strength of the design argument.

شعور وإدراك

علم جازم
صنائع
نقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think the author is denying divine knowledge.
Correction: The author is reporting an objection; he is not asserting that the world's creator acts on conjecture. He is showing that the design argument is not airtight unless one can rule out this possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the example of the writer in this passage?
A) To show that writing requires certain knowledge of every detail.
B) To prove that precise actions can be produced based on conjecture.
C) To argue that the world's design is impossible without a knower.
D) To demonstrate that conjecture always leads to error.

Key Takeaway
The objection from conjecture claims that precise actions can be produced without certain knowledge, thereby challenging the design argument's strength unless the possibility of conjecture is ruled out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on conjectures every day—your plans, your relationships, your beliefs. Yet do you ever admit that your certainty might be built on guesswork? When you claim to know God, is that knowledge or strong conjecture? What would happen if you held your beliefs with the humility that they might be conjecture?
?
The objector says the world's defects could be from a creator acting on conjecture. When you see suffering and imperfection in your own life, do you interpret that as evidence of a flawed creator or as a test from the All-Knowing? Which interpretation does your heart actually believe?
?
You have probably used the design argument to affirm God's knowledge. But have you ever considered that your own mind, which produces arguments, might itself be based on conjecture? If your reasoning is not certain, how can you trust any conclusion about God?

The Fifth Objection: Evil and Defects Do Not Negate the Creator's Knowledge
Presents the fifth objection concerning evil and defects and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence of evil and defects in the world does not disprove the Creator's knowledge or wisdom, and why the argument from design remains valid.

Core Idea
Well-ordered acts prove knowledge; defective acts do not prove ignorance.

The fifth objection challenges the argument from design by pointing to the existence of evil and defects. The objector argues that if the world were created with wisdom and mercy, such evils would not exist; therefore, the Creator must lack knowledge or power. The answer reframes the objection by showing that defective acts do not indicate ignorance, as a knowledgeable agent can produce them deliberately.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two-part structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify the objection's two horns
The objector presents a dilemma: either the world was created for wisdom and benefit, or for sheer power. Both lead to the conclusion that evil disproves divine knowledge or power. Find these two scenarios in the text.
3
Understand the answer's key distinction
The answer states that precision and perfection indicate knowledge, but defective acts do not indicate ignorance. Ask yourself why this distinction defeats the objection.
A knowledgeable agent can produce both perfect and defective acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : إنا كما رأينا في هذا العالم أفعالاً محكمة متقنة منسقة منظمة وأنها توهم كون مدير هذا العالم عالماً حكيماً ، فقد نرى هذا العالم أيضاً مملوءاً من الآفات والمخافات والتشويهات في الخلقة والأحوال المنفرة مثل العمي والزمانة والفقر الشديد . ونرى الإنسان الكامل في العلم والعمل محروماً عن الخيرات الدنيوية ، ذليلاً مهيناً ، تحت تصرف الجهال والأراذل . ونرى أخس الخلق مثل أرذل الصبيان والنسوان يحصل لهم الاستيلاء على أهل الدنيا والاستعلاء على الخلق وهذه الأحوال غير لائقة بالرحيم العليم الحكيم . وتقريره إنه تعالى إما أن يقال : إنه خلق هذا العالم لرعاية حكمة ومصلحة ، أو خلقه لمحض القدرة من غير رعاية الحكمة والمصلحة . فإن كان الحق هو الثاني فحينئذ هذه الوجوه الحاصلة من المصالح ما قصد الفاعل خلق العالم لأجلها ، بل اتفق أنه خصص هذا القسم بالإيجاد والتكوين دون سائر الوجوه من غير أن كان كونها مصلحة علة لرجحان هذا القسم على سائر الأقسام . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : مجرد القدرة كافي في إخراج هذه الأشياء من العدم إلى الوجود ؟ وأما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم لأجل الرحمة والحكمة وإفاضة المنفعة . فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك لما خلق فيها أنواع الآفات والمخافات . وحيث حصلت هذه الأشياء ، علمنا أنها إنما حصلت إما لعدم العلم ، وإما لعدم القدرة . وأجيب عن هذا السؤال . فقيل : الإحكام والإتقان يدل على علم الفاعل ، لأن الجاهل يمتنع أن يصدر عنه الفعل المحكم المتقن . أما الأفعال الفاسدة الخسيسة فإنها لا تدل على جهل الفاعل لأن العالم يمكنه أن يأتي بالأفعال الفاسدة الخسيسة . فعلم أنه ليس المقصود من السؤال المذكور ما توهمه هذا
The fifth question: Indeed, just as we see in this world well-ordered, precise, coordinated, organized acts, and they suggest that the manager of this world is knowing and wise, we also see this world filled with afflictions, terrors, and deformities in creation and repulsive conditions such as blindness, chronic illness, and severe poverty. And we see the perfect human in knowledge and action deprived of worldly goods, humiliated, under the control of the ignorant and base. And we see the lowest of creation, such as the vilest of boys and women, gaining domination over the people of the world and superiority over creation. And these conditions are not befitting the Merciful, the Knowing, the Wise. Its analysis: Either it is said that He, exalted, created this world for the sake of wisdom and benefit, or He created it for sheer power without regard for wisdom and benefit. If the truth is the second, then these instances of benefit that occur, the agent did not intend creation for their sake; rather, it happened that He specified this portion for bringing into existence and formation over other aspects, without it being that their being benefit was the cause for preferring this portion over other portions. And if it is so, then why is it not permissible to say: mere power is sufficient to bring these things from non-existence into existence? And if we say that He, exalted, created this world for mercy, wisdom, and the pouring forth of benefit, we say: If it were so, He would not have created in it kinds of afflictions and terrors. And since these things have occurred, we know that they occurred either due to lack of knowledge or lack of power. And it was answered concerning this question. It was said: Precision and perfection indicate the knowledge of the agent, because the ignorant cannot possibly produce a precise, perfect act. As for corrupt, base acts, they do not indicate the ignorance of the agent, because the knowledgeable can produce corrupt, base acts. So it is known that the intention of the aforementioned question is not what this one imagined.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the argument from design (the inference from well-ordered acts to a knowing Creator). The objector raises the problem of evil: alongside many well-ordered acts, we see afflictions, deformities, and injustices. The objector argues that if the world were created for wisdom and mercy, such evils should not exist; therefore, the Creator either lacks knowledge or power. The author then gives the answer: well-ordered acts prove the Creator's knowledge, but defective acts do not prove ignorance, because a knowledgeable agent can deliberately produce defective acts. Thus the objection is based on a misunderstanding: the existence of evil does not negate the Creator's knowledge or wisdom; it simply shows that the Creator permits evil for some reason, but the objection improperly infers that the agent must be ignorant. The answer preserves the argument from design by restricting its scope: only well-ordered acts indicate knowledge; defective acts are neutral.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil and defects in the world proves that God lacks either knowledge or power.
Correction: The answer shows that evil does not disprove divine knowledge because a knowledgeable agent can deliberately produce defective acts. Well-ordered acts, on the other hand, do prove knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the answer given to the objection that the presence of defects and evils in the world indicates the Creator's lack of knowledge or power?
A) The defects are not real; they are illusions.
B) Well-ordered acts prove knowledge, but defective acts do not prove ignorance because a knowledgeable agent can produce defective acts.
C) The defects are caused by secondary agents, not by the Creator.
D) The objection is invalid because the world is actually perfect.

Key Takeaway
The presence of evil does not disprove the Creator's knowledge, because a knowledgeable agent can deliberately produce defective acts; only well-ordered acts indicate knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a tragedy or injustice, does your heart immediately question God's knowledge or mercy? What would it cost you to believe, like this passage teaches, that evil does not disprove His knowledge?
?
Think of a time you suffered or saw evil triumph. Did you secretly assume that God was either unaware or uncaring? How would your faith change if you truly internalized that God knowingly permits evil for a wisdom you may never understand?
?
If you really believe that well-ordered acts prove God's knowledge, how should that affect your daily trust in Him, especially when life feels chaotic and disordered? What one step can you take today to anchor your confidence in His knowledge rather than in your circumstances?
